Spring approaches, and summer will quickly follow.
Wliat are you doing about the roof on your new house?
Have you given it serious consideration? There is one
roofing that excels all others for the Australian climate;
that is PABCO. In appearance Pahco is delightfully
restful to the eye, with its soft ivory tint. In tempera-
ture it is lower than any other material. To live under
it is to enjoy spring conditions at mid-summer. In
durability it lasts practically for ever, and a deluge could
not affect it so far as being waterproof.

The Red Page
VERSES by W. H. DAVIES.
Slum Children.
Your songs at night a drunkard sings,
Stone, sticks and rags your daily flowers;
Like fishers’ lips, a hluey white,
Such lips, poor mites, are yours.
Poor little things, so sad and solemn,
Whose lives are passed in human crowds —
When in the water I cap see
Heaven with a flock of clouds.
Poor little mites that breathe foul air,
Where garbage chokes the sink and drain —
Now when the hawthorn smells so sweet,
Wet with the summer rain.
But few of ye Will live for long;
Ye are but small new islands seen,
To disappear before your lives
Can grow and be made green.
Days Too Short.
When primroses are out in Spring,
And small, blue violets come between;
When merry birds sing on boughs green,
And rills, as soon as born, must sing;
When butterflies unll make side-leaps,
As though escaped from Nature's hand
Ere perfect quite; and bees will stand
Upon their heads in fragrant deeps;
When small clouds are so silvery white
Each seems a broken rimmed moon —
When such things are, this world too soon.
For me, doth wear the veil of Night.
The Little Ones.
The little ones are put in bed,
And both are laughing, lying down;
Their father, and their mother, too.
Are gone on Christmas Eve to town.
“Old Santa Claus will bring a horse;
Gee up!” cried little Will, with glee;
“If I am good, I’ll have a doll
From Santa Claus,” laughed Emily.
The little ones are gone to sleep,
Their father and their mother now
Are coming home, with many more —
They’re drunk, and make a merry row.
The little ones on Christmas morn
Jump up, like skylarks from the grass;
And then they stand as still as stones,
And just as cold as stones, Alas!
No horse, no doll beside their bed;
No sadder little ones could be;
“We did some tvrong,” said little Will —
“We must have sinned,” sobbed Emily.
How to Write a play
“There are no rules for writing a play.”
So, in the first page of Play-Making: A
Manual of Craftsmanship (Chapman and
Hall), William Archer pontifies—and calmly
proceeds to devote 312 pages to the discus-
sion of those non-existent rules. Archer
cannot write plays himself: his job is to
criticise them; and his taste for the drama
has no “high-brow” limitations. Anything,
to Archer, is a play that has been success-
fully produced. Ibsen is, for his critical
purposes, of no more value than R. C.
Carton. He draws for his illustrations upon
anything that has happened behind the foot-
lights. And yet, to those of you who are,
despite the killing environment of Australia,
interested in playwriting, his manual is
packed with valuable advice and expert
criticism. Archer is writing for those who
wish to write the common, or calcium, vari-
ety of play. He addresses his book to “Stu-
dents of play-writing who sincerely desire to
db sound, artistic work under the conditions
and limitations of the actual, living play-
house.”
It is, of course, only the art of structure
—a comparatively Mechanical and formal
part of the dramatist’s art —that can be
taught. A play, it must be remembered, is
not a play till it has been produced.
The kindergarten class of Australian play-
wrights will now commence :- —
The essence in drama is crisis. A play is
a more or less rapidly developing crisis in
destiny or circumstance, and a dramatic
scene is a crisis within a crisis, clearly fur-
thering the ultimate event. The drama gives
us only the culminating points, or the inter-
secting culminations of two or three des-
tinies. But every crisis is not dramatic.
The avferage marriage is a crisis. Archer
provides a rough definition of the dramatic:
“Any representation of imaginary person-
ages which is capable of interesting an aver-
age audience assembled in the theatre.”
Begin by drawing up a tentative scenario
—unless your name is Bernard Shaw, or
Galsworthy. Pinero makes sure before start-
ing to write his plays that there is a way of
doing them, but whether he ultimately fol-
lows that way is a different matter. Sutro
plans his exit and entrances beforehand.
First, however, you have got to get familiar
with your characters, to know and live
among the people you have created. And
when you pin them down into your scenario,
if you do not find that they want to say more
than you can allow them to say, or rush
headlong into blind alleys, or do things that
upset the balance of the play, and have to
be resolutely undone —unless, in short, your
characters are so alive to you that they take
charge of your play, then you can’t write
plays. And a word to the beginner: never
mark your entrances and exits as they are
marked in F'rench’s acting editions, in the
out-of-date “R.2.E.,” “L.C..” “L.U.E.” rem-
iniscent of days when the stage had none of
the conveniences of setting possessed by the
modern theatre. And keep your stage direc-
tions as brief and as impersonal as possible
—unless, again, your name happens to be
Shaw. And be chary of introducing charac-
ters who have n,o personal share in the
drama, and are mere mouthpieces for the
conveyance of information.
In building your play you can either give
the whole of your adventure, or start in the
middle of your adventure, and leave the
beginning outside the frame of the picture.
For light comedy and romantic plays the
former method is desirable: for a play of
complex substance, the method of attacking
the crisis in the middle or toward the end —
Ibsen nearly always followed the latter prac-
tice —enables the playwright to relax the
narrow bounds of theatrical representation
and to deal with a larger segment of human
experience. And remember that Shakespeare
—a third of his plays are omitted in their
modern presentation—had far more elbow-
room than you are allowed. The best advice
is to leave outside the frame of the picture—
to indicate in the opening dialogue, and not
to represent on the stage—the slow and
gradual processes and the separate lines of
causation leading up to your theme, and to
raise your curtain at the point where
separate lines have converged, and where the
crisis begins to move toward its solution
with some rapidity and continuity—that
point when the action could be carried con-
tinuously forward by one set of characters.
And—a special concession rendered necessary
by the late arrival —let nothing absolutely
essential to the comprehension of your play
be said or done during the first 10 minutes
after the rise of the curtain. (It takes that
time, too, for most women to sit down.)
The division of plays into acts is not solely
due to the limited power of attention of the
human mind, nor to the thirst of the human
male. It serves to give the play a rhythm,
and allows the audience to review the past
and anticipate the next act. There is more
pleasure—at least, to a cultivated palate—
in drinking three separate beers than in
draining the barrel at one draught. And let
each of your acts have a private descriptive
title of its own, thereby assuring for it an
individual character. Test your plot by
seeing if you can give the gist of it in 100
words. If you can’t, it is liable to weary
the limited measure of attention of the aver-
age audience. The first act should place
the situation clearly before the audience—it
may be largely exposition—but it. should
also carry us some way toward the heart of
your drama, or, at least, point distinctly
toward the quarter of the horizon where the
clouds are gathering up. Head your audi-
ence’s anticipation in a definite direction —
or else the audience might not remain to see
the next act. At least, prepare to fire your
fuse.
Do not keep from the audience funda-
mental secrets upon which your plot de-
pends, else you take from your spectators
the pleasing illusion that they are “gods con-
templating, with miraculous clairvoyance,
the fumbling of poor blind mortals through
the labyrinth of life.” The strongest reason
against keeping a secret from your audience
is that you cannot do it. Barrie’s “Little
Mary” joke was a secret for the first-night
audience; but everybody who went to that
play afterwards knew it before the curtain
rose. But there are dramatic effects to Be
got by keeping a secret till it is the right
theatrical time to disclose it. But fore-
shadow :do not forestall. Arouse in your audi-
ence the feeling of expectancy, but let it be a
vague expectancy. Your first act should
never end in a blank wall. Break a window
in it, and let it afford a glimpse of something
attractive beyond.
d*
You have now got to your second act.
Here the tension begins—‘the tension of the
audience: not necessarily of the actors. The
audience must be kept anticipating some-
thing imminent. “To engender, maintain,
suspend, heighten and resolve a state of ten-
sion—that is the main object of the
dramatist’s craft.”
A reasonable audience will endure a cer-
tain amount of exposition, a certain positing
of character and circumstance, before the
tension sets in; but when it once has set in,
the playwright must on no account suffer it
to relax until he deliberately resolves it just
before the fall of the curtain. There are, of
course, minor rhythms of tension and resolu-
tion, like the harmonic vibrations of a violin-
string. That is implied when we say that a
play consists of a great crisis worked out
through a series of minor crises. But the
main tension, once initiated, must never be
relaxed. If it is, the play is over, though the
author may have omitted to notice the fact.
And beware of the underplot. It should
not be a plot, but a very slight thread of in-
terest, connected with the main plot, but
involving no strain on the attention. And
each act must progress. It must not leave
the action just where it stood at the begin-
ning. Help your audience to divine the way
it is going, but leave it to wonder how it is
to get there. Set up finger-posts; but don’t
let them be too legible. Else you get “the
well-made play”—a lifeless, mechanical alge-
braic formula. And don’t forget your scene
a, faire— the obligatory scene which you have
led the audience to expect and desire. Archer
provides admirable and elucidative examples.
Above all, be plausible.
There is no time, in the rush of dramatic
action, for a mathematical calculation of
the chances for and against a given event,
or for experimental proof that such and
such a thing can or cannot be done. If a
thing seems plausible, an audience will
accept it without cavil; if it seems incredible
on the face of it, no evidence of its credi-
bility will be of much avail.
And the audience is more apt to swallow
the camel that happens “off” than the gnat
under the calcium.
Now for your last act. (Three or four acts
are the most useful length.) “If it were as
easy to write a good last act as a good first
act, we should be able to reckon three mas-
terpieces for every one that we can name at
present.” A crisis is comparatively easy to
dramatise; but few crises come to a definite
or dramatic conclusion; in life they usually
end in some petty compromise, or just get tired
and subside. Your chief difficulty will be to
find a crisis with an ending that is artisti-
cally satisfactory and yet dramatic. And
there aren’t many. Hence the frequency of
the “weak last act.” Archer remarks that
such an act is often necessary—there is no
obligation on you to end a play emphati-
cally. You might deliberately design an
anti-climax, or a philosophic last act that
would artistically relieve the tension of your
penultimate act. And the “curtain” is not
always necessary.
Beware, too, of blind-alley themes. (1)
The “white marriage” theme, where the
penniless Alice sells herself to Mr. Hogfat,
or where Lady Alicia mates with the “rough
diamond.” The audience knows that the
trouble can end in only one dramatic way
on the stage—-the marital happiness of the
parties. (2) Cold-storage revenge—where
the one party waits for four acts to vent his
vindictiveness on his enemy. This sort of
thing is not done now in life or on the stage.
(3) Heroic self-sacrifice —also outworn. (4)
The “my son” idiotcy, where the father
finds in a comparative stranger his own
“cheild,” and immediately becomes the dot-
ing parent. Shaw, in You Never Can Tell,
smashed that theatricality. (5) The absurd
oath of secrecy, kept for four acts merely to
sustain the plot. Go to life for your themes,
not to the theatre.
The rule that during a play there ought
to be a development of character should be
interpreted to mean that there should not be
a change of character —a thing that does
not happen in life within the time limits of
a play—but an unveiling and disclosure of
character. “A drama ought to bring out
character as the photographer’s chemicals
bring out the forms latent in the film.” And
“style, in prose drama, is the sifting of com-
mon speech.” Avert your face from that
dead form, blank verse. And, above all,
flee from the wrath that is to come if your
skill is so poor that you have to use the soli-
loquy and the aside. Still, a character on
the stage may be allowed to say “Damn” to
himself; and the convention of reading a let-
ter aloud may pass.
These are a few of the rules that don’t
exist for writing plays, gathered and aptly
illustrated in William Archer’s valuable
analysis.
Further information can be gained from
that sprightly book, The Footlights Fore
and Aft, by Channing Pollock (Badger, Bos-
ton). This book covers a wider range; the
author has had years of practical acquaint-
ance with the stage and in playwriting. His
is a book of the theatre, not of the drama.
If you were a manager and wanted a play
by a well-known author you would go to his
agent and ask if he had time to write it. If
so, you probably would pay him £5O for
attaching his name to a contract stipulating
that the MS. must be delivered on a cer-
tain date. Before that time, he would send
you a scenario of his story. If you accepted
that, you would give the author another £5O;
if you rejected it, all would be over between
you. The acceptance of the completed
“script” would be likely to cost you an
additional £lOO, and the whole £2OO would
be placed to your credit and deducted from
the first royalties accruing to the dramatist.
Authors’ royalties usually are on a sliding
scale. This author might get £lOO per week,
from which the agent would take 10 per
cent. This is the average remuneration.
The author of “The Lion and the Mouse,”
which has been acted pretty constantly by
two or three companies, has got from this
one play £50,000. Charles Klein has an in-
come from royalties of not less than £6OO a
week.
Every dramatist who has made a success
by “his first play” hasn’t. The professional
play readers know that every successful play-
wright has spent years and years writing
plays that wouldn’t sell. Playwrights must
be born and made. And the author assures
us that, in the United States, at least, all
plays submitted are read by professional
readers, as the demand far exceeds the
supply. But the average play submitted is
written by a person who can’t write plays.
Pollock tabulated some 200 plays submitted
to him as follows. The chief plots were: 37
dramas in which a woman goes to a man's
rooms at midnight; 31 farces based on mis-
taken identity; 28 farces built around the
necessity of a man lying to his wife; 24
dramas built of the question of love or duty;
22 dramas in which wealth is unexpectedly
derived from a mine or a patent; 20 com-
edies in which husband or wife ends the
philandering of wife or husband by seeming
to condone it; 19 dramas in which a woman
betrays a man and then saves him.
Plays which no manager would accept
are: (1) Those in which the crucial situa-
tion is the concealment of the heroine in a
man’s apartments; (2) those in which the
conclusion of the third act did not bring a
surprise calculated to make an audience sit
up and take notice ; (3) those beginning with
a conversation between a maid and a butler;
(4) those that conceal a character behind a
screen; (5) those that conclude with the
finding of a bundle of papers.
And, finally: “It’s a wise author that
knows his own play on its first night.”
Wanted: An Australian One-Act
Play
Having had your kindergarten lesson,
apply it. There are three repertory organ-
isations in Australia who are willing to
produce Australian plays: the Adelaide Lit-
erary Theatre, the Melb. Repertory Theatre,
and the Sydney Stage Society. The manage-
ment of each of these organisations have
expressed themselves willing favorably to
consider an Australian one-act play selected
in competition by this page. Gregan
McMahon states that he will judge it chiefly
from the view-point of practicability.
This page, therefore, offers an author’s
fee of £2 2s. for the best one-act Australian
play submitted within the next three months.
It will award the prize to the best practical
play that will act within 45 minutes —a
rough test is to reckon that each of your
type-written pages of MS. will occupy one
minute in performance—and will forward
copies of the winning play, and of any others
that show decided dramatic ability, to the
three organisations mentioned for possible
production. In adition, this page will criti-
cise a number of the better plays sent in,
and will reserve the right to print portions
of any of the plays.

The Salon, under which title the admir-
able Art and Architecture makes its appear-
ance in the hands of a new editor, Nicholas
Shields, and a new publisher, Weston, Wil-
son Go., aims at being more than the journal
of the N.S.W. Institute of Architects. The
July-August issue is beautifully produced,
and proclaims itself a thoroughly satisfac-
tory artistic shilling’s worth. The most
valuable article contains a series of repro-
ductions of details from a number of the
more interesting plans for the Federal Capi-
tal competition, while, when certain of the
difficulties in the reproduction of some of
the other plans are remedied, a further series
is promised. Articles on the Federal Gov-
ernment House, Sydney, on the prospects of
mural painting in Australia, by Norman
Garter, and information on architectural sub-
jects give the magazine an interest to all art
lovers, and indicate The Salon’s aim to
free itself as much as possible from its mere
technical appeal.
“Y.Z.” : I have found the following nara-
graph, from a Bulletin item by T. B.
Clegg, in four publishers’ circulars to date:
If, to be “one of the best sellers,” and conse-
quently, fill one’s purse, is hot the sole object of
your author, he may gain some reward for his act of
creation. He has brought a new life into the world.
It may be a poor thing, but it is his own; and he
cherishes this often maimed, misshapen bit of crea-
tion with the self-satisfied affection of a parent—if
the world buffets it, what matter, it is the way of
the world, says Sir Egoist, and they could not ap-
preciate all that lies in mj r beautiful child. He has
his consolations, truly!
It will probably appear in 400 more before
it finishes its round. Publishers dearly love
that sort of gospel for beginners in the liter-
ary mill, and are filled with disgust when the
more hardened word-stringer displays symp-
toms of outgrowing the fond, fatuous, pater-
nal stage, and insists on his beautiful
literary kids bringing a little oof to the
homestead. I’ve had dealings with many
publishers, but have never met one who for
one minute was running his factory as a
doting papa. Mr. Publisher stands in loco
parentis to a large family, but Lord help the
little beggars that bring no money home.

Why Suffer from Sore Throat ?
You need not do so for one hour longer than you
choose, for Science has discovered a rapid cure for
Sore Throat and its allied conditions, like Tonsillitis.
This is Wulfing’s Formamint.
“ I have never had Sore Throat myself since I began
to use Formamint, although I suffered periodically before,”
writes a physician in The Practitioner , one
of thousands of doctors who not only prescribe
Formamint to cure their patients’ Sore Throats, but
also habitually use it themselves for that purpose.
Sore Throat and those infectious diseases of
which it is a symptom, are due to germs which find
an ideal breeding-place in the throat, and, under
certain conditions, multiply with terrible rapidity,
infecting the body with their poison.
Danger of Diphtheria.
Formamint destroys these germs so
rapidly that when a physician mixed a little
Formamint with water and added it to the
germs taken from the throat of a patient
dangerously ill with Diphtheria they were
all killed within ten minutes.
Formamint should always be borne in
mind, not only as a cure, but also as a
preventive, when infectious diseases are
prevalent. By killing the germs causing
them, it prevents such diseases as Tonsil-
litis, Mumps, Whooping Cough, Measles,
Scarlet Fever, Consumption and Diphtheria.
For this reason, doctors always use
Formamint to safeguard themselves when
attending infectious diseases.

Formamint’s marvellous power of curing and
preventing throat diseases is vividly shown by the
following abstract of a case recently published in
The General Practitioner :
Two children with Diphtheria were taken from
their home to a hospital, where one of them died.
The mother and another child who had occupied
the same room with the patients complained of
Sore Throat. They were in obvious danger of
Diphtheria. Formamint Tablets were given to
them and the other people in the house, and not
one got ill. •
Evidence of this kind might be multiplied
almost indefinitely to prove the wonderful power
Formamint has of curing patients of dangerous and
infectious throat diseases and preventing other
people catching them.
A Warning to the Public.
Stimulated by the success of Wulfing’s
Formamint, many preparations claiming to
be as good are being offered to the public.
This claim is false. Wulfing’s Forma-
mint marks the discovery of a new chemical
compound, which is manufactured by a
patented process and can only be produced
by the firm of Messrs. A. Wulfing & Co.
To obtain the certainty of cure of all
forms of Sore Throat and immunity against
germ diseases, insist on having Wulfing’s
Formamint and take no substitute for it..
It can be obtained of all Chemists, in
bottles of fifty tablets. Write for free sample
to-day to A. Wulfing & Co., 17 Bond Street,
Sydney, N.S.W., mentioning “The Bulletin,
and enclosing two penny stamps towards postage.

Glenelg, Adelaide’s front-door and popu-
lar watering-place, is to have its breakwater
and boat-harbor at last. It has waited
nearly 80 years since Governor Hindmarsh
landed in what he called Hobson’s “Bay”
it being an open roadstead in which the Eye
of Faith is required to see any bay at all.
The P. and O. boats anchored there for
years, but the Orient craft never would, and
for the last 20 years the place has been left
to yachts —which on any big blow either
run for the Port river or get driven ashore
on the sands. The yacht club says it has
sustained £2OOO worth of damage in the
five years since the Price Government pro-
mised a breakwater. Unfortunately, the
town itself has only just finished paying off
£25,000 of debentures —money spent long
ago in damming the Patawalonga mouth, a
job which quickly silted up and became use-
less ; so there is neither cash nor confidence
to be had locally. The Peake Government
has now promised to endorse the Price and
Verran promises, and get to work. It is
just a matter of solid pile-driving in shal-
low water and sand, for there isn’t a rock
nearer than the Rock of Ages.
“The Religious Editor” : How defenceless
the average home is against the well-meaning
time-yvasters who, for want of a better word,
we Pall “visitors”! In the leisured and bet-
ter organised circles, the rich man or woman
has a hireling, but dignified, liar, who chal-
lenges the enemy on the threshold, and holds
him up until his employer says whether he
is to be admitted or turned away. The small
householder, with no hired front-door guard,
answers the bell himself, expecting to find
merely an inoffensive dun or canvasser, and,
on letting down the drawbridge, finds him-
self rushed and overpowered by one or more
bandits, who have come to steal his time and
refreshments, under pretence of seeing after
his health and well-being. As one who lives
by spoiling paper, I am jealous of my spare
hours. They mean money to me, and I
object to being deprived of them. Yet I
find myself being robbed on an average two
nights a week. I wouldn’t mind if my in-
terrupters came for (say) half an hour, and
contrived to be bright and interesting for
(say) 15 minutes. In return I would try to
glow for the same period, and we would
part cheerfully. But every caller has the
idea that he or she has to put in the whole
evening. And they do—but only by dint of
scraping up conversation, inspecting books,
furtive yawns, and the free use of (my)
stimulants. They make no attempt to sub-
edit their conversation as they go along, but
cruelly and shamelessly expand paragraphs
to Columns, and columns to full pages with
illustrations, cross-headings and footnotes.
Last night our house was invaded by two
ladies who would have been agreeable if they
had condensed themselves into half a column
each. But they stayed from eight till eleven,
and gave myself and wife bound volumes of
featherweight conversation, dull reminis-
cences, inferior theatrical criticism and
libellous gossip of no interest whatever.
Another caller is a whole library of reminis-
cences, and we get a complete set of volumes
at each visit, with “Compliments of the
AuthCr” Written on the fly page. I like to
be friendly; but, though time is getting
scarcer and more preeious every year, most
people seem to be growing more garrulous
and wasteful of the clock. You see it in
every department of life, from politics to
petty socialities. My ideal visitor is an
elderly gentleman, who, after asking after
my health, and saying he is “all right—
never better,” puts a large red handkerchief
over his face and goes off to sleep, leaving
me free to read or scribble as I choose. Some
night, when I can spare the time, I will
wake him up gently and tell him . what a
treasure he is.
“10-2-4”: It has come, as I have always
said it would. Consider this cable which
the dailies published last week: —
Kingston (Ontario).
Elder Johnson, a Seventh Day Adventist, speaking
at the Lord’s Day Alliance Conference, offered £2OO
to anyone producing satisfactory evidence of Divine
authority for keeping the first day of the week as the
Christian Sabbath and Day of Rest.
In other words, the Elder lays a level £2OO
that the cheerful citizens who on Sundays
go surf-bathing, play cricket, dance, leap,
bound and generally perform the operation
known to Wowserdom as “defiling the Sab-
bath,” cannot be shown, by reference to the
Bible, to be sinners at all. And without a
doubt, if the wager were submitted to the
arbitrament of any educated body of men,
the Elder would win hands down. The
Sabbath, or day of cessation from
day which the Bible enjoins must be kept
holy—is the seventh day of the week, which
is supposed to be our Saturday. The Book
which the Sabbatarian clamors to have “kept
open ” lays particular stress on the point
that no work whatever shall be done on the
Sabbath—though no one is forbidden to toil
or revel on Sunday. (See Deut. v. 13-15
and Exod. xiii. 12). The very Early Chris-
tians, good Bible students that they were,
observed the Sabbath with the utmost devo-
tion, and never thought of worshipping on
the Pagan festival of Sunday or Dies Solis.
Then the R.C. Church arose and abolished
the Sabbath and declared it bogus, and,
simultaneously, the Roman Emperors of the
day became converts to the new faith, and
presently, with the assistance of the Church,
brought the modern Sunday—known some-
times among the baser or more illiterate sort
of Wowsers as “the Sabbath” —into being.
The gist of the Canon Law of Rome on this
subject is to be found (vide the “Encyclo-
paedia Britannica” or any other standard
reference book) in the Decretals of- Gre-
gory, as follows : —•
We decree that all Sundays be observed fronj
vespers to vespers, and that all unlawful work be
abstained from, so that in them trading or legal
proceedings be not carried on, etc.
In fact, Sunday is as much a Romish insti-
tution as Good Friday or Holy Thursday.
I can understand convinced R.C.’s following,
as regards neglect of the Sabbath and
observance on Sunday, the dictates of a
Church which they believe to be inspired
from Heaven to order things as it wills, but
why frantic Wowsers who hotly deny the
infallibility of that Church, in every other
direction, should bow, to its decree in this
matter is a mystery. But, then, most of the
actions of the uproarious saints in the mis-
shapen clothes and small flat hats are mys-
terious and inexplicable on any logical
grounds.

IN THE HEART OF THE BLUE MOUNTAINS

“THE CARRINGTON,” KATOOMBA.
Nestling in the heart of the ever-alluring Blue Mountains, with their wealth of rugged scenery and misty valleys, Katoomba
presents illimitable attractions to the Tourist.
Summer or Winter the air is deliciously crisp, and such famous Beauty Spots as the Leura and Katoomha Palls, the Federal
Pass, the Jamieson Yalley, Orphan Pock, and Three Sisters are situated within easy walking distance of the Town.
The Tourist, for health or pleasure, will find Katoomba an ideal centre from which he may visit various sights, and, in the
“Carrington,” an up-to-date and luxurious hostelry, situated in its own extensive grounds.
The “Carrington” overlooks, from Katoomba’s highest point, Leura, Spring wood, Penrith, and, in dim distance, the misty haze
of the Metropolis. It is sumptuously furnished throughout, and every .detail, tending to increase the comfort and enjoyment of
the visitor, is carefully studied. Since its inception, “The Carrington” has always been patronised by all Vice-regal representatives.
“THE CARRINGTON” to-day stands second
to none in the Commonwealth. Sufficient
accommodation is provided for 120 guests.
The Dining Room is exceedingly spacious
and will seat fully 400 with comfort. Each
Bedroom is richly decorated and furnished ;
no two rooms being alike. A large section
of the first floor is devoted to a luxurious
Lounge and palatial Drawing and Music
Rooms. A well-appointed Billiard Room in-
troduces an additional source of up-to-date
amusement, whilst an up-to-date Bar, lately
completed, at a cost of over £5OOO, faces the
main street.
The Cuisine of the “Carrington” is in charge
of a capable Chef , and is as perfect as it is
possible to conceive. The Kitchen —a very
important detail in a Mountain Hotel, where
keen appetites are the rule —is fully supplied
with the latest improvements in Stoves and
appurtenances for the Culinary Art.
LIGHTING—A modern, complete Lighting
Plant, of 1000 Kilowatts, has been
installed by the Management at great ex-
pense. This Plant—large enough in itself to
light the whole Town —ensures an ever-ready
source of steady light and power. Electric
Light is installed in each Bedroom, whilst
the main part of the Hotel is illuminated by
light clusters.
Ventilation is made a special study in the
“Carrington.” During the Winter months,
the Building throughout is efficiently heated
by the latest system of American Radiators.
This system, combined with scientific ventila-
tion, ensures a maximum of comfort in the
chilliest weather.
For the use of Touring Motorists, a roomy
and modern Garage is a feature. It is thor-
oughly equipped with every Motor necessary,
and each Car is housed under a separate
lock and key. It is here that the famous
“Itala” Cars, used on the Jenolan Caves’
Trip,, are stalled. .
The Tariff Scale in vogue at the “Carring-
ton” is quite in keeping with the charges
of Modern Tourist Hotels. The Summer
Tariff, extending from November to April, is
£3/10/- per week, or 12/6 per day—the
Winter Tariff, extending from May to Octo-
ber, £3/3/- per week, or TO/- per day. These
charges cover full attendance, etc.
The “Carrington” also possesses a modernly-
equipped Hydropathic Department, for the
use of its patrons. This Department is
under the charge of experts, who are pre-
pared to treat Ladies and Gentlemen in ac-
cordance with medical instructions. High
Frequency, Electric Light, Yapor Baths and
Electric Baths (Faradic or Galvanic). An
expert Masseur is always
in attendance.
Daily Motor Trips to
The Jenolan Caves.
The Management of the “Carrington” maintains an
up-to-date fleet of the famous “Itala” Cars, to
conduct an exclusive Motor Service to the Caves.
The Caves, 47 miles away, are reached comfortably
in these Cars, which are noted for their easy riding
and extreme reliability. The Cars leave the
“Carrington” at 9 a.in., and proceed to the Jenolan
Caves by Blackheath and Medlow Bath, returning
in the afternoon, in time to connect with the
Sydney train, ,if required. Return fare from
Katoomba, £2.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Spring and the Lord Mayor.
The dust that yesterday was mud
Doth blind and choke all living men;
To-morrow there will come a flood
And make it into mud again.
The Winter is asleep, and Spring
Smiles with a blue and radiant smile;
Upon a shelf your papers fling,
And walk with me a dusty mile.
The good sun shining in the sky,
The treasury of Summer loots.
(To-day you get it in your eye ;
Last week you got it on your boots.)
The south wind is a zephyr now;
Last week it laid the wide seas waste.
(To ev’ry cloud of dust we bow;
Last week we met it as a paste.)
My name is Mud. The wind of Death
Will one day dry me up, and then
A mumbling parson, scant of breath,
Shall say above my dust “Amen.”
How wonderful are Death and Dust!
How warm and dry the dusty town.
(The good Lord Mayor, so grave and just,
Is thinking deeply in his gown!)
Lo! the Solution comes this way!
Upon the street descends a flood !
The Lord Mayor’s barrel on a shay
Transforms the blessed dust to mud!
N.s.w.
FOE.
A Financial Suggestion.
Does anyone know what it means to spend
£2,000,000 in New South Wales? —Pathetic inquiry
of Chief Commissioner of Railways.
I want to spend two million pounds,
To spend it here in New South Wales,
Where every pleasant thing abounds,
And only cash or credit fails;
Give me the splosh, and I will demonstrate
The glad resources of our shining State.
With various pigments choice and wet
The city should be painted pink,
While at a gushing rivulet
Of amber joy the world might drink;
Yes, I should shout for all the thirsty town,
And risk the Wowser’s homily and frown.
Cash I should squander through the land
Till gladness laughed in all the ways,
And youth and pleasure, hand in hand,
Became a fashionable craze.
I’d even try again to introduce
The kissing habit, falling to disuse.
About the harbor up and down
Wherever grows a quiet tree,
A thousand friends should sit apd drown
Their sorrows —"possibly in tea —
And everyone I always should invite
To picnics which should last all day and
night.'
And I should spend a lot of gold
In buying pictures to adorn
A noble building that might hold
What boldest critics would not scorn,
And statues I should plant about the park
To shelter tender lovers after dark.
And I should, subsidise all games,
Excepting only politics,
And aid the gentle crank who tames
Our garden herbs to fancy tricks ;
In fact, I fancy I could make a splash
In fifty nice directions with my cash.
And with the surplus still in hand
I should construct a line or two,
Short links to open up the land
As politicians will not do;
For after helping pleasure, art and sport,
I still could give both North and South a
port.
I want to spend two millions pounds,
To spend it here in New South Wales
On girls, and wines, and sights, and sounds,
And some of it on metal rails—
Port Stephens and old Eden I should wake
For less than minor duplications take.
N.s.w.
CURSE O’ MOSES.
Aftermath.
N.s.w.
Flowers are around me,
Stars are on high,
Slowly the moon-ship
Sinks in the sky.
Red are the ripples
Flooding the hill.
While lonely the valley lies,
Shadowed and still.
Down the. old paths• I go,
Tangled in vine.
Dead days leap up to me,
Hours that were mine.
Heavy I step and slow,
I am afraid!
Hunting in shadows
The ghost of a glade.
Here once the moonshine gleamed
Clasping the bough..
Oh, those lost bowers of mine,
Where are they now?
Naught can the future bring.
Nothing can count.
Now waste are the magic meads
Levelled the mount.
This is the corpse of me,
Passion and pain.
Just a grey ghost of me
Haunting again,
Soft, shadowed paths of fern
Brittle and sere.
This is the pain I know
Year after year.
Lost is the soul of me.
Burdened and dead.
Gone is the afterglow,
Youthtime has fled.
Oh. for the lure of you,
You who forget.
Just the warm touch of you,
Ere the stars set.
CARO.

THE ADMIRABLE AUSTIN
A Splendid Hill Climber
lO : 15 s 18/24 H.p.
The following extract from The Autocar, of June 29, 1912, is instructive:
“Mr. H. C. Holder, on his 40-h.p. Austin, was third on time in lmin.
11 2-ssec. in the open event.
“The 40-h.p. Austin took the corners —well, just as they should be taken,
and clipped 3-ssec. off his time on his second essay.
“It is interesting to note as an example of consistent running and driving,
that Mr. H. C. Holder’s time last year on the same 40-h.p. Austin Car that he
used this year was lmin. 10 4-5 sec., or 3-5 of a second faster than this
year in the open class with a heavier load. Mr. Holder’s Austin carried the
heavier load, some 2cwt. more than last year, so that when this is borne in
mind, it will be seen that his time is actually better than last year, as he only
lost 3-ssec. in transporting an extra 2cwt. to the summit.”

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) by taking
some care as to ’methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.

Insufficiently stainped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.

ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” col-
umn all contributions submitted, whether in the
form of Political. Social or other Articles, Verse,
Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially accept-
able), Paragraphs, Letters or Newspaper Clivpings.
All communications will be regarded as strictly cunfi-
den tial.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS.
when the address is specified and stamps are en-
closed. But no MS. will be returned, or preserved,
unless these conditions are observed. When MS. is
forwarded without stamps for return it will be un-
derstood that the copy is to be destroyed if not ac-
ceptable. Personal applications for return of MS.
cannot receive attention. The Bulletin desires,
further, those sending MS. to distinctly understand
that it is not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of MS.

THURSDAY, SEPTEMBER 5, 1912.
THE NORTHERN TERRITORY
LEASEHOLD.
The Bulletin was recently, moved to re-
mark that the poor old Northern Territory
seemed to get everything that was going
except the one essential —the direct railroad.
The railroad can’t be granted without rousing
the animosity of somebody, for there are two
parties, and these parties clamor for two
widely different routes, and, as a railway
can’t go two roads at once, the construction
of it either way must aggravate the crowd
which wants it to go the other way. To
aggravate any sort of crowd means the loss
of votes, and the loss of votes is bad for any
Ministry. But in the matter of new officials
and new regulations the Territory suffers
from no scarcity. Of regulations, especially,
it has more than enough.
* * * *
The Labor party, both State and Federal, is
committed to the doctrine of the public
ownership of land. It believes in the lease-
hold as against the freehold, and it desires
to secure the unearned increment for the
people. The idea is altogether commend-
able, but there was never yet a commendable
idea that couldn’t be turned into disaster
by foolish or ignorant administration,
and amid a multitude of land regulations a
suspicion is engendered that the leasehold
principle may bring the Territory to grief.
The Government of this great Federal pos-
session of over 500,000 square miles was
provided for, in the first place, by an Act
of Parliament. The Act contained very few
details, but, in a general sort of way, it
validated any ordinances that might be issued
for the control and management of the
Federal estate. It was something in the
nature of a blank cheque—in other words, it
legalised something or anything, without
Parliament having any clear knowledge of
what that something or anything might be.
The land policy of the Territory is based on
the perpetual leasehold system, which is sup-
posed to preserve the advantage of public
ownership, while giving the tenant a title
which is almost equal to the freehold. But
the term “perpetual leasehold” counts for
little. All terms count for little unless there
are realities behind them. Perpetual lease-
hold, if the conditions are sufficiently easy,
may be even better for the tenant than free-
hold ; while, if the conditions, as regards re-
appraisement and other matters, are
sufficiently onerous, it may be, for all practi-
cal purposes, a mere tenancy at the land-
lord’s option. It may be a thing which the
landgrabber would rush at and seize with both
hands, and it may be something that even
the legitimate, hard-working settler wouldn’t
touch with a long clothes-prop. There
is reason to suspect that the perpetual lease-
hold scheme which has been devised for the
Territory is something which isn’t calculated
to attract settlers.
* * * *
The Ordinance divides pastoral lands into
three sections. Of first-class pastoral land
perpetual leases may be granted for as much
as 500 square miles. Second-class cow and
bullock land may be leased in blocks up to
1000 square miles, and a single lessee may
hold, in nominal perpetuity, as much as 3000
square miles of third-class beeve land. This
looks wildly lavish, for if there is any land
in the Territory which requires nearly
2,000,000 acres to constitute a living area,
the fact has not yet become known. Agri-
cultural leases may cover as much as 2560
acres, and mixed farming and grazing leases
as much as 64,000 acres. Then there are
town and miscellaneous leases. All these are
supposed to be perpetual, subject to a re-
appraisement of rent every 14 years in the
case of town lands, and every 21 years in
other cases. Also, by way of giving things
a start, the first 5000 agricultural settlers
are to be free of rent either for the lifetime
of the settler, or for 21 years, whichever is
the longer period; and sundry other leases
carry a 10 years exemption from rent. This
is a liberal method of. promoting settlement.
And in an empty country, which has always
been cursed by the lack of railway com-
munication, and which, apparently, has had
its railway indefinitely postponed because
the Government can’t decide on a route,
liberality is necessary. It might be a great
deal cheaper to build the railway and save a
lot of the liberality, but that is another
matter.
Then the Government, in its desire to
safeguard the leasehold principle and retain
the unearned increment for the nation, be-
gins to hedge its lavishness with restrictions.
The lavishness looks vast and wide, but on
the other hand the restrictions are narrow.
To begin with, the country leases are sub-
ject to re-classification. Thus, the man who
has 3000 square miles of soil, specified as
the poorest sort of pastoral land, may find
his area re-specified as the best agricultural
land, on which the maximum holding is fixed
at one square mile. The re-classification
can’t happen without a considerable amount
of notice, and even when it does happen the
lessee may still hold his 3000 square miles,
but in the end he must pay rent at first-class
country rates instead of at seventh-class
country rates. It is most probable that this
variation may serve him right, and in regard
to the lessee with 3000, or 1000, or even 500
square miles of land, the matter isn’t of
much importance. Such a lessee clamors for
extinction, but, all the same, the business
seems incongruous. A matter of real
importance, however, concerns the town
lands The nf U» Q Ft n f ''orlh
on which the Anthony Hordern or Mark
1 oy of the Norm erects his is
nominally perpetual, but the rent is
subject to re-appraisement every 14 years.
It is also subject to re-appraisement at
any time inside the 14 years, “if by
reason of the carrying out of any public
work in the Northern Territory any increase
in the value of the leased land takes place.”
Now, almost any kind of public work will
increase the selling value of land, though it
may not necessarily increase its value to the
lessee who has put up a costly building on
it. Thus, the establishment of a railway
station on one side of St. Mary’s Cathedral,
in' Sydney, and a national theatre on the
other side, and a Mclntosh stadium in
front, and a monster drapery behind, end
a pub adjacent, would vastly increase the
value of the bit of ground on which St.
Mary’s stands; but it might not enhance
the value of St. Mary’s as a place of wor-
ship, which is the only thing it is fitted for.
And in a new, fresh, raw, crude country,
public works will be going on all the time,
and going on everywhere, and re-appraise-
ments may happen any day. A perpetual
lease with a re-appraisement every 14 years
is really only a lease for 14 years, because
the revised rental may make the position un-
tenable. And a perpetual lease, which is
subject to a possible re-appraisement at any
time, is no better than a weekly or monthly
tenancy. The mere fact of a lessee putting
up a building like that of the A.M.P. Society
in Sydney, in a new Northern Territory city,
would bring around it other similar build-
ings. Then would come pavements, a wood-
blocked road, drains, gutters, tramways, per-
haps a railway station, possibly a telegraph
station, and a leper hospital and a gaol, and
all manner of public works. Each and every
one of these might shove up the rent, quite
apart from the regular and periodical shove-
uo which would arise through the increase
of population and the consequent enhance-
ment of land values. There is no evidence
that the rents would be extortionate, but
they might be: and the uncertainty of the
business is calculated to prevent the poor
old Territory having an'"' cities at all, or
enough settlers to be worth mentioning.
The Bulletin has urged for years nast
the opening of the neglected deep-water ports
of N.S.W.. Victoria and other States, the
building of railways to make them accessi-
ble, and the establishment, at places like
Port Stephens, Twofold Bay, W esternport
and Portland, of cities whereof the State
should be sole ground landlord for all time.
But it never dreamed that these cities could
be built if the man who hired a bit of land
whereon to put £lOO,OOO or _£2so,ooo’s worth
of warehouse or factory didn’t know from
week to week, and hardly knew from day to
day, whether his ground rent wouldn’t be
raised by an irresponsible landlord to a
figure which would make his investment
quite unprofitable. Fat, whose other name
is Private Enterprise, is a hard master, but
even Fat grants ground leases for 50 or 60
or sometimes 99 years without re-ap-
praisement. If he wasn’t fairly liberal
in this direction no one would build six-
storey structures on his property. He is
moved by no high and generous motives in
acting as he does. He simply recognises that
man is a gambling animal; that the possi-
bilities of the unearned increment are among
the most fascinating of business gambles;
and that unless he shares the possibilities of
the gamble with his tenant, he may not get
any tenant, and then there will be no unearned
increment to gamble with. The one thing
that Fat really knows is how to make money
for himself. If the Australian Government,
which is the landlord —otherwise the Fat —
of the Northern Territory, thinks it can
screw more out of its tenants than he does,
it is too sanguine for this world. Yet it
seems to have that idea. The preservation
of the unearned increment for the nation is a
great idea. So, for that matter, is the
preservation of the gold and silver and cop-
per deposits for the nation. But _ gold
and silver and copper and unearned incre-
ment are the attractions which lure people
out into the wilderness, and if the pioneers
aren’t allowed to annex some part of them,
they are apt to remain in Paddington or
Collingwood, and go to the sixpenny picture
show.
It is well to remember that the public
ownership of land is not a gospel in itself.
It is not an end —only a means to an end, the
end being the general diffusion of wealth and
happiness. Provided the laws prevent the
aggregation of great estates, and the soil is
fairly well distributed among those who want
soil, and are capable of putting it to good
use, freehold is about as good as State owner-
ship. One is a device by which each man
possesses his little enterprise; the other is a
method by which he is a shareholder in a
big enterprise. One way he gets his indivi-
dual bit of unearned increment and manages
it himself; the other way he gets his pro-
portion as a citizen, and consequently a
shareholder of the great mass of un-
earned increment, provided people like King
O’Malley and McGowen and Holman and
Hughes don’t happen to muddle it away.
The drawback of private land ownership
lies in the difficulty of preventing the estab-
lishment of great estates end great land
monopolies. The drawback of public land
ownership lies in the difficulty of managing
so vast a business, and the possibility of a
correct or incapable person at the head of
the Public Estate Department becoming as
great an evil as the private landlord. Pri-
vate ownership is advocated, not only by
people who really believe in it on principle,
but also by thieves, land boomers and swin-
dlers. Public ownership is advocated, not
only by people who honestly believe in it,
but also by political frauds, who see, in the
management of the public estate, gigantic
opportunties for self-aggrandisement and even
peculation. It is impossible to bring about a sort
of Soil Millennium merely by resolving that
all the unearned increment shall belong to
the community, for unless the pioneers come
there will be no unearned increment, and in
a country which has still plenty of elbow-
room, the pioneers won’t come unless there
is some better prize ahead of them than in
the crowded lands of their origin. The idea
of moving from the flesh-pots of Adelaide,
and the bean-feasts of Melbourne, and the
fatted calf of Sydney, and putting ur> a
costly block of buildings in a raw new citv
which has no railway communication with
anywhere, and on a bit of ground the rent of
which may rise indefinitely on all sorts of
occasions, is not attractive.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Railway that is Built from the
North.
The Fisher Government has made some-
thing in the shape of an indefinite pronounce-
ment of its policy in the matter of the
Northern Territory railway, and from the
standpoint of Australia’s interests things
could hardly be worse. The misfortune of
the case lies, as usual, with the two rival
routes. The Commonwealth is pledged abso-
lutely to the completion of the present un-
finished Port Darwin-Port Augusta line, up
through the centre of Australia. That was
one of the conditions on which the Territory
was handed over, and it can in no way be
honorably evaded. Besides, the logic of
circumstances clamors for the work. On
the other hand, a section desires to have the
agreement broken, and the line deviated at
great length and much extra cost through
the eastern States. A curious idea seems to
prevail that Australia is a place with no
future, and that it will never want more
than one railway from south to north, and
that the party which doesn’t snatch this
chance is lost. Hence the cat-fight and the
complication. As matters stand, any Govern-
ment which proposes to build the railway
right through the centre of Australia, as per
agreement, will lose votes in Queensland and
N.S.W., and the one which proposes to build
it by the deviation will lose votes in S.A.
and Westralia, while any Government which
attempted to build both lines at once would,
in the present state of the finances, burst up.
Meanwhile, the Cabinet which does nothing
definite may postpone the row for a long
time —perhaps for all its political life. Un-
fortunately, this also means postponing the
settlement of the Territory, and putting off
the date when it will be possible to rush
troops overland to its defence, should defence
be badly needed and an enemy in possession
of the sea, and on that settlement and that
sort of defence the very life of Australia may
any day depend. The present idea is to
resume railway-building from the north,
and construct very slowly—even the neces-
sary Bill will probably not be passed until
next Parliament—the little section as far as
Katherine River, and then further consider
the position. The scrap to Katherine River
—or even perhaps to Bitter Springs or All
Saints Well—is equally on the road to Ade-
laide or to Brisbane, and so much can be
done without the party in power committing
itself and arousing serious trouble. It
represents a handy compromise. Unfortu-
nately, though this scheme may be good poli-
tics, it isn’t good policy or good patriotism.
If the work were commenced from the south
every mile constructed would serve to bring
Australian troops nearer to the scene of
trouble, if trouble should come. A line built
from the north will—until it is completed
some time in the dim future —only serve to
carry further into the heart of Australia
any enemy who may arrive by sea n nd
possess himself of Port Darwin, but it won’t
make it a whit easier to bring a single man
or gun from Adelaide or Melbourne or Syd-
ney for purposes of protection. Six
hundred miles of iron highway, built from the
south northward, would redeem part of the
Territory from its isolation by giving it direct
communication with the big southern cities,
and would start settlement at that end of
the country which is furthest from any pos-
sible attack. Six hundred miles of line, built
from the north southward, would start settle-
ment where the new wealth lay handiest to
an enemy, but it wouldn’t make any part of
the Territory less isolated than it is now, in
the sense that the place would still depend,
just as much as it does to-day, and as it did
100 years ago, on the sea for its connection
with the other States. If an enemy happens
along while the railway is unfinished, and
the control of the sea is lost, then it is plain
that the enemy, with the Territory railway
to help him, can advance south as far as one
side of the gap where the work is unfinished,
and the Australian troops can hurry north as
far as the other side of the gap. Building
from the south means moving the gap north-
ward, and making the area which Australia
can hold larger, and that which the enemy
can conveniently occupy smaller. Building
from the north means shoving the gap south-
ward —in other words, it means less terri-
tory saved to Australia, and more lost to the
invader. If these points are not worth con-
sideration, then it is hard to see where in all
this country there can be a point that is
worth consideration. Of course, construc-
tion from the south involves nmking a
plunge, and either going back on tne agree-
ment made with S.A., which is unthinkable,
or breaking with the deviation party in the
eastern States, which is unpleasant. But
the plunge must be made some day, unless it
is proposed to leave the question for the
Japanese or Chinaman or German to decide
and let him build a track to suit himself
at our expense.

The Muddle in Maoriland.
“Maorilander” writes: —
Premier Massey, of Maoriland, is already discover-
ing that it is easier to promise than to perform.
For so heavy a man, he must needs tread very deli-
cately just now. In the first place . he was virtually
put into power by the vote of the big landed interest,
while still owing much to the great body of inde-
pendent Democrats who own no land at all, and are
enthusiasts for closer settlement. Now, if Massey
honestly keeps the promises he has made to the inde-
pendent or moderate Democrats, he must in the end
antagonise his loyal Conservative following; while,
if he adheres to his professed anti-borrowing policy,
there must be such an apparent shrinkage of public
‘works as will speedily incense the popular majority.
The difficulty-of the position is not to be evaded.
On the hustings* Massey announced his determina-
tion to foster settlement in the backbloeks. But
to make the available backbloeks accessible to settle-
ment, much money must be spent on needed com-
munications—on roads and bridges, and other things
not generally classed as reproductive. Many of the
backbloeks, already in some parts settled, are starving
for roads. There are some partially settled dis-
tricts so badly roaded as to be almost isolated —
districts whose women have not seen a town since
. the settlement was made, and whose children- have
never seen a town at all. Some of Massey's
staunchest supporters in Parliament are already de-
manding that relief shall be afforded to such dis-
tricts, so that for the unoccupied parts it is desir-
able to settle there can be very little hope. The
thing that Massey should do, of course —and the
thing that Massey will never dare to do —-is to cut
up some of the big estates along the railways. Be
dare rot do that, because he knows pe r 'ectly well
that the moment Massey is mad enough to snap
>s fingers in the teeth of his masters, Massey will
be no more. An anti-borrowing policy is a good
■'olicv; but it is merely farcical to pretend that
Massey is a serious anti-borrower. How, otherwise,
does Massey stand? Fe has little to exnect from
O«toms, for in that department he is pledged to
reduce the burden, and he is a devout foreign-trader
in his heart. His so-called increase of the gradu-
ated land-tax amounts to next to nothing. A
Ministry so constituted dare not, attempt any con-
siderable increase of the tax on wealth. On the
oFer hand, there is to be faced the problem of the
influential Labor section. In Maoriland no Govern-
ment can live, once it incurs the enmity of Labor,
and at Massey Labor is already looking askance,
with a very sour eye. When the new Trades Hall was
opened in Auckland, a few days ago, Joseph Ward
was enthusiastically received, and the name of
Massey was greeted with thinly-veiled derision. And
this is only the beginning. Still, the Massey party
will probably hang on to office for some time yet,
if for no better reason than that certain huckstering
politicians, who have given it temporary support,
dare not vote against it without signing their own
warrant for political execution.

About High Prices and Low Sirrits.
It is the fashion, these times, to allege
that this is an era of phenomenally high
prices, and that though wages are much
better than they were 10 or 15 years ago,
yet the general whooping-up of the cost of
living has left the ordinary citizen just
where he was, or made him even worse off
than he was. But the ordinary citizen,
being a person who trusts in a vague sort
of way to his memory, and never studies
volumes of back statistics, or even verifies
his hazy impressions by looking up old news-
paper files, is not always the authority he is
commonly supposed to be. The rise in
prices has not mooped up all the worker’s
extra earnings. Even the trust and the
landlord have not scooped quite all his pro-
fits. He not only lives much better than in
the wretched low-price years immediately
before and immediately after the Common-
wealth was established —the years of soup-
kitchens and relief works—but he has a
larger surplus. Thus, in 1902. 267 out of
every 1000 inhabitants of the Common-
wealth had each a little bit in the Savings
Bank ; in 1911, 262 out of every 1000 were
thus provided. In 1902 the rather scarce
depositor had, on an average, £32 ss. lOd.
put away. In 1911, the much more numer-
ous one had £37 2s. 4d. The total amount
of savings in the banks of the people in-
creased between 1902 and 1911 by over
£26.000,000 —from about £33,000,000 to a
good deal more than £59,000,000. In other
words, the people accumulated nearly as
-much in these nine years of high nrices as
in the century or so that went before. In
1902, the savings of the people, if they had


been evenly distributed among the whole
population, would have represented £8 12s.
Sd. per head ; in 1911, they stood for £l3 Bs.
sd. per head. It is noteworthy that the
year in which the people had the biggest
surplus and added most to their savings was
last year—which was also the one when the
clamor about high prices became most con-
spicuous. In that one twelvemonth, more
than £6,000,000 were added to the accumu-
lations in the banks of the people. There
was never anything like it before. In the
four comparatively low-price years up to
1906 only about £5,000,000 were saved —-
say £1,300,000 per annum. There have
been occasions in Australia when the
people’s savings were actually diminishing,
but these were times of cheap living, when
a man could buy as much, in some lines, for
6d. as he now can for Is., and had
only sd. to buy the goods with,
where he now has Is. 3d. And all the above
figures take no account of the increase in
the investments of the people in friendly
societies, building societies, life assurance
offices and the like. High prices are un-
pleasant, but there remains the fact that
in Australia the masses of the people—the
laboring end artisan class —have never been
so well off as when prices were high. The
lady who writes to the paper signing herself
“Mother of Seven” is no doubt quite honest
in her statement that it is much more diffi-
cult to make ends meet than it was, say, 10
years ago. In the first place, she has read
in the paper that it is more difficult, and
she thinks she remembers it because she has
read it. In the second place, she has only a
general recollection of how things were 10
years ago, and the details have been lost.
In the third place, she was 10 years younger
then, and life looks much less rosy as grey
hairs accumulate. And in the fourth place,
she was only a Mother of One in those by-
gone days when all the world was gay—to
look back upon. And her young contempo-
rary of to-day—the bride who “Expects to
be the Mother of One” —wasn’t old enough
in the alleged times of cheap food, and cheap
wages, and unemployed, and soup-kitchens,
and_ charitable sand-shifting jobs, to realise
their glories. So she also trusts to popular
report, and says she doesn’t know what
things are coming to.
A Glittering but Empaupered Citizen.
All through history, from the first dim be-
ginning of things warlike, to the present day,
it has been the fashion to underpay the
soldier. The practice had its origin, prob-
ably, in the fact that the old-time warrior
was supposed to be in a position to do with-
out wages. War was frequent when the
world was young, and looting was regarded
with a sympathetic eye. An officer who
couldn’t amass sufficient in the course of
one campaign to keep him in comfort till
the next, was extraordinarily slow off the
mark and deficient in brain-power. Noble-
men and the sons of noblemen went into the
sweated, though profitable, industry with
much enthusiasm. By the time the good old
days of looting were over, the profession ot
arms had become fashionable. Ihousands
were prepared, in aristocratic countries such
as Britain, to pay large sums directly and
indirectly for commissions in a calling whicfi
remunerated its members on a starvation
basis, and offered but faint hope, of financial
advancement to the vast majority of those
who engaged in it. That is more or less the
position to-day. The wealthy youth ot ob-
scure birth automatically achieves a status
in London society by bursting into the
Household Brigade, or the 10th Hussars, or
the 9th Lancers, or any one of a dozen other
corps that might be cited. The ornate and
splendid uniforms of these regiments make a
powerful appeal to persons possessed of
sufficient money to pay the outrageous price
they cost, and sufficient vanity to wish to
wear them. And so, taking one thing with
another, the supply of British ofiicer shows
no serious sign of falling oil The Australian
Defence authorities are differently situated.
A soldier cuts no more ice in this country,
in a social manner of speaking, than a solici-
tor or a hat-importer. The uniform which
the local warrior wears is a serviceable-look-
ing but drab affair. No romantic traditions
cling to the garment as yet. It has not had
time to suggest, to the imaginative spectator
such stirring things as Balaclava charges and
the like; if it brings anything to the mind
of the onlooker it is a mental picture of a
robbed hen-roost or a Boer cottage piano
with an axe in its vitals. In short, there are,
up to now, no fashionable aids to popularity
as regards the profession of soldiering in
Australia. Regardless of this plain fact, the
authorities are proceeding in the matter of
remuneration on the basis of those countries
whose officers are mostly men of means, and
occasionally men of great wealth. The posi-
tion of the area ofiicer is a particularly glar-
ing case in point. At a time when the
cost of living is not quite so low as it used
to be, his remuneration has been fixed at £3
per week —a sum that any willing navvy
can earn with ease. It may be said that
he can earn other money in his spare time.
But if he seriously carries out his duties of
registering and organising the senior cadets,
inspecting the training of junior cadets in
schools, supervising the training of all re-
cruits, organising the reserve units, obtain-
ing “a thorough “knowledge of the distribution
of population, conditions of employment, in-
dustries and all matters affecting the interests
of those liable for training in the area,” etc.,
etc., as directed by the regulations, he will
have but scant leisure. And if he doesn’t
approach these duties in a serious spirit, he
is worth nothing. The authors of Aus-
tralia’s defence scheme recognised the fact
that the area officer was worth something
approximating to a living wage, by laying it
down that the lads who will emerge from
Duntroon in four years’ time, shall start as
area officers at £5 per week. The present
men are worth at least as much as the boys
who will ultimately supplant them. By all
the laws of equity, since they are handling
a new, and consequently difficult, job, and
since their tenure of the said job is insecure,
they should be paid on a considerably more
generous scale than their successors.
Sweating in High Places.
According to the Melbourne correspondent
of one of the Sydney dailies :
The inability of the High Court to cope with the
long list of cases awaiting consideration, followed
by the compulsory postponement of the Melbourne
term (which was to. begin on September 1), has
caught the attention of the Prime Minister, and the
Government is considering, the question of appointing
more High Court Judges.
This paper pointed out the need of the above
move upwards of 18 months ago, and the
urgency of it has been growing steadily more
apparent ever since. Under the present sys-
tem the Judges are sweated in a way that
no Wages Board would tolerate. One result
is that the frailer members of the High
Court Bench keep breaking down in health.
Justice Barton knocked up badly the year
before last, and was in retirement for
months; O’Connor had to take a long sea
trip this year; and to those who frequent the
courts it is obvious that C.J. Griffith is
rapidly tiring. The State Judges are en-
titled to long holidays every five years, and
it may be fairly said that they earn them.
The members of the High Court have no
such privilege (though their work is consid-
erably more arduous), and if they had they
would be only able to exercise it at the cost
off immense public inconvenience and loss.
Two big industrial matters —the Engine
Drivers and Merchant Service Guild cases
-—have been held up owing to O’Connor’s
recent illness. They were argued at great
length and proportionate cost before the
absent Judge’s four colleagues. There was
a deadlock, and, as the outcome, both these
big cases have to be re-argued, at ruinous
expense to all concerned. Various urgent
matters will be delayed thereby, and mean-
while there are some 20 Land Tax appeals
to be dealt with in N. S. Wales alone; also,
the Vend appeal has to finish dragging its
portentous and costly length across the legal
landscape. It may be mentioned, incident-
ally, that this appeal only came on for hear-
ing last week, after being postponed three
times on account of C.J. Griffith’s ill-
health. On each occasion Mitchell, K.C.,
Starke and other highly-fee’d Victorian bar-
risters hurried to Sydney to no purpose, and
all round the disbursal of unnecessary money
was absolutely tragic. This demented condi-
tion of things has been going on for years,
and so long as there are but five Judges to
handle a job that is capable of keeping about
twice that number in pretty constant work, it
is bound to continue. In fact, it is going to
get w r orse in precise ratio to the number of
Judges that break down. A sort of vicious
circle is created by the position; the absent
Judge or Judges causing more work, the in-
creased work knocking out more Judges, and
so on. The original proposal of old Henry
Parkes was that there should be 10 High
Court - Judges. It was scoffed at as a wildly
extravagant idea at the time. Experience
has proved that it was not so. The fewest
Australia can well do with is seven High
Court Judges, a quota which will bring the
first Court of the Commonwealth on a num-
erical level with (say) the N. S. Wales
State Court. Failing at least two more of
these officials, the High Court of Australia
is bound shortly to find itself in the same
grotesque predicament as the American
Supreme Court, which, from a like cause, is
at the present time a matter of four years
in arrears with its work.
“Hermes” writes: —
The remarks ox Rev. F. B. Cowling and Mr.
Varney Parkes, at a meeting of the Peace Society,
or some such traitorous organisation, in Sydney, last
week, leave one- undecided whether this pair of
beauties should be sent to join Mr. Chidley, or for
an early morning walk with a firing party. The
Rev. Cowling said that, if Australia was to have a
fighting force, it would not be got by compelling
young men to join the military. Later, lie said
that, if the Japanese did come, it would be in twos
and sixes and dozens, qnd they would locate them-
selves for a time before, they made a hostile move.
Then he went on to that he had it on the
authority of Mr. Btrtl£§; ’that there were thousands
and thousands of Japanese settled in the Northern
Territory at some distance from the coast. That
remarkable son of his father, Mr. Varney Parkes,
said that parents had more to do with their boys
than train them up as soldiers. If they were going
to heve a sound democracy, let the lads be trained
to till the land, or become mechanics. The soldier’s
Die was a lazy one.
According to the remarkable parson Cowling, the
Japanese are already here, “located for a time before
they make a hostile move,” which is advanced appai-
ently as a reason why Australia should discard compul-
sory military service ! Hasn’t this parson read the Bible
and learned that the goods of the strong man armed
are safe, while the goods of the other fellow are the
perks of the spoiler? If he hasn’t, it is time he did.
The bewildered and dazed Parkes seemed to be
unaer the impression that the boys who are under-
going compulsory military service are being trained
exclusively to be soldiers, and have been compelled
to drop the learning of trades or agriculture. The
concluding remark of this gentleman, to the effect
that we wall never have the need for soldiers so long
as the boys are taught to be good citizens, reveals
such an amazing ignorance of the affairs of the
world as to suggest that, when a Japanese army
arrives, Mr. Parkes will propose telephoning for a
grocer or a churchwarden. Liberal electors who
have wives and daughters, let alone property, to
defend should really consider if it is wise to have
such a person as Parkes in Parliament in any
capacity. A man who seriously regards the training
of a boy to a trade as a safeguard against invasion is
not fit to be at large, let alone in a Legislature.
However, even this gathering of traitors had its
humorist —humorists run in all shapes. This was the
Rev; Rivett, who objected to the compulsory military
training, because the boys had to train at night in
the ..open, and occasionally heard filthy language. It
never seemed to occur to this pure-minded angel in
trousers that, if there is filthy language “in the
open,” a boy is just as liable to hear it while going
to school, or going to church. Does he want to keep
all boys shut up in kegs? And if, after all the
money the human race has paid to people like
Rivett for many centuries, all God’s splendid Out-
doors is so full of “language” as to be unfit for a
boy to inhabit, why wgste any more money on
Rivett ?
