Last week, in the o/dney District Court,
the widow of a carter sued Geo. Hudson
and Co., the big timber people, for compen-
sation for the loss of her husband. He was
accidentally killed while driving one of the
firm’s carts near Blackwattlc Swamp. The
defence was that the accident did not occur
“in, on or about” the factory of the em-
ployer, Blackwattle Swamp being six
miles away from Hudson’s head office. And
it succeeded. Now it is a common failing
for carters to travel away from their em-
ployers’ factory. In fact, they do about 27
times more work away from the place than
they do “in or about it.” A man who is
employed conveying goods through Sydney’s
congested, steep and motor-haunted tho-
roughfares is far more exposed to risk than
he who simply does a job of loading, or what
not, in the backyard of the boss’s premises.
Yet, by last week’s decision, while the latter
is legally protected, the former has no
redress whatever. This glaringly obvious
hole, in an otherwise sound Act, should be
plugged up before the Law gets a further
chance to be shown up as an illogical and
inconsequential ass, through whose well-
meaning provisions any old Gent. One can
drive a herd of dinosaurs.
“Blue Tape” writes; —
The Post Office has had many clods heaved at it
since the Commonwealth took over the letter-carrying
job ; but the time now seems to be ripe to fire
another. It concerns the question of “temporary
employees.” A temporary employee, in ordinary
business life, knows that his job is fleeting. He
regards it merely as a bough to rest his financial
feet, ere resuming his flight in search of a per-
manency whereon he may build his nest. The
Government “temporary” is a different bird alto-
gether. He sometimes dies of old age before his
casual job peters out. The general impression seems
to be that a Government “temporary” in hand is
worth two private permanencies in the offing. So the
“temporary” hangs on and lets other chances go by.
After two or three temporary year? he begins to
regard himself as a fixture. He is working as hard
as the next man, knows his job as’well as any man
in the service, and is, therefore, abld to give the
public good value for his salary. Probably, owing
to his acquired feeling of security, he enters into the
terribly permanent contract of matrimony and
acquires a permanent wife, and a permanent child
or two with permanent appetites. All is well—or
apparently so. Then out of , a clear sky falls a ton
of red tape. For some unexplained reason the
system of “temporary” hands has been judged un-
satisfactory, and is to be discontinued. A new, raw
“permanent” blunders in, is taught his business by
the experienced “temporary,” and presently sup-
plants his tutor in the job. The unfortunate “tem-
porary,” having drifted past the age’ limit during his
term of service, is ineligible to make a fresh start
up the ladder he knows, and is turned adrift with
his private permanencies on his hands, while the new
man fumbles his way and the public rages at the
counter. One, signing himself “Letter-carrier,” in-
dicated lately, in a letter to Sydney Sun, that the
Fisher Government is about to carry out some
manosuvre of this. kind. As well as being unjust, it
seems, on the face of it, to be a waste of time and
experience. If the “temporary” hands, who are
about to die, have been so painfully inefficient as to
deserve such treatment, the Fisher Government
might explain why some of these useless young men
have been allowed to linger on in the service for
from one to three years. If, on the other hand, they
have done reasonably good work, and are merely
being automatically choked out of the service with
the surplus red tape, Prime Minister Andrew
would do well to see that they are taken on the per-
manent staff. The Department has been so roundly
cursed by press and public, and has acquired such a
horde of enemies, that it really can’t afford to make
any more serious mistakes.
These are the days when the more rampant
variety of Australian child-warrior “takes a
lunar” at his commander-in-chief with im-
punity, and his commander-in-chief doesn’t
know how to out an end to the impunity.
The curse of shiftlessness and half-hearted-
ness is coming home to roost. It was re-
solved, in the first place, to train the humble
and unfinished and unserviceable “kid” in-
stead of the citizen of 21 and upwards, be-
cause the former hadn’t a vote, and so
couldn’t throw out the Government if he
didn’t like the arrangement. That looked
like taking the line of least resistance, but
the line proved, as it usually does, to be
greased and slippery. If a brawny, full-
grown male recruit was absolutely unman-
ageable, an upstanding area officer might
meet him in a quiet place, and bid him “put
them up,” and after six rounds there might
be a better understanding. He can’t tell
somebody about as high as his knee to “put
them nn,” no matter how furious he mav
feel. The recruit of 20 or 21 might be fined.
and have the amount garnisheed out of his
wages; but the first-year cadet has no wages.
He can’t be sent to gaol for shirking ; there
are limits to the possibility of herding an
infant with criminals. He can hardly even
be punished by confinement in a place of
military detention, lest he should howl too
bitterly for his mother and his feeding bottle.
Out of an easy-going desire to avoid trouble,
the whole compulsory military training sys-
tem is in difficulties. Even a robust and
off-hand officer, who would cheerfully take
on a dispute with three mutinous coal-heav-
ing recruits, feels himself helpless as things
stand. When an infant insurrectionist tells
him to go and hit somebody his own size that
practically ends the discussion.
“Plain Snaffle” writes on a Defence
matter: —
There is something utterly rotten in Australia’s
arrangements for supplying itself with remounts for
war purposes. For reasons over which the military
authorities have no control, the class of steed avail-
able is steadily deteriorating. And to this is added
the further catastrophe that such decent horses as
do offer are liable, apparently, to be ruined by bad
horse-mastership. A Board of Inquiry investigated
this latter scandal in N. S. Wales last week, and
whatever its findings are, the main fact will stand
that of late some hundreds of remounts have been
rendered useless during the humorously miscalled
“breaking-in” operations for any service other than
that of figuring as stars in a peripatetic buckjumping
show. The reason becomes glaringly obvious when
the methods which were employed with the wretched
animals are taken into consideration. A thousand of
them were yarded at Chipping North, near Liverpool,
the other day. They were not carefully “handled”
and mouthed, made to understand that there was
nothing horrifying or agonising about their new
vocation in life, and, in general, treated as the mere
coper treats his equine goods. Instead, a small
band of breakers was let loose on them. They were
energetically “roughed,” one by one—which is to say
they were ridden to a standstill, by experts at such
feats. They were turned adrift to await their period
of service. What wonder that scores of the animals,
which, with reasonable treatment, would have be-
come quiet, handy, mounts, were turned into out-
laws? The men at Victoria Barracks wondered
blankly what had smitten them when the allegedly
tamed remounts arrived there. Experienced horse-
men were thrown right and left. Three men had to
be taken to the hospital. A team bolted in the bar-
rack square and did a deal of damage. When the
horses went into camp the same performance was re-
peated on an extended scale. None but skilled buck-
jump riders were able to stay on a number of them.
A kind of panic set in. One of the officers com-
manding a Light Horse Regiment refused to accept
any of the half-demented beasts for his gun-section.
The daily papers chronicled the occurrence, and the
above-mentioned inquiry is one outcome of the reve-
lations that were made. Careless or unintelligent
handling of valuable animals, that have been bought
with the public’s money, is an offence that needs
to be scotched with firmness. The thousand horses
that were mishandled at Chipping North cost, on
an average, probably £2O each. As a result of
being “broken-in,” a goodly proportion of them
would be now unsaleable at £8 per head. It is
rotten economy, and the position is rendered all the
more tragic by reason of the fact that the Common-
wealth has no serviceable remounts to spare.
-<>
On a recent Sunday English Noncom-
formity shouted with gladness over its 250th
birthday. Strictly speaking, its origin was
much farther back than the beginning of the
Act of Uniformity on Black Bartholo-
mew’s Day, but if it likes to look younger
than it is, that is its own affair. Whether
there is much to whoop about since 1662 is
another matter. The Nonconformist think-
ing machinery has had a tremendous influ-
ence in politics, and that influence has
generally been exercised on the side of some-
thing which looked like reform. It has also
made enormous progress in the numerical
sense, and there are now rather more people
in the various dissenting churches of Eng-
land than there are in the established places
of prayer and psalm singing. Also, there
are more people in neither than in both.
The churches of all brands in London
wouldn’t nearly hold its population, even if
the people were packed in layers like 1 sar-
dines instead of being planted in rows in the
pews. Nonconformity stands for the survi-
val of the individualistic idea in religion,
and in the days when individualism was the
moving force in Europe it did big things.
To-day it is clean up against the spirit of
the times, and everywhere faint mutterings
of re-union and closer association among
the various religious bodies are being heard.
It is just as well to celebrate any old event
in its history while there is enough enthusi-
asm to go round, for it doesn’t look as if
there was likely to be very much more his-
tory left after the reorganisation schemes
have got fairly started. Also, all sorts of
commemorations are associated with a
special collection-plate, and collection-plates
fill a very important part in what is left of
Nonconformist history.

Scandalised cry from “Ex-Public Ser-
vant” :
Do I sleep, do I dream, or is all the windy talk
of the Right-thinking Person about the Public Ser-
vice being free from political control a delusion
and a mendacity. I ask, because I chanced to stray
into the N. S. Wales Assembly on the 27th ult., what
time J. Macartiiuk Onslow, 'of Waverley, was bleat-
ing angrily about one Pike. Onslow wanted a
Select’ Committee to consider the case of Pike. It
presently appeared that the protegg of Onslow was
a coal-heaver in the pilot service, and that he pos-
sessed a grievance dated 1900. What precise wrong
it was that had been inflicted on Onslow's coal-
heaving pal I never quite gathered, and don’t know
now. Anyhow, it couldn’t have been anything par-
ticularly terrible, nor could the Department have
been inspired with excessive venom against Pike,
since he is still employed in the Public Service, being
the persuader of a Government launch on Sydney
Harbor. After the business of the country had been
held up for the best part of a sitting in connection
with his mysterious grievance or wrong., most of the
Liberals present trotted off to vote for the com-
mittee, while the Laborites, aided by Twaddell, an
ex-Treasurer, who had struggled in his time with
the Pike trouble, voted the other way. And so
Pike lost by a pair of votes. Now, suppose Pike
had got his committee, and a friendly crowd of
politicians had wept tears over his 12-year-old griev-
ance, and had spoken rudely to the P. S. Board.
Suppose, furthermore, that Parliament had upheld
the committee, and had the Board. What
then? It is said that there are 1000 appeals now
pending against the Board, and the whole 1000 ap-
pellants would be able to claim an equal right to
trundle their little hoops before the Legislature. By
their attitude in the case of Pike, the WADE-ites
have tacitly admitted the right of a public officer
with a political pull to butt into the middle of a
busy session. With a general election imminent, they
directly invite every employee in the Public Service
to manipulate any political card at his disposal. The
fact that the Premier upheld the P. S. Board and kept
the Waverley volunte°r warrior at bay, does not
affect the point, which is that the Pike case should
never have been so much as seriously discussed. As
things turned out, it was a pity that the motion was
lost. The granting of the committee would have
forced the hand of the Board. It would have been
driven in that case either to abandon every shred of
its authority over the Service or to fight. It must
n-irp rintin t' e latter wi f h the result that such a
scrap would have broken loose as would have made
the flinging of a newspaper out of the Assembly
appear a mere casual trifle.
The Defence.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
While sternly opposing all forms of sweating in
Australian industries, the Federal Government con-
tinues to admit sweated goods through the Customs
in increasing quantities.-— Lamentable fact.
Not guilty, yer Honers! I talks to yer
straight!
An’ I calls it a pretty crook game—
An’ an ’og of a thing, if the coppers should
bring
Such dishonor as this on me name —
On me name that ’as stood fer up’oldin’ the
good !
It’s enough to make any bloke weep !
An’ the goods as they says I received—spare
me days!
Don’t I tell yer I bought ’em dirt cheap!
Yes, I bought ’em dirt cheap; an’ I says to
the Bench,
As a man ’oo acts honest an’ square,
That me solid defence is poun’s, shillin’s an’
pence,
An’ I arsts yer to deal with me fair.
Fer it’s more than a joke when a square-
livin’ bloke
Is ’ad up fer committin’ a crime;
Fer the John, ’ere, ’e swears to the goods,
an’ declares
As I knoo they was pinched at the time.
But, gentlemen, listen, I bought ’em dirt
cheap!
As the bloke ’ere, who sold ’em kin swear.
But they say ’e was crook, an’ the goods
that ’e took
Wasn’t got, so to speak, on the square.
Well, per’aps that’s all true, an’ they ses
that I knoo
They wus pinched when I made ’im the
bid.
It ’ud be a fair sin fer to miss that chuck in;
So I arsts: Wot’s the odds if I did?
’Ere’s the Cop ses I’m charged with receivin’
the goods,
As I bloomin’ well knoo to be stole;
But then that isn’t stealin’ meself, an’ I
feel
As I ortn’t to be in this ’ole.
Fer I never stole things in me life, an’ I
brings
Twenty witnesses ’ere fer to say
That in all er me days I ’ave alius earned
praise
Fer up’oldin’ the straight, honest way.
Yus, I alius ’ave preached that to steal wus
a sin,
An’ I never pinched things in me life;
An’ I brings up me kids as strict honesty
bids,
An’ I gotter respectable wife.
I am known in our street as a man ’ard to
beat
At condemnin’ all thievin’ an’ crime;
An’ there’s clergymen ’ere as is ready to
clear
Up me character every time.
So, yer Honers, I arsts to be cleared o’ the
charge;
An’ I think yer should say, at the time,
Me respectable name is quite free frum all
blame
In connection with thievin’ and crime.
Fer me great repitation’s at stake, in a way;
It’s a thing as I’m anxious ter keep;
Fer I never been once in the — Wot’s that?
Six munce !
But, yer Honers, I got them things cheap!
VICTORIA.

WHERE
EVERYBODY
GOES
The Allegedly Wonderful Cost of
Living.
The Commission which was appointed _in
Maoriland to consider the allegedly amazing
cost of living has investigated long and
earnestly, and has presented its report. The
document covers many points, and goes into
considerable detail. It is reckoned that
within the last 16 years (since “the middle
of the later ’9o’s”) the price of food, cloth-
ing, lighting, fuel and rent have all risen.
The total cost of existence is said to have
gone up 16 per cent. At the same time, the
average income has gone up so much faster
that the dear food of to-day absorbs less of
the toiler’s wages than did the cheaper food
of 17 years ago. So greatly, it is alleged,
has the condition of the public improved
since the low-tariff times of the ’Bo’s of last
century that the average individual has in-
creased his consumption of commodities by
100 per cent. As he couldn’t hold twice as
much victuals in bulk, the inference is that
he holds twice as much in quality—that he
has got out of the dry-bread stage and into
the caviare stage. The increase of prices
was, of course, unavoidable; if a number of
men used to get 3d. for making an article
worth 4d.. it is plain that they can’t get
sd. and the article be still worth only 4d.
But the great point is that while prices have
gone up in Maoriland. wages have gone up
faster, unlike England—the country where
dog lately became an article of diet —where
the rise of prices was not accompanied by
anv corresponding movement in wages. Rather
did Bull’s wages tend downwards in cer-
tain years. And the rise of food prices in
Maoriland, at its worst, wasn’t so steep as
that in England. Thus the British Board
of Trade recently reported that, when the
price of a fixed quantity of goods—cwt. or
stone or gross or whatever weight or measure
is commonly used in selling the article —for
1896 was compared with that of 1910 the
rise was as follows: —
1896. 19] 0. Increase.
Bread .. .. £4 13 0 £5 14 0 \£l 1 0
Flour .. .. 480 60 0 1 12 0
Beef . . . . 4 12 0 5 13 0 1 1 0
Mutton .. 490 540 0 15 0
Pork .. .. 4140 550 0110

malade .. 500 5 9 0 090

Oatmeal . . 5 0 0 5 13 0 0 13 0
That is what Bull, the Freetrader, has had
to put up with, with no upward tendency of
wages to counterbalance the trouble. There-
fore the Maorilander should consider that he
is on a pretty good wicket so far, and not
grumble overmuch. During the 10 years to
1910 his Post Office Savings Bank deposits
rose from £2O to £25 per inhabitant, which
isn’t too bad, and showed that he had a
steadily increasing balance of receipts over
expenditure. At all events, it is better than
eating the roast dog of Old England.
One James Gilmour told what the S. M.
Herald of last Monday called a li—beg
pardon—a story illustrating the late General
Booth’s “indomitable courage.” The
General, it appeared, was once sneaking to
an Australian crowd of Darkest England.
Inspired by “white , Australian” feelings, the
audience did not want any of the criminal classes
transplanted out here. The General was keenly in-
tuitive, and felt exactly what was in the minds of his
audience. As he spoke, he described a shinload of
happy families coming to Australia. Suddenly the
ship sprang a leak. A passing ocean liner, appealed
to for help, could not assist because the people must
not be brought to Australia. The General turned on
the crowd, and with his frame quivering with oas-
sion, said : “There is a judgment day, and we shall
tneet again.” “I look upon those words as pro-
phetic,” said Mr. Gilmour.
It was apparently intended by Mr. Gilmour
or the Herald or somebody for a warning,
or a Stinking Fish, or something, but it
strikes this paper as the most futile thing
that couid well be nut in print. The ship-
load of happy families was “coming to Aus-
tralia.” The ship struck a leak—scuttled,
possibly, by an anti-immigrationist. who
afterwards’ fled in a dinghy, with a jug of
water and a tin of ox-tongue. Then the pass-
ing liner refused to help because these people,
who were “coming to Australia” in any
case, “must not be brought to Australia.”
Why they must not be brought when they
were being brought isn’t mentioned, and
their after tote is wrapped in the gloom of
allegory. Then the General turned to the
crowd (what crowd?! and said, “his frame
quivering with passion” (though Heaven
knows what he quivered about), that he and
the crowd would “meet again.” That sort of
event happens often. When the average
man parts from the waitress at his restaur-
ant he often says—quivering perhaps with
excess of soup, or by reason of an overcharge
of 3d. —-that they will meet again, and tliev
generally do- —next day. What is the trash
all about, and where does the General’s “in-
domitable courage” come in? Also, what
was the name of the shin, and the liner, and
the scuttler (if there was such a person)
who got away with the tog of water and the
tin of ox-tomrue, and where did it happen,
and when? It seems like a pork dream.

“Nightbirds” is back in Sydney—this time
at Her Majesty’s. About the most popular
turns are the singing by W. Talleur Andrews
of the “Blue Danube” number, and the
dancing of the “bunny hug” by Edward
Sherras and Miss Arundale. Incidentally,
Jack Cannot knocks a quantity of ripe,
fruity humor out of the role of Count Max
Clicquot, and Miss Young sings nicely as
his countess, and W. S. Percy and Victor
Prince tear down mirth and applause
from the gallery like famished jesters, and
Miss Dorothy Brunton dances and carols
divinely and looks the last thing in allure-
ment, as Minna. If the writer has omitted
to give three cheers for any member of the
cast —the head scene-shifter or the chief
limelight expert, or anyone like that—he
hereby abases himself and throws moun-
tains on his head. All are admirable, and
the cheery show goes from start to finish
with the swing and vim and rush of some
melodious motor-car bolting down a steep
hill.
“GET- RICH-QUICK WALLINGFORD, ’
AT SYDNEY CRITERION.
Wallingford (fired N'tblo) and Daw (Corson
Clarke) getting the “ Boobs" exceted.
Man’s insatiable longing for change is
pandered to at the National Amphitheatre
this week by the Wynfords, amongst others.
Those persons leap over chairs in a spry and
careless fashion, so that everyone is pleased.
Ultimately, one of them leaps over 10 chairs,
and then the delight of the onlookers ap-
proximates to delirium. There has been
nothing like it since the cow jumped over
the moon in the children’s rhyme, to the
horror of the sky populace, who saw a large
animal approaching at a fearsome pace. The
Olracs are still there, likewise Grace Comp-
ton and Mabel and Molfe.
“The Girl Without a Home” will again
be turned adrift to-morrow night, after hav-
ing put in a fairly busy three weeks at the
Adelphi. On Saturday night, what is de-
scribed in the “London Press” (what part
of it, or how much of it, doesn’t, of course,
matter) as “a play which presents all possi-
ble sensations in spasms, and goes through
the fullest gamut of various emotions, etc.,”
besides being a “whirlwind drama of sur-
prises,” and several minor items, will lob
into Sydney. But it will only remain for 12
nights, and will be the last appearance of
the Paget-Buckler combination. The new-
comer answers to the homely name of “Her
Forbidden Marriage.”
“Get-Rich-Slick Wallingford,” at the Cri-
terion. seems about as permanent as the
eternal hills, the rise in prices, Speaker
Willis, the Scotchbyterian yearning to wear
kilts, and the policy of dragging everything
to Sydney. In sheer weariness of influenza
this scribe states once more that there is-
no sign yet of the last 742 nights.
No newer artist having come to the Tivoli,
Minnie Kaufmann is still cycling in the
forefront of the bill, and keeping up the
delightful illusion that it is 1596 again.
H. G. Wells’s bicycling story, “The Wheels
of Chance,” is a literary Heat in store. The
balancing Schenk Brothers are supporting
themselves by supporting each other, the
Stavordale Quintette keep twanging, and
Rich and Rich continue to be agreeably
idiotic, in clothes that would get them
rushed away to an asylum if they fell out
of the theatre. Chinko’s juggling, the
Sprightly Sisters’ burlesques, and Jessie
Stuart’s songs are the other desirable pro-
longations of the second part.
“The Girl from Wayback,” at present
residing at the Palace, does not live up to
her large poster—the one which shows a
partly-wrecked mail coach, with the severed
head of the funny man sticking to the box
seat, and a Past Grand Sundowner (or per-
haps it is an Arch Rabbit) trying to discon-
ceal himself behind a totally-inadequate log,
while the villain does something with a wire
in the middle of the road, and two faces at
the window of the broken-down vehicle emit
silent howls. (The writer is describing from
a fleeting tram glimpse, so it is possible that
some of the fine details may have escaped
him. The above, however, is a faithful
record of the impression he carried away.)
Of course, no play could live up to a splendid
poster like that and remain in Australia.
London, Paris and New York would clamor
for it by cable, and it would only get back
here in its old age, when all the great capi-
tals had discovered a new joke. “The Girl”
isn’t really that sort of production. It is a
more or less serious Australiah drama, which
carefully avoids the permanent and reproduc-
tive bushranger, and substitutes a persistent
sort of villain, who is more or less mixed up
with wire all through the piece; and the
Arch Rabbit or Past Grand Sundowner, who
knows enough about telegraphy to foil him
in his efforts to effect the ruin of a bright
young postal official who was choked out of
the muddled service by an excess of red tape.
The lamentable stupidity of the handsome
Girl, who can’t tell the villain from the
hero, keeps the play going on and the bad
man cursing till a suitably late hour. A
brighter heroine would have cut the play
short; this one keeps it tottering along until
the usual horse hurries home in front of the
other animals, thus getting the hero out of
debt in a thoroughly immoral manner. “A
serviceable melodrama” is the curious de-
scription applied to the play by the morning
paper which was not thrown out of the
N.S.W. Assembly by Speaker Willis. And
yet it does not in the least resemble a pair
of strong boots. Madge Plope is the heroine
who doesn’t know the hero when she sees
him; Herbert Linden shouts with great
abandon as the villain : and Harry Beaumont
presents a careful study of an Arch Rabbit.
Other characters escape calumny here by
being of no importance.
Although “At the Last Hour” was repre-
sented as the star film at Spencer’s this
week, “The Apple Industry in N.S.W.” (a
glimpse of the apple orchards in the Bath-
urst district) was of much more local in-
terest. The whole of the proceedings, from
picking the fruit to loading ; the ships, was
shown. The apples are collected and thrown
into cases, which are made dn* the premises.
The fruit is then graded, wrapped and
packed. At both the box-making and the
wrapping the men become wonderfully ex-
pert—the average wrapper doing over 100
apples a minute, and the case-maker knock-
ing together about 50 cases an hour. The
cases are then trucked to Sydney (most
things are), and shipped from there (most
things are, except the things that are shipped
to there).
Oscar Aselie abandons Sydney Royal after
to-morrow (Friday) night, and on Saturday
“Passers-By” is to arrive. The new play is
by C. Haddon Chambers, one time of Aus-
tralia, and now of London, and it has been
a howling, not to say shrieking, success in
New York. Miss Hilda Spong and Harcourt
Beatty are in the cast.

The chief item in a quiet Adelaide week
has been the Price Conigrave lectures. The
public has hardly thought so, but it re-
sponded a little better when the talks on
native customs were announced “for men
only.” (Mark Twain says something caustic
on this ground-bait for an audience.) Young
and bearded, Conigrave looks the ideal ex-
plorer, and fie has some fresh things to say
about the comparatively unknown north-
west of this, continent. Incidentally, he men-
tioned that it is no place for the small capi-
talist, that the big firms “carry the small
men on their backs,” and that lack of com-
bination has made cattle-raisers take £3 2s.
a head at Wyndham for beasts that were
resold at Fremantle within the fortnight for
£23 155.! The Asiatics, he thinks, will
" Oh, the Fashions! Which do yon like the
best? ” The Sprightly Sisters at the Tivoli.
come in not by way of a battle-fleet, but
even more irresistibly by steady, stealthy
immigration. The Bert Bailey co. has
moved on from the Royal to Broken Hill,
making way for William Anderson’s revival
of the “Prince and Beggar-Maid” romance.
Miss Eugenie Duggan is now the barefooted
princess, and Cyril Mackay the favored
prince, but Rutland Beckett, as the princely
villain, and Max Clifton, as the princely
hunchback, have about equal chances. The
whole atmosphere is intensely regal. The
variety shows are strong on the musical side.
The Tivoli has the grand tenor Marini, and
the King’s respond with the Jourdane quar-
tet and Romanoff’s inspired fiddle. At the
former, Fred Russell exchanges ventriloquial
insults with his Coster lad —a fine piece of
mechanism ; and Hera juggles marvellously.
At the latter is Edna Davies, a distinct
novelty as a lady jiu-jitsu exponent. Other
shows have varied from a University Con-
servatorium concert to a widespread holding
of “Wattle Day” on August 31 as a national
festival.

$ 5* x
At Melbourne Royal, which looks larger
for having turned pale in the presence of the
redecorator, “Ben Hur” is revealing itself
in six acts, 12 scenes and a prelude. In its
first and final scenes the picturesque drama
draws upon the New Testament for support.
The prelude is a misty depiction of three
wise men from the East kneeling before the
Star of Bethlehem, what time that guiding
emblem threatens to burst into fireworks
and emit a splutter of colored sparks. The
final tableaux represent a gathering of the
“very earliest Christians” on the Mount of
Olives and in the Valley of II inn on, where
Ben Ilur’s mother and sister are cleansed of
their leprosy by means too miraculous to be
lightly mentioned. But it seems permissible
to state that the assemblage of hymn-singing
ladies remind one of the recent decease of
General Booth, albeit they wear no bonnets
or tambourines, and are unaccompanied by a
brass band. If “Ben Hur” appealed only
to the Christian Faith of its audiences, the
response would not be hearty. The super-
natural incidents are unimpressive. Apart
from these, the show has worldly attractions
which put it in touch with the “Sign of the
Cross” on the one hand, and with Drury
Lane drama on the other.
* * * *
The arrest of Judah Ben Hur, on the false
charge of dropping a piece of his palace
parapet on the head of an unholy Roman in
the Jerusalem street below, and the sending
of that noble Semitic youth to work as a
galley-slave, whilst his mother and sister are
immured in a leprous dungeon, give the story
a forceful send-off. Hur, holding his bene-
factor, the Tribune, on a small raft on the
open sea is a fine stage picture. • When Hur
begins his new life as a Jew-Roman citizen,
the plot takes a sporting, Druriolanic turn.
A burly country squire, or Arab sheik, enters
his team of steeds for a chariot race, and
engages Hur to drive them. Properly
piloted they are bound to beat the favorites,
belonging to Messala ; the shiny young
Roman villain of the piece, who incidentally
is competing with the hero for the smile of
an Egyptian siren. Hur drives the Sheik’s
dark horses in a successful trial, with the
owner looking on and timing him, and then
the stable money is invested at five to one,

G. HOWARD POPE, Manag-er.
not in quids, but in talents. A Jew com-
missioner is employed to get the money on,
and he artfully tempts the offer of those
rash Roman odds before stating the amount
of his wager, or giving the name of his prin-
cipal. Subsequently, be offers to back Ben
once more for a level fiver (five talents),
whereupon the Romans rush him and the
lights go out. The Sheik’s pair of small
gees win the chariot race, in just the same
way as The Whip did the trick at Her
Majesty’s two years ago, and when they re-
turn to scale the pair of little ones have
expanded into four big ’uns, which is really
the best miracle of the evening. Messala,
the villain, takes the financial “knock.”
Then the villainous Iras, who had flirted
shockingly with Ben in a barge, before he
cooled off, conspires with Messala to recover
his losses, but the invalid father of the other
girl (a good little Jewess) comes along in a
bath ch'air of the period, and crushes the
conspiracy by producing a paper. And so
on, until the lepers are cleansed and Ben
falls into the arms of the faithful little
Jewess, who had loved him all along. After
marriage she will doubtless remind him often
of his _ misbehavior in the barge, and make
him wish the ocean had devoured him in the
second act.
Most of the histrionic strength is on the
male side of the co. Eric Maxon is only a little
too loud to be a quite admirable hero.
Eardley Turner is good as the honest steward
of the Hur property. Leonard Willey—the
villain-—supplies rough, noisy reminiscenses
of Julius Knight; and Winter-Hall, Atliol-
wood and Leslie Victor do full justice to
their small parts, whereas Styan’s sheik is
more like a large modern landowner in fancy
dress that an Arab potentate of a.d. 30 or
thereabouts. Among the ladies, Miss Bur-
roughs looks the showy part of the Egyptian
siren, and speaks it seductively, but is not
strong enough to realise its emotional possi-
bilities to the full. Which criticism applies
to Miss Dix, in the part of the dear little
Jewess. Miss Susie Vaughan gives a pleas-
ing performance of the unfortunate, fond
mother who gets lost and returns to notice
as a leper: Miss Phillips is a nice, unas-
suming sister of Ben, but Miss Alma
Vaughan, as the old nurse, is a solemn
failure. She doesn’t even suggest an aged
female in distress. A much more pathetic
item is the orchestra, which sits engloomed
between the acts, and appears to feel the
doleful music very keenly.

If “Dangerous Women,” the passing show
at the King’s, were as reasonable in the
plot as it is in two or three of its incidents ;
the acting of Harrie Ireland and Maud
D’Alton, in the name parts ; of Miss Remee,
as the heroine; and Maisie Posner, in the
pants of precocious boyhood, would entitle
this great mystic drama to kindly toleration.
Unfortunately, the plot hinges upon an act
of imbecility due to Robert Inman’s ghostly
adviser, and is infested with the madness
of a lunatic asylum keeper, who invents a
resurrection tonic which he injects into the
mouldering dead, and awaits results. When
the live heroine is deposited in a family
vault, the doctor greets her as a triumphant
proof of his tonic’s efficacy, and proposes
to murder her with a view to resurrecting
her some more. In addition to its lurid
defects, the drama suffers from the worst
form of comic relief. An inglorious career
of 10 nights will terminate on Saturday.
Five nights next week the Arion Operatic
Society will struggle with Edward German’s
romantic opera, “Merrie England,” for the
first time in Australia. And then Bert
Bailey and eo. come along to put Dad and
the family on their Selection.

The baby-choking drama at the Princess’s
had a cold time last week, but the crowd of
unsuspecting agricowturalists from off the
soil has rushed in where the locals forgot
to tread ; and “Brought to Ruin” will carry
on till further orders.
** * *
The bill at the Opera House is as before,
Hurley being the latest arrival, whilst Nella
Webb, the “diseuse,” is on the verge of de-
parture. If Hurley were a member of the
other sex in a bright hobble frock, he also
might rank as a “diseuse,” for his songs
have a conversational tendency, and his
smile is ingratiating. Owing to the accident
of birth, he became a Coster impersonator,
and constructed a pick-axe brigade on the
lines of Bluett’s boy scouts.
* * * *
Although anxious to speak kindly of its
old friends, “The World, the Flesh and the
Devil” (there used, by the way. to be a
rather fine historical play called “The Flesh
and the Devil,” in which Harry Diver was
the Devil), this paper remarks sadly that
things might be a lot better, and they would
have to hustle considerably to be very much
worse. The drama with which W. Cosgrove
appeals'to Melbourne at the Bijou might
perhaps be more appropriately called, “The
Tart Who Loved a Journalist.” Naomi
Rutherford is the maid thus afflicted, and she
expresses the opinion that journalism is “a
noble profession,” and waits for the applause
which, on the first night, failed to eventuate.
Naomi’s ma, a Smart Setter who looks dowdy
enough to be the wife of a curate, has other
plans for her daughter, having selected a
beefy stockbroker, hight Roger Swindon, to
be the architect of the girl’s misfortune. The
inkslinger of her heart is bluffed, by fat
Roger into purchasing Goldfinch scrip, which
is as worthless as discarded tram tickets;
but the Providence which watches over
drunkards and congenital idiots takes a hand,
and in the last act a Goldfinch boom sets in,
and the journalist is able to lay aside the
pastepot in favor of the fleshpot. Just
before the boom, Naomi flees from the meaty
speculator; and when he pursues her, he is
met by the pressman wearing a lamentable
dress suit and a stern demeanor. His worst-
laid schemes having come, to nought, the
villain retires beaten, and Is hurried out of
this vale of bulls and bears by the bullet of
a Socialist whose daughter he has betrayed.
As the bull of shares and the bear of the
domestic hearth, William Cosgrove is en-
titled to a certain amount of respect. His
style is not wholly unlike that of Arthur
Styan, performing at the theatre opposite,
in “Ben Ilur.” Norman Campbell repre-
sents a journalist who, had he not adopted
literature as a means of hunger, might have
become the pride of the most expensive hair-
dressery in Perth, where the stirring events
transpire. Harry McDona is moderately
amusing as a medico suffering from an im-
pediment in his elocution ; and Miss Ethel
Chadwick, as the heroine, is at least worthy
of the gentleman who leads her altar-wards.
Natural benevolence must be accepted as
this paper’s excuse for not mentioning the
other characters in detail. The scenery of
the production has topographical value.
“Hay-street, Perth,” as a case in point, seems
to be a wilderness of trees and rocks,
threaded by a river of promiscuous habits,
and with chasms occurring at intervals.

The Majestic opened portals on which the
paint was hardly dry in Flinders-street, Mel-
bourne, on Saturday, and an audience that
was both well dressed and highly nourished
sought to blot out the seating accommoda-
tion from the casual view. The manage-
ment lavishly supplies pictures, orchestra,
well-upholstered chairs, the use of cloak-
rooms and vestibules, and the ingratiating
smiles of the humblest ushers on earth, all
for prices ranging from one-tenth of a pound
to half a shilling. The opening programme,
which included two or three fine Kinema-
color subjects, was above the average; and
if the management can maintain its bills at
the same level the theatre’s future will be
nothing to grumble at.
When Spencer (Melbourne) stages a pic-
ture like “Saved from Siberia” he calls for
the loud compliment which expounds the
vacant want that he has filled. The adapter
has curiously plagiarised Max Pemberton’s
“Kronstadt” and improved on the yarn.
“Dr. La Fleur’s Theory” is an animated
subject recalling the proceedings in “Leah
Kleschna.” A film of some influence on
patriotic sentiment depicts the activity of
the naval and military branches of the Bull-
dog Breed, also a number of the late General
Booth’s military manoeuvres figure tiresomely
on the screen.
* :H * *
A fleet pair of hands, a mane of pale, fair
hair, and the sweet voice of a shop assistant
pushing a job line in socks, are the principal
assets of Hugard, who began a season at
Melbourne Athenaeum on Saturday last. The
visitor is in the same line of business as
Jansen and Nicola, and he shows a sort of
professional kinship with Chung Ling
Soo, the Scotch Mongol or Donald
Chinaman of other days. But he
goes one better than those in a notable
particular. Like the Duncan Chow already
mentioned, Hugard mysteriously produces
hot drinks and flowers and distributes them
freely among the populace, but on Saturday
he also turned an apparently empty cabinet
into a “penny dip,” and drew forth sweets,
cigarettes and cigars, which he flung to the
mob. This unexpected largesse filled the
great heart of the multitude with joy. Yet,
as a matter of fact, much of Hugard’s outfit
has the moss of time upon it. Here and
there, however, a pinnacle of bright excep-
tion appears above the wash of common-
place. For instance, Hugard allows you to
shoot him freely, and yet he isn’t dead.
Many other people are not dead, but then
they don’t urge the populace to fire cannon
at them.

“Norbell”: The revival of “Message from Mars”
with George Titheradge as the Tramp, reminds me of
how well William F. Hawtrey played that beery
wreck 12 years ago. Ilawtrey, of course, had many
critics, acting being one of the things everybody
kno\ is all about. Leake, the then Premier of Wes-
tralia, was in Ilawtrey’s dressing-room in Perth, be-
tween the acts of “Mars,” and was pointing out how
the Tramp should be played. He added, by way of
apology, “You know, Hawtrey, I was rather a good
actor myself some years ago, though you mightn’t
think it.” Hawtrey replied: “Oh, very likely. You
may be a very good actor although a very indifferent
Premier.”

EXIT WILLIAM
MURDOCH,
PIANIST.
Dear Bulletin, —You published a par. in May last
from a correspondent, who pointed out the simi-
larity, as regards plot, details, etc., of Galsworthy’s
“The Pigeon” and ' “Passers-by,” the play by my
brother, Haddon Chambers, which the J.O.W. man-
agement is staging- next Saturday night in
Sydney. According to this writer, the resem-
blance between the two plays was so close that
“it looked like plagiarism one way or the other.”
I wrote to Haddon on the subject, and got this reply:
“Galsworthy’s play, ‘The Pigeon,’ was produced in
London some months after the production of ‘Passers-
by.’ Some of the critics and many playgoers com-
mented on the similarity of Galsworthy’s work to
my own, but there the matter ended. I was out of
England and never saw ‘The Pigeon.’ The last-
named play found its way into publication in book-
form almost immediately, probably for the reason
that in the theatre it was not a success. The publi-
cation of ‘Passers-by’ has been postponed until such
time as the play’s popularity in America shall have
waned. Even to such reticences is one put in the
endeavor to frustrate the knavish tricks of one’s
friends, the transatlantic theatrical pirates.”
The point which I wish to establish (and I think
the facts disclosed in the above do establish it), is
that if there has been any plagiarism the blame
doesn’t lie with the Australian playwright.—Yours,
Lila Rooney.
“S.T.S.”: Presumably it is out of delicacy that
“Spasmode,” in his list of stage lovers who really
quarrelled (B. 22/8/’l2), passes Knight with a bare
reference: “What a lot of fair and unfair enemies
Julius Superbus had.” Yet he should have made a
standard instance, and one of much more interest to
Australian readers than Salvini with Ristori, or
Phelps with Helen Faueit. The first Mercia Australia
saw was Ada Ferrar, and probably no two stars ever
were on worse terms than those two all the time he
was playing the passion-struck Marcus. Knight,
being a gentleman, said little or nothing about this
private animosity. She, not being one, talked freely
to non-theatrical friends about the painfulness of
having to act with such a person.

“Stargazer": La Freya at Melbourne Opera House
is a development of the “La Milo” idea of our
friend “Cruickshank” Lang. Pansy Montague Milo
went on as undraped rock. La Freya appears as
a striped seraph, who presently has pictures and
colored dry goods sprayed over her by the man who
squirts the limelights. As a human sample in glove
tights, the lady is worthy of respectful admiration ;
as the centrepiece of the alleged “original, beautiful,
artistic visions,” she is something in the nature of a
lost chord. The pictures are crude, and the magic-
lantern garments that obscure La Freya’s native beauty
are never of sufficient interest to justify their being
considered as anything but an unmannerly intrusion.
The show holds only because of the public’s con-
sciousness of the fact that a lovely female in pearly
skin-tight fleshings is hidden away somewhere under
the motley mat of paint.
“Harrison O.”: Writing in Melbourne Akgus, G. S.
Titheradge deplores the attempts that are being made
to establish an Actors’ Union, and urges members of
the profesh to join, instead, the Actors’ Association,
which has been started as a counterblast. The latter
is not an industrial organisation, but it possesses a
place where mummers may “meet socially as in a
club, write their letters and read the papers.”'
Also, the Association hopes to be able to establish
an actors’ benevolent fund and a general theatrical
fund, from which members who pay a certain
weekly sum will be entitled to draw sick pay, and,
perhaps, a pension. Says Tith.: “This is the nearest
approach to an actors’ union possible under the
peculiar conditions attached to the theatrical call-
ing, and the council sincerely hopes that every actor
and actress interested in the welfare of their pro-
fession will recognise the advantages of member-
ship and join its ranks." If this is the best it can
offer, the association has a dashed cheek when it
urges its less bloated brethren to join. These latter
have asked for bread and have been offered a stone. In
place of the substance of a decent wage to-day, they
are offered the shadow of a pension in a more or less
remote future. They desire equitable working con-
ditions, and are promised a club, where (provided,
of course, they should happen to be performing in the
metropolitan area) they may meet and read the
papers.
“0.K.”: The Musicians’ Union of Sydney, which
recently passed a resolution, “that all persons receiv-
ing remuneration for their musical services shall be
invited to join the union,” has logic on its side. All
musical instrumentation is obviously a handicraft.
And what is the average pianist but a mechanic, and
a rotten bad one at that? Even the flautist and
cornetist, producing their musical noises with the
mouth, cannot achieve any melody to speak of, save
by manual labor. In fact, every musician is just as
much a mechanic as any other sprt of 'skilled
worker associated with trades unionism, and!
the haughty person who, talks ironically
of “Trades Hall mandates,” “union label,” etc.,
in connection with “the long haired, dreamy, un-
practical enthusiast, the devout lover of his art and
the scorner of base lucre,” is talking old bosh. As
regards the immemorial yearning for more wages,
there is no difference between one musical toiler and
another. The absurdly hairy star soloist is just as
rapacious, financially speaking,, as the underling who
bangs the triangle in company with other humble
noisemakers. In fact, he is usually considerably
more so.
Marion Dunn, daughter of Marcus Clarke (the
writer, not the draper), is of the third generation on
the Australian stage. Her mother, Marion Dunn, was
a daughter of John Dunn, an English comedian, who
came to Australia in the mid ’so’s of the lost last
century, and toured the country with his daughter
llosa (Mrs. L. L. Lewis). Marion had not then
appeared on the stage. John Dunn died in Melbourne,
and Rosa is now living in Paris, while Mrs. Marcus
Clarke has made her home at Oaldeigh, near Mel-
bourne.
J. S. Butters, the Melbourne identity who
passed out last Sunday (a most unsuitable
day for the passing of J. 5.8.), was not so
exceedingly ancient as rumor had alleged.
Ilis age is given as 83, which makes him
out to have been a mere youth of 38 when
he blazed in splendor as Mayor of Mel-
bourne at the time of the Duke of Edin-
burgh’s visit —45 years ago. All the same,
the prints which illustrated the Butters’ ball
of that period represent him as a man of
anything over 40. Probably Butters was
born middle-aged, hence jthe tradition that he.
had looked as old in J'§ss as he appeared in
the early years of the present century. Of
late, he was a bent and feeble old battler.
Apart from his capacity for knocking around!
town like an evergreen, there was nothing-
very remarkable about Butters. He was
always coupled with £ £ Smith in
Melbourne reminiscences/ and, like Smith, he
was a staunch believer in cold water for
outward application. Until he was well over
the three-score years and ten, Butters took
a cold plunge bath every night before going
to bed —winter and summer. But having
arrived at a bronchial-pneumonia stage in
his downward journey, he was persuaded by
the doctor to confine himself to a cold
shower in the morning.
THE ANGEL IN THE HASH-HOUSE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The dimpled smile of Elsie Jane
Is exquisitely fair ;
Around her like a golden rain
Ripples her shining hair ;
Her mouth is like the scarlet stain
That young geraniums wear.
I like to linger at her side
When sunset floods the sky,
And all the world is amber-dyed
Beyond the house-roofs high,
While solemn-hanclled knives divide
The stealc-and-kidney pie.
Large slabs of waitress come and go,
Bright soup-spoons rise and fall,
Floods of Calcutta curry flow,
And soft, far voices call
For various viands that I know
Through fissures in the wall.
But Elsie Jane with fingers white
Bears down the laden tray
To where I sit, and all delight
Blossoms amid the grey
Of steaming things that taste all right.
If served the proper way.
Sweet as the murmur of a psalm
In dim cathedrals bleak,
The tender silver of the ham
With rosy lean a-strealc;
O’er formless fronds of frizzled ram
My deathless love I speak.
I mention that the eve is cool,
Or that the day is good,
And sigh like tufts of cotton-wool
Across my simple food,
The while I give her glances full
Of special hardihood.
Oh, not for me the midnight way
With stout and oysters drear,
The crimson lustre of the Cray,
The song of sinful beer ! —
I only want at close’ of day
To come and eat down here.
But when the happy time is ripe,
My hot heart tells me so,
The last bright saucer she shall wipe,
And gladly we will go,
We two, afar from soup and tripe,
To see a picture show!
N.s.w.
CURSE O’ MOSES.

What wonder that Hazael of Syria, in the
bitterness of his soul, asked Elisha if his
servant was a “fair cow”?
Miss Rose Scott, of Sydney, once re-
marked there was no necessity to arm
against Japanese aggression, as the Japs
always treated very kindly those they van-
quished in war. The fate of the last Em-
press of Korea —the lady who was wiped out
by the Japanese embassy, being badly
wounded and then put on a fire before she
was quite dead —rather suggested that Miss
Scott’s views were rose-tinted. And they
were. Just now the whole of the English
press of Eastern Asia is saying things about
the awful methods of the Japanese Govern-
ment in a trial of some bu odd Koreans
charged with conspiracy to murder sundry
Jap officials. Between appearances at court
these unfortunate prisoners were tortured
separately, and to each one it was explained
that his friend had betrayed him. Confes-
sions were ready for these men to sign im-
mediately they got tired of torture, and by
this means convictions carrying the death
penalty were secured against tiie whole of
the accused. Killing, so long as it is done
quickly, is an unpleasant experience, but
bearable. The trouble about the Jap is that
he apparently prefaces it with a lot of pre-
liminary unpleasantness of a nature that
makes surrender quite a mistake —-of so un-
pleasant a nature, in fact, that it is better
to kill one’s wife and children first and then
die fighting. The trouble about people like
Miss Rose Scott is that they have done a lot
to prevent Australia getting into a condition
that would enable it to put up a most stupen-
dous fight before it was necessary to start
shooting the women and children as a crown-
ing mercy.
The Federal Cabinet has had further ideas
on the baby bonus, and has decided that the
amount should be paid in cases of still birth.
Which makes it a Dead Baby Bonus. Still,
it will appeal to that quaint class which
proudly advertises still births in the columns
of the daily paper.
Two historic residential corners of Sydney
will presently be transformed into modern
business premises. One is the south-east
corner of Liverpool and Elizabeth streets,
on which stands a very large house of ancient
date, the residence < " Judge Wylde in the
days when he waved the arm of the law over
N.S.W. In later years the house was occu-
pied by James Wright, who made beer on
the S.E. corner of Bathurst and George
streets. What sort of beer it was this
deponent knoweth not. The N.W. corner
of Liverpool and Elizabeth streets, on which
stand some solid houses, known as Clarendon
Terrace, will also make way for commerce.
In the long ago, on the corner was a square-
built red brick house, a common colonial
type then, which was the property of the
historic Mrs. Reibey. In the mid-forties it
was occupied as a ladies’ school, kept by
Mrs. Cousins. About 40 years ago the late
Dr. Arthur Renwiek purchased the corner
and erected thereon Clarendon Terrace,
which became the haunt of medical men and
tusk-pullers.

A new style of doorway to accommodate the new

The haphazard way in which the city of
Sydney has been allowed to grow up, or
tumble together, or fall over its own feet is
strikingly illustrated by the recent discovery
of a coffin in Clarence-street. The spot
where the decayed casket was found has
been shown by C. 11. Bertie to have been a
recognised burial-ground in 1792, but pri-
vate Enterprise, when it gets to work at
town-making, doesn’t worry over little mat-
ters like its grandmother’s grave or its
granduncle’s ashes. It just uses any old
tombstone that conies in its way for a door-
step, and piles its bricks over the place
where the dead are sleeping. It isn’t very
pretty, nor is it exactly sanitary, but when
ground is forced up in value to some pounds
per inch the public hasn’t room to be too
squeamish about such matters. If P.E.
had its own way Hookwood and Waverley
cemeteries would soon become solid masses
of terraced hovels, and even if memorial
tablets did stick up here and there through
the kitchen floor, there would be no redue
tion of rent on that account.
For nearly four months an apathetic mass
of constable has been sitting in a of
the Melbourne Hospital, waiting for Fre-
derick Kyle to make up his mind. On May
10 Frederick put in some smart work on an
attemnt to accomplish “the rash act” with
a razor. Since then he has been lying abed,
fluctuating between life and death, with
the eye of the law upon him. He is still
fluctuating. The eye of the law is never re-
moved, for fear the helpless patient might
fly without his clothes to some country
which has no extradition treaty. Police
surveillance in such circumstances is, it is
explained, a custom of the country. Mean-
while, the undermanned force is having its
usual bad luck with burglars, confidence
men, brutal assault people, murderers, bag-
'snatchers and sundries. All that doesn't
matter. An eye must be kept on Frederick
Kyle, who can’t turn in bed without assist-
ance. And yet if Frederick does recover he
may only get “the rising of the court.” Why
not give him the rising of the cop to <ro on
with? #

The recent sensa-
tional capture of a
burglar in Melbourne
by Geoffrey Syme was
promptly followed by
the usual feminine
narrative of a similar
exploit. A night or
two later the citizens
just across the river,
in Abbotsford, were
roused by the screams
and uproar of two
flurried females de-
manding that assist-
ance be immediately
forthcoming to cap-
ture _an armed bed-
room bushranger or
backyard brigand who
was nowhere visible.
W hen the police
reached the scene
rhey recognised one of the
ladies as Myra Adams, who was
recently discovered sitting, un-
conscious, in a motor, about 1
a.m., with an entire stranger,
and who told a tale of having
been asleep all day at St. Kilda
and having reached her then
position by a series of surpris-
ing exploits. Miss Adams told
how she and her mother had
been awakened by a condot-
tiere, who wore a brown-paper
mask and carried an electric
torch, a revolver and a gin
bottle filled with kerosene. The
obvious redundance of detail
blew a large hole of suspicion
in the tale, but the Johns al-
lowed the damsel to continue
while she narrated how the
burglar, having ransacked the
room to little purpose, had gone
to her father’s sleeping apart-
ment, and, without waking him,
had removed certain jewellery
from the pockets of his clothes,
hanging over the bed. He was,
she assured them, a tall man
with a fair complexion, and
wore a white felt hat and dark
clothes. The ’tecs listened ab-
stractedly, and, when she had
finished, one of them curtly asked
the half-baked storyteller to say just where
she had put the jewellery. She fenced for a
while, then said she had bestowed it in the
piano. That utensil, having been dismantled
without success, the pleasing child said she
must have put it behind the overmantel —
where it was found. She and her mother
ultimately excused themselves for their lapse
into fiction by blaming an overdose of cray-
fish for supper, but as this is the girls
second excursion into fictional narrative, this
writer would recommend her to drop cray-
fish, and try something large and meaty—
say, a whale or a couple of sharks. Thes«
crayfishy tales get wearisome.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It is tolerably safe to assume that the great mass
of the churchgoers and the great mass of the clergy
have little sympathy with the mediaeval action taken
by the Melbourne Council of Churches in regard to
the proposed baby bonus....lt is a sad commentary
on latter-day Christianity to find that any responsible
persons should shamelessly form themselves into a
vigilance committee for the purpose of hounding
down unfortunate girls who have violated the con-
ventions, and denying them, in their hour oi direst
need, the means of modifying the bitterness of tlieir
lot The world has been' congratulating itself that
the days of “The Scarlet Letter” were over
—Sydney Sun, 21/8/ 12.
Poor Mary’s none and earned the bonus.
Try an’ keep th’ news from gettin’ out.
Or the first thing that we’ll know,
We’ll be told to pack and go.
(‘Ave you looked inside the dustbox for a blighted
Bedroom Scout f)
There’s a sly and dirty legion of devoted holy gents ;
It has got no arms or colors, and you never see its
tents;
Yet it’s always on the warpath in a very private
way.
And it wins a little battle nearly every other day.
There are risks in all campaigning—there are men
with hobnailed feet,
And a gallant Scout at intervals is kicked into the
street;
But the heroes still keep at it, though the boots
are very hard, .
And the King hands out no medals to the Christian
Keyhole Guard.
If they haven’t mumbled o’er her —if she’s yielded
them no fee,
They will send the Cradle Sappers and the Blanket
Infantry;
And they’ll war with hints and whispers, in the
name of Him Who D'ed
For the sake of saint and sinner, with a thief on
either side.
Praise the rattlesnake and adder, give the honest
skunk a cheer,
Tell the toad that he is welcome, at the dingo never
sneer;
On the crawling things and wretched in a humble
spirit call,
For we’ve found a human Something that is meaner
than them all!
Jane went and got her kid “adovted.”
What become of it, well, goodness knows!
But take care now what you shout,
’Cause ; yer don’t know who’s about. ,
There might be a Bedroom Linesman in that pile 0’
dirty clothes !

The vegetarian is apt to run to hair, spec-
tacles, ’isms and soul-storms; but no one has
yet decided whether he is afflicted that way
because he lives without meat, or if
he does without meat because he
was going to be a crank, anyhow.
At any rate, he has the best of the argument
in Sydney, which was credited last week by
the experienced president of the Retail
Master Butchers’ Association with having
the most obsolete slaughtering methods
among the big white man’s cities of the
world. The same authority also alleged
that 33 per cent, of the meat sold in the
shops of Sydney is not fit for human con-
sumption, and that some of it is not fit for
dog consumption. The only possible consola-
tion for a dismayed capital is to earnestly
hope that the man of meat meant pure-bred
dogs of the most valuable sort —animals that
no person Would insult with inferior meat.
But the probabilities are that he was think-
ing of the gafint, dust-box raider who
travels most of his time as the advance agent
of a brickbat.
Sometimes the compositor builds better
than he knew, and the following (from the
Trundle Times) may or may not be a case
in point:—
WEDDING BELLS.
SMYTH V. PARNELL.
* * * *
Judging by a. case that was heard at
Brunswick (Vic.) Court lately, Premier
Watt might as well save a few pounds by
discharging his Police Magistrates and let-
ting the precious honorarium do as he
thinks best. In this matter, a milk dealer
was convicted for the fifth and sixth times
of watering his milk—thereby endangering
the health and lives of many infants, besides
robbing their parents—and the prosecuting
inspector asked for imprisonment without
the option, also that the conviction be pub-
lished. Seeing that the previous fines had
apparently only encouraged the milkman to
go “one better,” and that imprisonment would
certainly deter ■ him as long as he was in
prison, one would have expected the requests
to be granted without demur; but the four
honorariums on the Bench, having the usual
bourgeois views on commercial morality,
over-ruled the P.M., refused the order to
publish the conviction, and allowed the
delinquent to purge himself of his contempt
of the human stomach by paying £35 in
fines. What is the use of P.M.’s in courts
so constituted?

Volunteer Colonel Onslow, M.L.A., cut
loose in a Waverler (N.S.W.) Bethel the
other night, as follows: —
lie believed firmly in the voluntary system in
military service. Both Lord Roberts and Lord
Kitchener, who were advocates of the compulsory
system, made their way in command of black troops,
against whom he had not a word to say. A good
soldier might have his mind tinctured with ideas that
are not altogether suitable to the race from which
they sprang.
It was a kind thought on Onslow’s part to
act as apologist for his poor, misguided
fellow-warriors, Roberts and Kitchener; but
the good deed was superfluous. Neither
Roberts nor Kitchener ever commanded
“black troops” till they had become Generals.
Roberts’s first command was a European
field-battery—the Ivuram Field Force. He
remained with it till he got on the staff, and
from then till he became a General he never
commanded anything. Kitchener first joined
a company of sappers employed about the
barracks and fortifications on the Lower
Thames, and stayed there for eight years ;
then the company was sent to Cyprus. Dur-
ing the four years he stayed there Kitchener
learned Arabic, and, on the strength of that
accomplishment, got a staff-billet in Egypt.
But he commanded no blacks till his name
was made. In the nature of things, since
Britain has been almost exclusively fighting
niggers—and for the most part doing it with
other niggers—for the past half-century,
Kitchener, like Roberts, has had a good deal
of experience of “black troops.” But that is
not to say that he is disqualified from judging
as to the merits or otherwise of white sol-
diers, voluntary and compulsory. He, like
Roberts, had ample experience of the Cau-
casian volunteer fighter in S’Africa. Also,
like Roberts, he has had priceless opportuni-
ties to compare the volunteer of his own race
with the compulsory person of Germany and
France. If he and Roberts decide that the
British volunteer is dangerously ineffective,
by comparison with the soldier of Continental
Europe, the pertinent question arises:..Who
is Onslow, that he should speak in pitying
tolerance of them?

By the Oldest Inhabitant: —
Snow fell in Sydney on Thursday last. Previous
records show that on June 28, 1836, there was a
heavy fall in Sydney. It lasted half an hour, and
the oldest local Methuselah couldn’t remember an-
other. It lay about for a couple of hours, and Syd-
ney boys, for the first time in their lives, made
snowballs. In July, 1836, June, ’37, and September,
’37, snow also fell. On August 10, 1872, there was
a heavy snowstorm within 30 miles of Sydney. May
it never happen again.
A committee of the Uaori-
land House of Representa-
tives is proceeding, under
easy steam, to hear a peti-
tion from Captain Attwood,
one-time master of the
wrecked Elingamite, for
compensation for the loss of
his certificate after his ves-
sel tried to push the Three
Kings off the face of the
ocean, and sunk itself and a
heap of lives in the attempt.
That was a good many years
ago, and the skipper was
called many hard names and
told that he could go and
shovel coke, but that a life
on the rolling wave was not
for him, at least not as a
master. Then, only a few
months ago, it was discovered
that the chart, which was
official and supposedly of
ix-reproachable character, was
a liar, and that it not only
placed the Three Kings
where there wasn’t any King
at all, but it gave the posi-
tion they really occupied as
good, safe, deep water.
This was pleasant
hearing for the cap-
tain, but at the same
time it must be ad-
mitted that the honor-
able acquittal served
out to him at this late
date doesn’t make up
for the long years he
had to stand down
from his job. Of
course, the Maoriland
I authorities may say
that they didn’t make
the fraudulent chart,
but in the first place
they might have made an effort to
test its accuracy before accepting it as gospel,
and, in the second place, they might have
been expected to have some personal know-
ledge of the location of the boulder that stood
at their own front gate.

“Dungaree,” from the bridge:—
Our N.S.W. Navigation Department enters upon its
apparently final inspection of lighthouses this week.
(The loud cheering you hear in the distance is sup-
plied by the delighted local shellbacks.) Com-
mander Brewis, who has been pointing out the in-
numerable defects of the coastal lighting, accompanies
the party; consequently, we are hoping that this
time they will make an inspection of lights, not
lighthouses. Day-time inspections discover nothing
but beautifully polished lenses. I would ask the
officials to approach during the night that remark-
able illumination known as Wollongong Light. It
is a bright (?) fixed institution, with a red sector
showing over Bellambi Reef, and it is placed at the
end of the breakwater, level with a multitude of sur-
rounding bright lights. Its brightness or otherwise de-
pends, of course, upon the pressure of local mains, at
no time sufficiently, powerful to make the show dis-
tinguishable from the town lights. The fact is well
known to all Sydney shipmasters; and as one who
averages about three trips per week, I must say
that I have never been able to tell which of many
lamps is the one to keep me clear of danger. All
we mariners ask for is that our leading lanterns
shall have a character; by which we mean that when
seen they cannot possibly be mistaken for anything
else in the vicinity. Night cruising, of course, is
not as pleasant as sauntering down the coast in sun-
shine, but I think a little of it would prove in-
structive. At any rate, I would like the party to
stand off Wollqngong, after dark, and give one an-
other three guesses.
These are joyous times in the Sydney
tramcar industry, and bursts of humor are
mingled with objurgations. Sydney having
no city railway of any kind —ground level,
dead level, aerial, elevated, top-storey, sub-
terranean or otherwise —depends almost
wholly on trams. It being impossible to get
sufficient tramcars into the available space
to hold all passengers in an orderly fashion,
neople began to stand inside. Then they
stood on the footboards, first in one row
and then in two, and next they tried to
stand in three rows. About this time, con-
ductors began to fall off and get killed while
trying to collect fares, or to get the sack for
failing to collect fares. So they shut down
on the footboard riders, or tried to, but as
one conductor can’t fight 37 outside pas-
sengers, the only resource was the good old
strike. One evening recently about half a
mile of tramcars were held up because there
was on the first vehicle one obstinate foot-
board rider. He wouldn’t get off; it wasn’t
legal to kill him ; and the conductor declined
to move till he left. It was all so wildly
amusing that dozens of passengers almost
foamed at the mouth for pure joy. Things
of that sort add to the mirth of the com-
munity, but they make the community very
late for dinner.
The last handkerchief has
flapped, and the last parting
tear has splashed from the
promenade deck of the Orient
liner Opliir in Australian wat-
ers. It is to be over-hauled on
its arrival in London, and will
afterwards blossom forth in the
ads. as a “steam yacht,” and
will carry middle-class sons and
suburbanite daughters on Nor-
way trips to view the Midnight
Sun. It is 20 years since the
Ophir was launched. There
was just as much fuss made
then over its luxurious fittings,
magnificent appointments, huge
tonnage, and all the rest as are
splashed on the Olympics and
Titanics of to-day, and the dic-
tionary was dredged to its
depths to find high-falutin’ ad-
jectives to describe the “float-
ing palace,” “fair-sized towft-
sliip,” etc. Of course, the
Ophir’s high-water mark of
fame was touched when it was
selected as the royal yacht to
cart the Yorks and their retinue
around the world. Its first run
proved it a regular acrobat to
roll, and a prize coal-eater.
Various dodges remedied the
over-abundant rolling gait, but
a fuel glutton the Ophir re-
mained up to the last. It may
surprise people to know that
the apparently sluggish craft
was a first-class speedist if
pushed, and could run off 18
knots with the best of them.
But it was seldom asked to do
it, for at top speed it could
eat two coal-mines before break-
fast. The Norwegian trips will
suit the Ophir nicely, as it can
saunter along at a comfortable
and economical speed to give
the snap-shottists time to get
their focusses right. And the
royal memories" that linger
round its name will prove a
sure draw for the nobility of
Notting ’lll and the aristocrats
of ’Amnstead, Tghgate and
’Ammersmith.
It’s a wise lawyer that knows the right
hair to split. Last week, a N.S.W. country
policeman, who was proceeding against a
local publican for trading a few minutes
after hours, was beaten on the technical
point that his time wasn’t standard time, he
having obtained it from a railway porter,
who, of course, had nothing more reliable
than railway time to give away. If this
decision holds good, how is Constable Sleeper
to get on in future? One Horse Vale
has never had standard time, and doesn’t
know how to get it, and wouldn’t know
what to do with it if it got it. Anyhow,
what is the beast?
TUMMINESS.
Time’s nasty joke. The dread of every
beauty actor.
One of Nature’s dukes was last week
awarded six months retirement, at the Cen-
tral Police Court, Sydney. Poor gentleman,
he had stolen a rug from a sick horse. The
robbed animal coughed for help, and the dis-
gusted Law walked its despoiler slowly to
earth. . This is the sort of curiously miser-
able crime that brings the blush of shame to
the honest burglar’s nose.
SNOW IN SPRING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Snow in Melbourne! Snow on the South Coast! !
Snow in Sydney! ! !- —Daily paper headlines.
Spring looked in !
The morning sparrows cried her
And even I espied her
(At least I saw some fragments of the robe she wore
On the peach-tree blowing by the kitchen door).
\ \ \\ Spring looked in !
While Winter winds are blowing
And everywhere it’s snowing,
My heart is warmly hopeful, for her gown she tore
On the bare peach branches by the kitchen door.
N.S.W.
E. O’F.

q “Tangita Kino” : Onehunga, the little
wall-eyed, one-horse port to the west of Auckland
(M.L.), has a wrangle on about the establishment
of a zoological garden in its back yard. Some
people object to the presence of wild beasts under
their clothes-lines, and are . petitioning against the
iniquity of having their domestic poultry compelled
to associate with lions and tigers. Others are
eagerly asking the large and dangerous fauna of
nigger-infested lands to stay with them, and eat up
the marauding cow which roams in the streets and
devours the baby’s pinafore and the pink geraniums.
The tendency to herd strange wild beasts in the
vicinity of cities is growing in Maoriland. Where
you have any number of district capitals it is in-
evitable that each must,, in time, have its museum,
art gallery, zoo and botanical garden. The latter
is a simple affair, for even the humble cauliblossom
has its botanical interest, but the congregation of
wild carnivora is another matter. When the Shaky
Islands reach their final financial smash, and Cohen
puts in the bailiff, there may be enough lions and
tigers for sale to populate a- fair-sized tropical
jungle.
The careless farmer of the future will be
a man with one hand and half an ear. The use of
explosives for shattering subsoil and removing
stumps is now being demonstrated by various people
interested in the business, and the amount of mud
and wood hurled cheaply into the air at each report
is simply astonishing. The new system is going to
make farming a highly spectacular business, judging
by the results achieved at some of the demonstra-
tions—conducted, presumably, by people familiar
with the use of explosives. Huge stumps leap
bodily into the air, burst into pieces and spray their
fragments recklessly round the landscape. When the
casual, inexperienced farmer goes out to work with
an electric battery and a pocketful of sudden death,
a lot of the old dulness and safety will be blown
out of agriculture. Occasionally a farmer will be
blown out as well, but it will be in the noble cause
of progress. One advantage about the new idea is
that the cheap labor crowd will be able to barrack
for the cracking up of the Northern Territory by
bomb-throwing Italians and others. The day of the
agricultural anarchist is here.
* * * *
“Wal” : Quite the cutest way of fishing is
that of the Papuan who lives amongst the D’Entre-
casteaux Islands, to the east of Papua. The native
uses a kite to provide him with his dinner. The
fishing line is fastened to the tail of the kite, which
is connected with the fisherman in the usual way.
Brown brother seats himself comfortably in his
canoe and lets the kite go, its height being regulated
by the control cord, held by said B. brother, and
the wind does the rest. As the kite rises and dips,
the line, to which a native-made hook is attached,
trails along the surface, and, should a hungry “king”
or “salmon” be prowling around, he soon sees the
bait, with fatal effect to himself —and a feeling of
fulness and satisfaction to the man on the other
end of the line. The bait is a curiosity, being
simply a mass of spider’s web, but it has more to
do with the catching of the fish than the hook, for if
a fish once bites it he cannot let go, owing to his teeth
becoming entangled in the threads. The kite also
serves to tow the fisherman about in the canoe, so
he has little to do except smoke. The outfit is very
easily made. A few leaves serve for the kite, and
some native-made line and a sharpened fork of wood
for the fishing line and hook, whilst the bait can be
found under any house.

“Wal” : I was storekeeping on one of the
Papuan goldfields once, and, during my term as
guide, philosopher and friend to the miners I came
across some peculiar characters. Quite the hardest
case was an old German, Paul by name. One day,
when recovering from a more than usually purple
burst, he came to me in a penitent spirit, and asked
me to send away a fiver for some “drink cure” that
he had seen advertised in one of the Australian
papers. “Id oudt to do no harm if id no good me
shall do,” he said, “and if id cure me, den I in
your store no money more shall vaste on de blanky
trink.” After which kind
remark he got a bottle of
whisky, and was convoyed
to his claim by a couple
of his “boys.” I did not
see him for nearly a
month, when he came in
to ask if the “cure” had
come. He had a modest
one-night jag this time,
and then went off to his
claim. In due course the
mail brought a lot of pac-
kets of “drink cure” for
the old fellow, and I sent
them out to him with his
papers and a bottle Of
whisky. The. result was
extraordinary. A stream
of messengers kept coming
to the store with dirty
bits of paper, asking for
more grog; and I had
sent out quite a case of
whisky before I heard
what was going on. A
fellow came in and re-
marked that there was a
h—l of a jamboree going
on out at old Paul’s; guns
going off as if they were
having a Guy Fawkes Day
celebration, and all the
blokes as full of whisky
as a dog is of ticks. It
was the end of the month,
and I had to make my
usual round collecting the
accounts. I struck Paul’s
camp at dusk, being wel-
comed with a wealth of
joyous profanity and
invitations to take an
“oil.” The “drink cure”
was in great request by
the crowd. As old Paul
put it : “Stick a drob in
your bannikin and add a good nib o’ whisky, and
y’ get a vine donic—de best in de world.” And be-
fore I left I had an order for another fiver’s worth.
My opinion of “drink cures” after this experience
is nil.
“Jamboree”: Mrs. Murphy pQssessed a
tongue like a fret saw and an audience of one which
never smiled. It was her husband. None of us knew
that Murphy had a twin brother until he drifted
along from nowhere in particular and attached
himself to the household. He was not so worried
looking as Murphy; but as the idle days limped by
he developed the same harassed expression as his
host. I believe that I was the only one in the town-
ship who could distinguish Murphy from his brother.
The brother hadn’t quite the same life sentence look;
but you had to look hard before you could detect'
the difference. I soon noticed that the brothers were
taking it in turns to get drunk. Murphy was on a
good wicket, for his wife was short-sighted and her
glasses were usually blurred with her heavy breath-
ing—she always breathed hard while she raged at
her lawful lord and master. There were great pos-
sibilities in the situation, and I used to drop in
every day just to keep in touch and not miss any
developments. Nothing happened for a few months;
then, one night, Murphy got drunker than usual
and had to be delivered in a limp condition by cart.
I saw Mrs. Murphy in the main street next day, and
she told me that she had kicked her husband’s
brother out, as she couldn’t stand his drunken habits
any longer; besides there was a danger of him dis-
organising Murphy. I was passing the Murphy estate
a few days later and noticed the boss digging in the
garden. “Good day,” I shouted. The man with the
spade raised a haggard face. “Oh thunder!” I groan-
ed, and hurried away. Mrs. Murphy had thrown out
her husband !
