“Territorian” writes: The Yankee land
seekers, now exploring the Northern Territory, declare
that they are delighted with the country, which they
enthusiastically term, “Another God’s Own.” They
are surprised and fascinated by what they have seen
on the Victoria River. Small wonder. It is delight-
ful, and its atmosphere of infinite space is glorious.
The Victoria Downs have an area of 90,000 square
miles,, about 35,000 of which are most excellent
pasture land, divided into six cattle stations, with an
area of anything from 3000 to 12,000 square miles.
These are held on leasehold tenure, with 18 years yet
to run. The largest of them could muster 100,000
cattle, and the writer has seen 25,000 calves branded
in the season. The average carrying capacity is
safely 15 head of cattle to the square mile. In
Arnheim’s Land and round the Cambridge Gulf the
rainfall averages over 60 inches per annum. But on the
Victoria Downs the records read: Wave Hill station,
19in. ; AHctoria Downs, 22in.; Deldmere Downs,
30in. ; Bradshaw’s, 40in. The wet season sets in
during December, and continues till the end of
March, leaving the remainder of the year dry to all
practical purposes. With such a rainfall it is doubt-
ful if agricultural pursuits, as at present under-
stood, could be successfully carried on, but there is
no earthly doubt but that sheep- and Angora goat-
raising .would be a thriving industry, conjointly with
that of cattle and horses. Truly, sheep have been
tried and proved a failure. But that was owing to
the presence Of wild dogs. Closer settlement would
eliminate the dingo. The saddest part of the story
is that in this section of the Territory, 90,000 square
miles in extent, there are under 100 white men. A
station of 10,000 square miles, under existing con-
ditions, employs about seven whites and 50 or so
blacks. No wonder the Yankees are charmed and
surprised. The lure of this place is infinite and
eternal, and once a Territorian always one. What
surprised the cute Yankees probably was the fact that
such a splendid land of beautiful pastures should be
left to the mercy of a handful of monopolists in re-
turn for Is. per square mile rental. It would aston-
ish Satan himself, and he isn’t easily staggered. If
he were he would have long since given up his job.
* * * *
“Wal”: The discovery on the Lakekamu
goldfield of a stone mortar and pestle may be of
interest to students of the origin of the Papuan race.
The place where the relics were found is sparsely in-
habited, the only native population being people
known as “Kukukuku,” and they only visit the place
on their hunting trips. Who or what the people
were who used pestles and mortars will take a deal
of investigation, for they evidently do not inhabit
the country now. None of the known natives employ
these things. The nearest I have seen is the wooden
device that the coastal man uses to crush his betel
nut when his teeth have worn out. I should think the
pestle-and-mortar folk must have been of strong
physique. A man would want some Tiffing power to
handle a pestle like the Lakekamu one, as it weighs
over 201 b. The man that found it dug it up from
under 9ft. of wash, in a terrace claim some 100 ft.
above the present creek level. Perhaps some geolo-
gist can estimate the age of these relics of a forgotten
people.
“Montalex” : Some more about the sting-
aree (“Reka,” B. 15/8/T2). There’s a big family
of him ( Trygonidce ). About the Mediterranean
they eat him. Don’t know what particular species
that is, but have made unpleasant personal acquaint-
ance with the only kind that is found as far north as
the U.K. Trod on the beast in about three feet of
water and got nailed in the calf of the leg. Suffered
Sheol until I got morphia injected into the wound,:
but that didn’t cure the poisoning, which persisted
until the doctor began to drop hints about gangrene.
After about six months, however, the wound healed
up quite suddenly. The local fishermen said this,
was because the stingaree that stabbed me had got
killed. All this from a spine only two inches long,
so I can quite credit the stories of deaths caused by
the big tropical fellows. The torpedoes—the electric-
shockers—are cousins of the stingarees, and are

DOWN THE LACHLAN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Bearing bravely down the Lachlan, homeward bound
the wool-teams go,
Cargo ships of station traffic, beating out unto the
west;
Toiling to alluring havens set amid the sunset’s
glow;
Swaying in the mocking mirage dancing on the
sand-hill’s crest;
Steering out to lonely homesteads, nestling on a
grey-grassed shore,
Fetching to the waiting bush-folk, o’er the silent
troubled ways,
Freight more precious than the wealth the flying
Spanish galleons bore
From a sacked and shattered city in the far-off
fabled days.
Flying gaily down the Lachlan, westward bound the
drovers fare,
Hobble-chains and tinkling horse-bells sounding
music through the trees ;
They, the lightly-burdened clippers, scudding to the
regions where
Flock on flock of gold-fleeced wethers gild wide,
waving, verdant seas.
Hear their chanteys, lilting quaintly, when above the
grey plain’s rim,
Through the river-box and mallee blink and flash
the station lights;
Hear the swagman gladly hail them, when the
evening sun is dim:
“Any news from up the river?”—then' they yarn
away the nights.
Drifting sadly down the Lachlan, lurch the human
wrecks we know,
Derelicts of proud ambition, shattered hulls of high
desire,
Caught in casual currents, swirling to the unknown
depths below,
Flotsam of the social whirlpools, rotting by the
river’s mire.
River timbers hide their camp-fires, river voices
soothe their pain;
But the mirage, dancing, glinting, takes them to
some far-off scene,
Back where hope and love were luring, like the
mirage on the plain,
But to end in bitter memories of some things that
might have been.
N.S.W.
GORDON BENNETT.
BROWN PERRIGOT
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
BROWN Perrigot, that nice old man,
At work ’mid his cabbage rows,
Whistles a tune, and, out of a pan,
Spills salt each way as he goes—
Then stops, and cocks one eye at the sky,
While he feels with his paws for rain:
“Nor’, Nor’ East,” he says, with a sigh,
“An’ blowin’ great guns again.”
The wind pipes shrill, like a death-struck
P*S,
And the clothes on the next-door line
Dance an indelicate, legless jig,
Which the old boy thinks “just fine.”
Then the clouds spit fast the sharp, cold
hail,
And Perrigot turns to flee
Into the apple-house out of the gale
As fast as his legs can gee.
And there, on a bucket against the ground,
He sits, still holding his pan,
With pullets and cockerels hemming him
round,
A shivering, silly old man,
With small, red eyes and still’, blunt
bones —
There be few who ever would guess
That once on a time he was Blood scupper
Jones,
Of the pirate ship Black Bess!
N.S.W.
HUGH McCRAE.

Frank Bellman tells about a short cut: —
Flapping at the mosquitoes with his empty tucker-
bag, Scotty sat on tile table and told me wiiat he
was go.ng to do to lied Sam, when he met him on
the track. By exercising rigorous self-denial, Scotty
had brought a bottle of whisky from the township
to Sam, the men's cook at Wilga; expecting, in re-
turn, three or four days spell in the travellers’ hut and
the chance of getting Sam’s billet if the alleged
“Glenira” should start him on his bi-annual spree.
Unfortunately, Sam had been temporarily converted
by a bush missionary, and when Scotty handed him
the bottle he broke its neck, emptied the contents
into the pigs’ tub and slammed the door in the face
of the amazed donor.
Scotty inspected our small stock of food. ‘We 11
hae’ to gae to Nerong the nicht —na mair na sax
miles by cuttin’ off the bends o’ the river, an’ its
na that dark.”
I was tired, and protested: “Can’t we wait till
morning?”
Scotty sniffed compassionately: “Of a’ the ignor-
antest new-chums! Will Ina hae to lug the cook
afore the men’s aboot?”
As we were rolling up, the little Irishman in
the corner angrily slapped the iron wall: “Are youse
blokes iver goin’ to slape?”
(Here is an interval of three hours.)
Climbing over logs, splashing through shallow
lagoons and struggling with acres of wild rasp-
berry, we had travelled miles and miles. I was dog-
tired, and often wanted to throw myself down and
sleep, but as often Scotty would repeat a varying
yarn about the skeleton of a presumptive new-chum
that had been found on the river flats.
As Scotty was vigorously denying, for about the
tenth time, that he was bushed, some iron buildings
loomed ahead, and he pointed to them with a
triumphant grin. “Them’s Nerong.”
“Don’t they look like the huts at Wilga?” I
asked diffidently.
Scotty would not answer till we reached the
nearest door, then he growled: “Ane shearers’ hut.
is like anither.”
As we entered I struck a match, and a little
Irishman inquired plaintively, “Are youse blokes
iver goin’ to slape?”

“Tamnmu”: The blue paint recipe of
“Veritas”’ (B. 4/7/’l2), for making a ratless solitude,
is new to me. But I can tell him of a method
much favored in Maori iand bush camps. The
method is simplicity' itself. (1) Catch your rat.
(2) (jive him a bath in kerosene. (3) Apply a match
to him and let him go. His relations and friends
in the vicinity, on catching sight of this flaming
fantod, adjourn in haste and run hard for three
weeks in all directions. Daresay the method would
hardly recommend itself to the S.P.C.A., yet it used
to be employed by the Church in the case of heretics.
When a flaming heretic ran at full speed through a
congregation, I bet there was a dispersal.
“Hayland” : The wild pigs at the hack of
North Canterbury (M.L.) are fair cows. Was muster-
ing there recently, and saw across a deep-cut gully!
a porker emerge from the bush and make for a;
young lamb. The woolly infant started for home and
mother, but the hog ran down "his prey in short
order, killed it, and proceeded to have lunch. When
I left he was reaching for a toothpick and the liqueur
bottle.

“McNorth”: I had just arrived in the
Yodda Valley, Papua. It was. in the days of the
rush after the nimble pennyweight in those regions,
and camping ground was scarce, owing to
the lack of flat surface. Green food was only
less difficult to obtain than the yellow
metal itself. After a lot of looking round,
I pitched on a spot close to another
man’s camp. There was a mound be-
tween the two tents, and, when I inter-
viewed the other chap about its meaning
and purpose, he explained that his mate
had died the previous week, and that he
had buried him in the only place where
it was possible to sink a hole. I didn’t
like it. If I could have found another
camping ground I would have shifted in
a hurry. But there was no other flat
surface to be got for miles around, and I
was compelled to take my nightly rest by
the side of a new-made grave. One day,
coming home from the claim a little
earlier than usual, I saw something which,
even in that mild climate, caused the
temperature of my blood to drop several
degrees. My neighbor was exhuming his
dead mate. Shovelful by shovelful he
shifted the mullock, and at last brought
to light—no, it was not a corpse, but a
full bag of good potatoes and onions. The
alleged grave was his safe deposit for fresh food.
• * * *
“Mary McC.”: Fierce as the statement
may appear, there are some countries from which
Australia has something to learn. Cow fills a big
place in our national landscape, and butter-churning
Denmark might, with plenty of advantage to us and
herself, send us some of her surplus milk extracters
and bull coaxers. From time to time she has done
so, but in almost every case the immigrant Dane:
has returned to his own pastures with a look of dis-
gust settled upon his intelligent features. The
reason is not a joyful one. The man from Jutland
has no objection to work, and is used to putting in
long hours at his job, but he finds Australian condi-
tions, too much for him. It is the absence of -intel-
lectual companionship that breaks him up. In the
matter of education tlie Danish peasant is about five
centuries ahead of the -Australian cow cocky. Often
the man at the pail and churp speaks two or three lan-
guages,- and has a good knowledge of European litera-
ture. His Australian employer;- on the other hand, speaks
one language injuriously* and has a magnificent ignoi-
ance of everything outside his own eowyard. It is
worthy of note that Danish education hasn’t in any
way lowered the price of Danish butter in London.
THE AMATEUR DROTER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They were short of men at Castlebanlc,
As shearing time came round ;
-Five hundred ewes at the Three-Mile Tank
And no one to be found,
To bring them to the homestead yard ;
The track was wide and clear and hard.
The boss 'called, in his anguish deep,
The uew-chum, just to hand ;
And said: “Oh, fetch those crimson sheep.
There’s neither scrub nor sand ;
The track is plain ; you can’t go wrong,
The ewes are fat and fairly strong.”
’Twas mid-day out at Castlebanlc,
The new-chum galloped in ;
The foam was on his horse's flank,
The boss looked black as sin.
The new-chum softly mopped his face,
And touched his hat with courtly grace.
“There’s something wrong,” he gently said ;
“The sheep, I think, are sick.
They’re lying down, and some are dead,
At first they travelled quick,
And galloped fast, and came on well!”
“They galloped!” gasped the boss. “O Hell!”
“But now,” the new-chum babbled, “when
They canter hard they trip,
And won’t get up. I’ll try again,
I came back for a whip!”
The boss had swooned. His face went grey,
We watched beside his bed all day.
VICTORIA.
FRANK RADCLIFFE.
A New Minister.
This letter lias come to hand from some
party unknown
Mr Bulletin, Sir: I rise to move for the appoint-
ment of a Minister, who seems to be called for more
than the Vigorous Progressive Policy, the New
Liberal Leader and the Long-felt Want rolled into
one. He is to be called the Minister for Compli-
ments, and his. especial function will be the respon-
sibilitv for public congratulations and condolences.
At least they will be his strong suits, though he
will have other spheres of usefulness, as you shall
hear. Whenever any king, queen, pope, archbishop,
sultan, highcoekalorum, Methodist president, prize-
fighter, or other person Of great distinction is born,
married, ascends the throne, has his life saved, wins
a horse race or a prize in Tatt.’s, or is otherwise
entitled to shout drinks; or whenever the said
potentate or important bloke is sick, dead, re-
volushed' or shot at, the Minister for Compliments
is to be the sole officer for passing out the appro-
priate congratulation, sympathy, joy or grief of
this country to the person aforesaid or his next of
kin. All governors, politicians, mayors, aldermen,
shire councillors and newspaper editors will be re-
quired, under Section 2 of the Enabling Act, to
keep their eloquence severely repressed. The object
of the Minister’s appointment is to save the absurd
waste of time, ink and gab which would otherwise
be let loose on a long-suffering public.
By way of illustrating- how this appointment is
really shouted for, consider these facts. A short
time ago we were solemnly informed that the
Mikado of Japan was dead. The Japanese Consul in
the city this screed comes from portentously an-
nounced the news to the daily papers, with observa-
tions about his “mournful duty,” etc., and in
Parliament the Premier moved a vote of condolence
or something, and talked the usual piffle about the
profound sorrow of the Australian people. The
same consul, also from his place in the House, spoke
feelingly of the deep grief that must be felt by the
Japanese subjects. A day or two afterwards he
made another public announcement about how he
had communicated it all to the Jap Foreign Min-
ister, who had replied that our expressions of
sorrow, etc., and the fact “that the Town Hall bells
had been rung (!) were highly valued,” etc. It
was, as a fact, a comparatively light let-off for the
public at that.
Occasionally an Admiral happens along at some
Australian State capital. When this occurs sundry
guns are banged off from the ships, followed by
corresponding explosions from the shore. Then the
Admiral calls on the Governor, the Governor calls
on the Admiral, the Mayor calls on the Admiral, the
Admiral calls on the Mayor, the Aide-de-camp calls
on the Town Clerk, and the boatswain calls on the
Inspector of Nuisances. .By this time the calling
business has got so complex that the Admiral ends
it by heaving anchor and clearing out.
Under my -scheme, all’ this ridiculous waste of
cards- and wear and tear of uniforms is saved. Sup-
pose, for instance, a royal marriage is announced.
The Minister for Compliments cables the parties:
“Good luck.’ Don’t, do. it again. Butter-dish follows
by-post.”. Or, if the Admiral arrives, he. will, send
up an A.B. With his card to the Minister. The
Minister will endorse it, “Received,” and if he feels
so inclined, will say to the A. 8.: “Tell your boss
we’re glad he’s here, and ask him to drop in for a
drink any time he’s passing. Have one yourself
now.” Honor is satisfied, and -the-whole business
done expeditiously. You see how simple it is!
If at any time it seems necessary to take any
extra notice.-of an .event, the Speaker may perhaps
be allowed (with the consent .of the Minister) to
rise and say: “Gentlemen, the Princess Bullterria,
of England, is affianced to the Grand Duke of
Dachshund —er question of congratulations the
Minister for Compliments.” ' The -Minister will
probably interject: “Righto,” or “Fair thing,” and
the Speaker,’ without .losing his stride, will add:
“Done. Next business.” Or he may announce:
“Gentlemen, the Shah of . Heligoland is: reported
dead. That this House is agreed that he is
dead. Forthequestionthosewhosayayeontheeontraryno
theayeshaveit. MotionforthesuppressionofWowsersthe
honorableMemberforWallabyHills.” Then if any fat-
headed Member gets up and tries to air his grief,
the Speaker will promptly say: “The honorable
Member is disorderly,” and the Sergeant-at-Arms
will get his tin sword ready.
Balls, weddings, funerals and receptions will be
side-lines of the Minister, and his department will,
of course, be kept adequately supplied with butter-
dishes, confetti, kid gloves and crape. This line
will appear on the annual Estimates, and whenever
the Opposition feel critical about the Minister’s con-
duct, it will take the form of a motion to reduce
the Confetti-and-Crape vote, but that will be a
trifling circumstance. —Yours, Bill O’Costs.
“Batman’s Ghost” : The grimy coal-hulk —
the stripped body of an old-time tea clipper
—lay alongside the 12,000-ton P. and O.
steamer. The poop deck, once the scoured
and burnished pride of a captain who pro-
bably scorned steam, was desecrated with a
line of assorted female washing hung out by
the lighterman’s wife. So much for the
proud clipper of 40 years ago. Not far
away, looking absurdly small and dirty, lay
what had once been an inter-State steamer
of importance, now humbly engaged in carry-
ing coal. So much for the steamer of 20
years ago. Time passes and ships grow taller
and deeper. Only the harbors remain the
same. All the Harbor Trusts’ dredges and
men can’t do much more for Sydney
and Melbourne —-especially Melbourne—than
Nature has done already. Sand-shifting is
a costly and never-ending business, and sub-
merged rock-blasting in a tide-race like the
Port Phillip Rip has its financial limits. It
is sorrowful to have these facts in mind
when considering three generations of ships,
the last of which, the 12,000-ton P. and 0.,
represents about the limit of Victoria’s one
harbor, or rather the one dashed bad harbor
which the congestion policy allows Victoria
to use. How many years has Melbourne
got before such items as the following will
cease to brighten the shipping column of the
Age :
In consequence of a large influx of ocean liners
yesterday morning, Hobson’s Bay presented an un-
usually busy appearance. The first passenger steamer
of note to put in an appearance was the five-masted
Federal Shire liner Argyllshire, from Liverpool, via
Capetown and Adelaide. Almost simultaneous with
the arrival of the Argyllshire was that of another
Federal Shire liner, the Cornwall, from London, via
Adelaide. Steaming up the bay behind the Federal
Shire boats came the two magnificent mail steamers
Osterley and Malwa. The Orient liner was coming
from Sydney and Brisbane, outward bound, and
the P. and O. boat was arriving from London. An-
other notable arrival to consort the mail steamers
up the bay was that of the Tyser line’s latest ad-
dition to the fleet, the immigrant s. Makarini.
The thin shadow of the future is thrown
across the column in the very next item: —
Beyond the little Launceston trader Wareatea and
a number of small coasting steamers, the only depar-
ture from port yesterday was the tramp s. Tordensk-
jold, which arrived from Ocean Island, via Wallaroo,
a week ago.
Tramps and little coasters! How many
more years for the one congested port of
Victoria? You can figure it out pretty
accurately by contrasting the new ships
with the old, and allowing a discount—a
fairly liberal discount —for the more rapid
development of the scornful and towering
40,000-tonner of the future.
From “Silaba” : “Dano” (B. 8/8/T2) errs
as to South Africa being the only place capable of
supplying Australia with the ostriches required to
make a really good start in the feather trade. As a
matter of fact, the Union is far from satisfied with
the quality of its birds. I myself saw a shipment of
80 ostriches landed in Capetown in May this year.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
Ex-Premier Jack Verran, of S.A., in
attacking a Liberal member who had hint.d
maladministration on the part of the late
Labor Cabinet, said: “The honorable mem-
ber likes telling stories like these. He glories
in it. He sticks his spike into every dead
carcase he finds in the street, and holds it
up before the public, and then rolls it under
his tongue as a rich morsel.”
The Melbourne Historical Society is not
living in vain. It has summoned a spirit of
unrelenting piety from the dead past. The
other night the Rev. Waterfield, son of the
first Congregationalist shepherd in Mel-
bourne, beguiled the society with some ex-
tracts from the diary of his revered father.
Rev. Waterfield, deceased, seems to have re-
garded the frivolity of youth from the same
point of view as the lamentable John Wes-
ley, who railed against the parental indis-
cretion of permitting children to have holi-
days from school. His diary recorded how
the youth of the period imperilled their sal-
vation at a certain New Year’s Day picnic
by “sinfully walking about, instead of re-
maining with their elders, as young folks
nhould.” Fortunately, one or two* of them
fell over stumps and were bruised. Rev.
Waterfield, deceased, seems to have drawn
some comfort from the reflection that their
sin had found them out. Yet he was, doubt-
less, a Good Man in his generation. He was
a Wowser of Early Melbourne, neither better
nor worse than his up-to-date brethren who
prowl around the parks seeking for sights
to be shocked at.

GHOSTS , OR A TROUBLED
CONSCIENCE.
Apropos Federal Labor’s relations to Increased Im-
portations, the Hop Poon Gooey precedent, defaulting
cadets, etc., etc.
Andy has fallen into a bad though easy
habit , which doesn't altogether make for peace
of mind.
Wilfred Blackett, barrister, of Sydney, has
become the 16th K.C. on the N.S.W. roll,
and wears the silk gown with aplomb and
everything else a silk gown should be worn
with. The new K.C. drove a brilliant pen
on The Bulletin some 26 years ago, and
in a period when typewriters were almost
unknown, and the best journalist had a habit
of being also the most illegible, the fact that
he wrote a clerkly hand made him a good
exception to the bad old rule. There are still
compositors hereabouts who, if they commit
murder in their later years and make a
failure of the concealment of the body, will
clamor to have Mr. Blackett defenci tnem re-
gardless of cost (though the defence of
elderly and bloodstained compositors isn t
his general line of business) for the sake of
those pleasant bygone days.
“H.” re a hook-nosed apostle:—
The best notice of General Booth is in the auto-
biography of ex-Commissioner Nicol, written with-
out 0 the tinge of animus which is evinced by other
ex-officers. Nicol and wife joined the Army over 30
vears ago. He rose to bo Booth’s most valuable man,
next to son Bramwell. Nicol came with Booth on
his first trip to Australia, and engineered it. A more
devoted adherent there could not be. Like Pollard,
Booth’s chief man in another visit to Aus-
tralia ; and Pollard also has left the Army.
Why do such men go? Mr. _ and Mrs. Nicol
gave up a comfortable position in life when they
joined the Salvationists, and underwent severe priva-
tions. Nicol renders every justice to the General, and
recognises his commanding ability. Yet Nicol feels
that the Army is a failure. He. could not continue
in association with what he thought to be dubious
practices. He joined a great religious movement,
which he thought came like Wesley’s, to quicken
spiritual life. It has changed into something else.
The members of the Army, apart from the enormous
body of paid officers, are really few all over the
world. The myriads of so-called conversions a.re like
pouring water through a colander. The social up-
heaval attempted by Booth is beyond the strength
of anv such organisation, though Government praises
such things for feeble endeavors to perform the duty
which belongs to it. Citizens at large feel this more
and more as the chorus of praise swells over General
Booth. What he is praised for people do not exactly
know. The most graphic part of Nicol’s book is the
account of how Ballington Booth was dislodged from
America. After leaving Australia, Ballington had
nine glorious years there, and, indeed, consolidated
the Army. Nicol shows how scrupulously fair the
General was in dealing with his family, wherefore
the secessions of Catherine, Ballington and Herbert.
The usual term of a commanding officer in anv country
is four years. In Australia this was lengthened by
the General, through the conspicuous success of
Howard, Coombs and McKie, and in Barker’s case
through his putting somewhat of a screw on. How-
ever, when Ballington had enjoyed his U.S. billet for
more than double the term, the General said: “In
all fairness I must remove you.” Ballington jibbed.
The General sent daughter Eva to supersede him,
with Nicol as Chief Officer. Ballington shut them
out of the New York barracks, where he had con-
vened a meeting of American officers. To these he
expounded his scheme for an American Army, and
claimed their adhesion. Eva and Nicol were outside
in despair, till Nicol introduced Eva in a surreptitious
way, apparently through a back door or window.
She flew to the room where Ballington was address-
ing the officers, burst in with an impassioned oration,
and won the day. It makes one think of the great
crisis in Napoleon’s life, before the Legislature in
Paris, only the Napoleon of this case had to depart
into the wilderness. Ballington departed in baffled
rage. Such are behind-scene doings.

course I know you very well, Mr. Abra-
hams.”
“W’lang”: Dr. G. E.
Morrison’s biographer (B.
15/8/T2) is as inaccu-
rate as they are made.
G.E.M. is not a Mel;
bournite —he’s a Geelong
native, being a son of Dr.
Morrison, for years head
of the local Scotch Col-
lege. His first long walk
was from Queenscliff to
Adelaide, when he was 18
or thereabouts. It was
on this journey that he
learned the great and
abiding truth that boots
without stiffening at the
heels are best for long
walking trips. The spear
thrusts he got were one
in the eye and the other
in the abdomen. Tart
of the weapon which ac-
complished the latter
puncture was extracted
from him while he was
doing his medical course
at Glasgow. I met him
after his return from
Papua. His lack of the
swelled-head complaint
was a gracious contrast
to the condition of Julian
Thomas, alias “The Vag-
abond.” Julian represented the Argus in
an expedition to Tapua at the same time as
Morrison, and had the misfortune to sustain
an attack of atrophy of the sense of modesty,
from which he never fully recovered.
“O.Iv.”: When the death of Bonnor, the
cricket giant, was announced it also tran-
spired, to the surprise of many people, that
the mighty smiter of yore was in a bad way
financially for years before his health broke
up. Poor Bonnor! Why, he might have
earned a decent living on the stage as
Charles the Wrestler, or any character in
which his handsome Saxon face and 78
inches of towering manhood would have
made him the ideal exponent. Neither on
the cricket field nor off it did the batting
Goliath make proper use of his physique.
“Road and Bridges” writes : —-
As foretold in The Bulletin some months ago,
“Holy Joe” Davis is to return from London to pre-
side over the N.S.W. Works Department. Davis was
Under-Secretarv in the more or less dim past, but
when Cecil Darley skied the job of engineering ex-
pert in London, Joseph, somewhat to the relief of
the P.W. Department, succeeded him there. The pre-
sent intention is to make Davis the Director of
Works and Permanent Head of the Department. This
has been made possible by appointing Under-Sec.
Hanna Public Service Commissioner. The Govern-
ment is well satisfied with the change, doubtless, but
we of the P.W. Department are the very reverse. As
regards persons named Joseph we have no luck.
Once another J oseph—Barling—was our boss, and
when he went over as P.S.B. Commissioner, we were
wildly happy. Now we lose a popular boss the same
way and get another Joseph.
“Logos” : Let me raise a howl against the
increasing misuse of the word “obsessed.”
The demonological distinction was that,
while a person would be “possessed” by a
devil dwelling within him, he would be “ob-
sessed” by a devil approaching him from
without. A Callan Park (N.S.W.) medico
states that Chidley is obsessed by his theory.
But, since Childey’s theory, whatever it is,
resides permanently in his own head, or
stomach, or wherever theories dwell, it pos-
sesses him, or he possesses it. There’s no
obsession in the matter. #

The Old Man’s send off: —
One of the best known and oldest identities of
Melbourne, James Stewart Butters, . passed out on
Saturday last in his 81st year. He was born at Bal-
gownie, and entered a softgoods’ warehouse in Dun-
dee (Scotland), where he remained five years. In
1853 he married Miss Helen Sparkie, and sailed in
the Pauline for Australia, arriving after a voyage of
134 days. Two days after his arrival he entered the
service of Nathaniel Dismoor, a silk mercer of
Collins-street East (he was a son-in-law of the first
Anthony Hordern, and had his establishment within
a few doors of Swanston-street, on the north
side of Collins-street). Subsequently he went
to Ross’s, in Bourke-street, where he met one
Wilkie, and the pair opened business in Warrnambool.
He lost his wife there and came to Melbourne. In
1860 he married Miss O’Neill, a squatter’s daughter.
She died in 1878. A third time he wedded, Miss
Ilattermann, a native of Bendigo, of German ex-
traction. She died in 1885 of consumption. In
1856 Butters joined W. G. Baillie in the business of
gold-buyers and stockbrokers, which trade was car-
ried on for 14 years. They were about the first
sharebrokers in Melbourne, Mr. Butters being one of
the founders of the Stock Exchange. In 1865 he
became a member of the City Council, and in 1868,
the year of the arrival of the Duke of Edinburgh,
he was Mayor. At the fancy dress ball (at which the
writer was present), Mrs. Butters appeared as “The
Press,” her dress being the newspapers of the day,
printed on silk. In that year Butters was elected
member for Portland in the Legislative Assembly,
and held the seat under difficulties. There was a
fund in existence from which members of Parliament
were alleged to be paid for services, and Butters
was, on inquiry, deemed to be one of the agents for
the distribution of the fund. Expulsion followed,
but he was re-elected. In 1870, however, having
acquired land interests in Fiji, he and his partner
Baillie went there. Here Baillie was murdered by
parties who are still at large. A large number of
people from Victoria and N. S. Wales about that
time established themselves in Fiji. Thackambau
fell in with the idea of a local Parliament and
Ministry. Sydne}' Burt, of Sydney, was Chief Secre-
tary ; Lockwood, of Victoria, was in the Ministry;
Butters was Speaker of the Assembly. Britain woke
up, however, and sent Hercules Robinson along to
“annex,” and the local Parliament was thrown out.
In 1874 Butters returned to Melbourne, and started
the Victorian Club adjoining the Town Hall. Since
then his career has been of a varied character. Of
late years he had an interest in an hotel at the sea-
side, out Mornington way. He was an able worker
in all public charities, but was too open-handed a
character to pile up money. He has left a grown-up
family. His mining ventures were of the biggest,
yet he died poor. Perhaps it should be said that he
naturally died poor because of his ventures. But he
lived every day of his strenuous 81 years, and found
this world a good place to dwell in.
Thomas George Macarthy,
the white-haired ancient who.
until lately, built terraces of
beer in Wellington (M.L.),
created a sensation by the will
he left behind him. The brew-
ing of decayed barley and frag-
rant herbs had made him the
owner of £500,000. Part of the
cash is to go to charitable and
educational purposes, but some
pleasing little legacies and an-
nuities are to be scattered
round. The poor and afflicted
of Geelong (Vic.) are to get
£lOOO, and an annuity for the
deceased’s mother-in-law is also
in the bill. But the most re-
markable provision is one which
instructs the Public Trustee to
look after any husband whom
the widow of the testator may
at any time annex or otherwise acquire.

bably appeal for rebutting evidence to men
who are not so far through.
THE MAN WITH THE AXE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The council would not be justified in giving Colonel
Onslow information. He would simply go and raise
Cain in the House. Ail Colonel Onslow wanted was
to get an axe with which to attack the Government.
—The Mayor of Vaucluse (Sydney).
Behold, where runs a wild and lurid bloke
Who leaves a stream of gore in all his tracks —
No, it is not a prehistoric joke ;
It is the gallant Colonel with the axe.
How will the Ministry fall in affright
And James McGowen tremble in his shoes,
Before the dreadful and horrific sight
Of Onslow, shrieking “Vengeance and Vaucluse!”
Most frightful is the Colonel in his wrath !
His followers are Agony and Pain ;
And Blood and Murder prowl along the path,
For Onslow is abroad, and raising Cain.
Cain, all red-handed with his brother’s life,
Whose greedy thirst the heart’s blood hardly slakes,
While plunges deep the fratricidal knife—
Oh, such is Onslow when his fury wakes!
Only, when Onslow waves his hatchet wild,
And resurrects the awful ghost of Cain,
Why has the other party always smiled,
And sunk to sweet forgetfulness again?
Wade waves the sword, and Onslow shakes the spear,
Fitzpatrick pulls the vizor o’er his brow,
But no one shows the slightest sign of fear—
We’ve grown too well accustomed to it now.
N.S.W.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The Australian PostmasteuGeneral’s De-
partment—the concern which is behind time with the
letters and deaf with the telephone—isn’t making
much of a name for itself in the matter of finance.
This is the record of recent years:—
Revenue. Expenses. Surplus.
1906- .. £3,128,574 £2,690,363 £438,211
1907- . . 3,300,096 2,933,835 366,261
1908- .. 3,409,425 3,069,868 339,557
1909- . . 3,731,741 3,231,198 500,543
1910- .. 3,905,131 3,561,928 343,203
1911- .. 3,911,286 3,827,160 84,126
Estimate. Shortage.
1912- .. 4,202,000 4,253,410 51,410
Now, in the first six years enumerated, there was paid
for new works out of the general revenue —not out
of loans —about £3,900,000, and another £BOO,OOO
is to be expended this year. There being no interest
bill to pay on these sums, the profits of the new
works are all available to help the revenue, and
should have greatly improved the position. Even if
necessary increases of wages consumed a good deal
the department should at least have kept solvent.
The slump of £551,000 in three years looks an ap-
palling case of muddle and bad management. The
Fisher Government reckons on a general shortage
of about £2,261,500 this year, which may just
about be covered by the surpluses left over from pre-
vious years. After they are gone it looks as if
serious troubles might be expected. A good part of
the expenditure arises through the construction of
public works out of revenue instead of the old
familiar loans, and this is a wholly commendable
business. A deficit caused in such a fashion isn’t
the ordinary kind of deficit. But the advantage of
building public works out of revenue is that the re-
ceipts from them may add to the income instead of
going to Cohen. And in the case of the posts and
telegraphs, it seems that the more unencumbered
public works there are to yield revenue the less net
revenue there is. That is a miserable outlook for
the Baby Bonus Ministry.
The N.S.W. Government flounders along
with its Gas Bill, and seems to lose some of its con-
fidence as it goes, just as it did over its wonderful
Income Tax Bill —the one which proposed to tax
the small local manufacturing co. at about twice the
rate of the fairly large absentee landlord. In fact,
its measure is so full of anomalies that confidence is
bound to leak away somewhat. Minister Carmichael
seems to have based all his estimates on English
figures and ideas, and then decided to be a good
deal more drastic than the British Government ever
dreamed of being. Yet wages are much higher here
than in Britain —the wages of the men who dig out
the coal, and drive it about in carts, and load it on
ships or trains, and drive these ships and trains, and
shovel the mineral into the gas company’s furnaces,
and lay pipes to carry the gas, and make these
pipes, and dig out the iron to make the pipes. And
interest is higher here, consequently money is ex-
pected to yield a better return ; if it were not so, the
Government of which Carmichael is an ornament,
wouldn’t be borrowing with difficulty at 4 p.c.
Again, in rushing headlong at things, a "most material
point was overlooked. There are in many lines, and
there may be in the gas business also, companies in
which large reserves have been transferred to capital;
thus, the shareholders might have put up £50,000
and the nominal capital be £lOO,OOO. There are
others, in which shares have been sold at very high
premiums, and the premiums carried to reserve
fund ; in other words, the shareholders may have put
up £lOO,OOO, and yet the nominal capital be
£50,000. And there are concerns in which the
nominal capital is just what the shareholders put
up. If three such companies were limited equally
to a 4 p.c. dividend on nominal capital, the first lot
would really get 8 p.c. on the money it put up ; the
second would receive 2 p.c. ; the third 4 p.c. The
Government banged into the business apparently with-
out the slightest regard for these possible intri-
cacies of finance. If all reserves were capitalised,
and all companies started from scratch under new
and sensible regulations, the position would be at
least more equitable.

This company supplies both gas and electric light,
though it supplies the latter in strict moderation,
and there is always a lurking suspicion that it isn’t
particularly interested in showing profits from that
source. It has had the field to itself hitherto, having
escaped the woeful experience of the Launceston co.,
which was bumped by a cheaply-produced municipal
supply of electric current. The difference is shown
in the dividends paid—Hobart, 8f p.c. approx. ;
Launceston, 4 p.c. How long the Hobart co.’s better
luck is to continue it would be hard to say. Cer-
tainly, a municipal supply doesn’t threaten it, but
the people who have secured the water power from
the Great Lake will be in the field presently, and
they are people of considerable tonnage in Hobart—
“ Jam” Jones and others. Probably “cracking hardy,”
this co. remarks, “Owing to the continued and very
satisfactory increase in the business, it is necessarv
to instal additional plant forthwith,” and the direc-
tors propose to borrow up to £20,000 for the purpose.
When they have to go to the local Cohen for such
a comparatively small matter as that, it suggests
that they have not been hanging on to much of the
profit. Seven years ago there was a credit balance
of £603 at the bank. Now there is an overdraft of
£5948. True, there have been some additions to
the plant. According to the balance-sheet, premises,
plant, etc., used in the gas business, have increased
in value from £88,205 to £97,884. In the same
seven-year period gas sales for the half-year have
grown from £11,312 to £15,642. The increase in
business is thus considerably bigger than the increase
in the book value of plant, etc. ; so that some ad-
ditions may have been made out of profits and the
cost written off against depreciation. Still, the
figures are not striking, and there has probably been
very little undisclosed profit stowed away. Which
means that even under recent very favorable condi-
tions the co. has been paying away pretty well all it
has earned.

The co. has evidently decided to cast aside the 12 p.c.
until it can really afford it. Meantime, it divides
another 10 p.c., but even this appears too much, as,
after allowing £5O for depreciation, only £lB is left
to be carried to reserves. Certainly the position is
better than it was a few years back, when the
reserves were constantly being drawn upon to pay
the div. Profits are also moving in the wrong direc-
tion, the £2168 for the six months being the lowest
for a good many years, and only slightly over 10 p.c.
on the capital. Gas sales are dropping in a very
decided manner. The revenue from that source this
half-year shows £5150, as compared with £5485 at
Dec., 1911 (a drop of £335), and £5446 at June
1911 (a drop of £296 for the corresponding half-year).
This gives another view of the case: —
June, Dec., June,
1905. 1911. 1912.
Liquid assets .. £9OlB £6274 £5074

Surplus.. .. £BB3l £4787 £3937
So, in its effort to pay the excessive dividend, and
provide for renewals and additions, the co. has drawn
on its liquid assets to the extent of £4894 in seven
years. And the additions, etc., don’t seem to have
been very profitable, either, judging from the con-
tinued decrease in gas sales. It is a very poor show-
ing, and the safest remedy is for the shareholders to
forego more of their dividends for a couple of vears.

The co.’s capital is £lO,OOO, so the half-year’s profit

make shareholders feel more cheerful than consumers.

So the consumers have added £6748 to the surplus
of liquid assets, besides paying the shareholders an
annual 15 p.c. for a good many years, and have paid
for all renewals, repairs and additions, on which, of
course, they will continue to pay a dividend of 15 or
so p.c., just as if the# shareholders had bought the
new works with their own money. It is a glorious
arrangement for the shareholders, but it looks as
though it won’t last much longer. Last week Goul-
burn decided, by vote, to authorise the council to
borrow £13,000 for electric lighting purposes, and
when a municipal electric lighting station is opened,
this co. is bound to lose some of its custom.

Thus does the bloated Protected Australian manufac-
turer, of whose machinations the semi-Freetrade
Labor party is so alarmed, pile up great wealth and
lead the riotous life in his marble halls. It is 10
years since this company saw a dividend, and then
it got a wretched 2 b p.c. In the interim it has
written off one-half its capital, so its present divi-
dend is only in reality 1.) p.c. And when 1 j p.c.
happens once in 10 years it is really half a crown
per £lOO per annum. And when that half-crown
per £lOO per annum has to be applied to cover
loss of capital, it becomes a great deal less than
nothing per cent, per annum. The way of the Aus-
tralian manufacturing co. is hard. Labor people,
who will boycott an Australian co. if it pays less
than £3 a week for 44 hours toil, will cheerfully
buy the goods of an English concern, which pays
255. a week for 60 hours. Labor people who loathe
sweating in Australia will cheerfully buy the worst
variety of sweated goods provided they come from
abroad. Enthusiastic Australian unionists will buy
non-union goods if assured that they arrive here on
a foreign-owned ship, manned by niggers. So things
are cold and bleak for the Australian white labor
and union-wage factory owner. It is especially so in
Queensland, where Harry Coyne and his general
strikers hailed a mob of twopenny Lascar seamen as
brothers, because they struck work and marched to
the Trades Hall, and where the Labor paper declared
that Socialism obliterated the color line. Taking one
thing with another, the Queensland Woollen Manufac-
turing Co. hoes a hard row. Things look a little
better with it than they did. Still, it struggles
with an overdraft of more than £16,000, and it pays a
dividend that, if such a dividend were paid by the
importing middleman, would be regarded as sheer
pauperism.

The Marshall Shoe Co., Ltd. (Melb.),
was formed last year to carry on a manufacturing
and distributing business, established about four years
earlier. The co. began with a goodwill asset of
£7500, but this deficit was reduced by a renewal
reserve (£4320) and a general reserve for contin-
gencies (£100(p, leaving £2lBO of paper, plus
£892 of preliminary expenses, or total paper assets
of £3072, to wipe out: —
Towards
Profits. Dividends. Reserves. Deficit.
July, 1911.. -- „ £3072
Reserves.
June, 1912.. £15,029 8 p.c. =£6367 £8662 5590
The co. now issues its first statement of accounts. The
balance-sheet shows a renewal reserve of £4320, and
a general reserve of £1000; it can only be assumed
that these funds were in existence when the company
was formed. Anyway, there is nothing to suggest
that they are appropriations out of the current year s
revenue. On tlie contrary, certain appropriations are
now expressly made, bringing the renewal fund to
£lO,OOO, and the other fund to £l5OO. The capital
to start with was apparently £84,522. Subsequently
£20,320 more was issued, making the total at the
end of the year £104,842, in 24,000 8 p.c. cumula-
tive prefs. and 97,500 ordinaries. All these shares
are £1 each, but an aggregate of £16,658 is unpaid
upon them. The vendors took 42,403 shares in pay-
ment for the business. Goodwill was included, being
valued at £7500. In the foregoing this intangible
commodity is not regarded as an asset, and the
management itself very properly proceeded to get rid
of it by writing off £5OO. 'The co. has got away
off the mark in rare style. The profit shown above
is £15,029, out of which £5680 is earmarked for
the renewal fund. It does not transpire whether any-
thing is written off against depreciation. It is quite
poss ble, therefore, that this £5680 should be de-
ducted from the profits, assuming that that is a fail-
amount to cover the 12 months’ wear and tear of
plant, etc. Even with this deduction, the remaining
profit would be equal to about 11 p.c. on what
appears to be the average capital held during the
year—a very satisfactory figure, when it is remem-
bered that some of the money raised for the pur-
pose of purchasing new plant must have been unpro-
ductive for some time. Plant is valued''at £31,128.
Most shoe factories are more or less under the heel
of the Shoe Trust, which hires out its machines the
Marshall people own their plant absolutely, and
consequently have no unending royalties ahead of
them. The other principal assets are freeholds
£11,344, stock £62,090 and book debts £33,049;
on the other side, liabilities total £25,648. The
co. is very progressive, and its business seems to be
growing rapidlv ; in view of that its financial position
is very fair. The bulk of the liabilities are made up
of sundry trade creditors.

Like most building societies this one derives its
supplies from two principal sources, shareholders'
capita] and deposits, secured in this case by deben-
tures. The capital is withdrawable ir. certain cir-
cumstances, but, under the Maoriland law, the
deposits are regulated by the amount of the share
capital, so that any great reduction of the latter
must be followed by a repayment of deposits. The
paid capital in this case is £124,322, while the
deposits amount to £69,581, which is barely more
than half the total allowed by law. The funds are
represented mainly by advances on mortgages
£200,425, and there is not a foreclosed property on
the books. From this it looks as though the credi-
tors need not lose any sleep over their risks. The
steady growth of the concern is further evident by
the following detailed figures:—-
Ad- Properties
Capital. Debentures. vances. in hand.

The weak feature of the business is the almost con-
temptible total of reserves. For some weird reason
or other the rules of the society lay it down that
profits shall not be accumulated beyond 1 p.c. of the
amount of the advances on mortgage. This altogether
inadequate provision against a slump in the value of
securities means that, in the event of such a slump,
shareholders’ capital must pay the piper, although the
advances on which the losses are made may have
been sanctioned years previously, and, therefore,
before the existing shares were created.

The Australian Drug Co. (Sydney) :
Re-
June, Profits. Dividends. Toßeserves. serves.
1911 .. £4766 5 p.c. =£3750 £lOl6 £10,252
1912 „ 3419 5 „ „ 3750 9,921
This is a disappointment. Two years or so back
city men in Sydney told one another that Adam
Forsyth had got hold of another gold-lined thing.
This business was It. It had a capital of only
£lO,OOO, and was over-burdened with debt. The
new company put the capital up to £75,000, and
proceeded to wipe out the liabilities and prepare for
new factories and premises. The profits for the first
year were affected by the existence of the old debt,
which couldn’t all be got rid of at once. Altogether
£2484 was paid in interest on debentures, which
were eventually wiped out by the issue of the new
shares, and if that amount is added to the first year’s
profit, as shown above, the total rises to £7250.
It was pretty confidently expected that the next
year would show a profit big enough to allow of a
10 p.c. dividend. Then the accidents began to
happen. Strikes in Sydney and Brisbane interfered
with the inter-State trade; the building boom in
Sydney resulted in a delay of the eo.’s building
operations; and while the company was shifting
itself, business was disorganised, though it is alleged
that none of the connection was lost. Thus, between
one thing and another, the directors are not able to
show enough profit to pay even 5 p.c.; nevertheless,
they chop into reserves, and make that distribution.
The most cheerful feature from the shareholders’ point
of view will, no doubt, be the directors’ assurance
that tliey don’t think any less of the business and its
golden prospects than they did in the beg.lining.
As to the reserves mentioned above, the reconstructed
company apparently began with £.4374. The co.
was, it seems, floated privately, and the men who
took it in hand made a bargain with the vendors
that the whole of the brokerage thus saved should
be put by them to the credit of the company. This
cave the reserves another whoop of £4861. The
balance of the reserve consists of undivided profit.
As at June 30, liabilities totalled £48,582, includin'.
£25,000 of 5 p.c. debentures; the assets consist
of freehold property £19,307, stock £70,144,
sundry debtors £45,450, and a few minor items —
£2251 in the aggregate. “Sundry debtors” looks a
big item, especially alongside the diminutive profits.
Whether it has increased or not during the year, this
paper can’t say, because nreviously stocks and sundri-
debt were lumped with good old “etc.” The
directors practically ask that judgment shall be
suspended until things settle down into an even
stride, and The Bulletin is prepared to leave it at
that.

It is just 10 years since the company cleaned up the
wreckage which remained after Queensland’s previous
great borrowing boom had burst. The capital had
been £154,262, in shares of 255., most of them paid
to £l. Ten shillings was written off and a call of
2s. 6d. made. The capital was thus left _at a trifle
over £77,000, at which figure it has since stood.
This writing-off allowed of a big cut in the book
value of freeholds, including, no doubt, pub pro-
perties. Since that cleaning up the management has
pursued a modest policy in the matter of dividends,
and has thus gradually improved what may be
roughly called its cash position. The following
shows that aspect■

Thus, in 10 years the co. has made a change of
£39,575 in its outward appearance. To some ex-
tent this may, no doubt, be due to the realisation
of non-liquid assets, such as premises and hotels;
but it does not appear that a very great deal has been
done in that respect. The next table certainly shows
some reduction in both items during the last three
years, but part of it is probably due to the writing
down of these assets: —-

A very fine increase of profits is shown for the past
12 months. Looking at the last table it will be seen
that expenses have fallen very considerably, and that
isn’t a feature presented by many recent accounts.
The chances are that the reduction in this case is
dup to a smaller amount having been provided to
meet depreciation and similar standing charges on
account of the jump in Queensland property values.
It would be more comforting if that jump were not
due mainly to a borrowing boom. For the rest, a
beer war which had raged in Brisbane has been
ended, and profits have no doubt improved in con-
sequence. The least satisfactory feature of the ac-
counts is the lumping of goodwill with freeholds,
etc. This casts an aspersion on the reserves which,
in consequence, are branded as nominal in the table
with which these remarks began.

A spring in profits from £2307 in 1905 to £11,305
in 1912 looks very fine and large, but there is this
to be taken into account—in the former year the
capital was £16,025, whereas now it is £45,000.
Like most other things in Queensland, this co. had
a bad time —so bad, in fact, that when it put a wet
towel round its aching head and considered its assets,
it couldn’t see anything for it but to write down
its £1 shares to 4s. 6d. and its 15s. shares to 9d.
If it had deferred the painful job a little while
longer it might have thought that even that wasn’t
enough. Then Queensland put its nose to the
plough, and eschewed its old extravagant habits,
while Nature worked overtime at the task of restor-
ing the State’s prosperity. And there are few
countries in the world where Nature is such a
strenuous workwoman. To add to its chance this
co. got into better business hands, and so things
soon started to mend. Unfortunately for the look
of the column devoted to profits, a beer war raged
in Brisbane in 1910-11, and spoiled the look of the
figures for that year; but things are all right again,
and a profit is shown which, were it all available,
would be sufficient to pay a dividend of about 25
p.c. The wise old birds who now rule the roost
evidently don’t feel too sure that Queensland will
always have as much beer-money as it has got at
present, consequently they put by £2846 to pro-
vide for contingencies. This is shown as written
off, it being th.e company’s habit to swallow up
amounts put to this fund, presumably using them
to 1 write down assets. The next small table shows
a few other details:—

along with the fact that the liabilities amount to
£26,000, the directors have now decided to create
another 100,000 shares of 4s. fid. each, two-thirds of
which it is proposed to issue forthwith at par to
holders of present shares. This will make the paid
capital £60,000, and there will be still 33,334
shares in reserve. The figures justify the issue.

WATER LEVELS.—Many painful accidents have
occurred through fracture of water gauge glasses on.
steam boilers, and various devices to prevent injury
to boiler attendants have been adopted. These,
however, are more or less objectionable, because
the clear view of the water-level is thereby ob-
scured. At last a practically unbreakable Gauge
Glass has been invented,_ namely, “BISHOP’S ADA-
MANT,” which will resist considerable pressure as
well as high and varying temperature. To be ob-
tained from Bell’s Asbestos, Ltd., Engineers’ Factors,
315 Kent Street, Sydney.*
* * . *
A simple and continuous Vacuum Slimes Filter is
the “Wilfley.” It consists of a revolving drum,
which is partly submerged in the material to be
filtered. The drum, covered with filter-cloth, has a
vacuum pipe passing through it, by means of which
liquid and air are withdrawn by a vacuum pump.-
The filtered slime is removed by a simplet apparatus,
placed at or below the level of the pulp. Directly
the cake is removed the filter-cloth is re-immersed,
ready to receive a fresh cake. The residual liquid in
the slime cake is removed by distributing water over
the surface of the semi-dried cake. Owing to the
fact that the filter is circular in form the work 'is
being done continuously during the whole revolution
of the drum. The Austral Otis Eng. Co., Ltd., of
Melbourne, have the sole manufacturing rights of
this machine.* [2

* * -lb «
Melbourne Cope’s Creek, which, in the
latter half of 1911, paid two divs., amounting: to
£l5OO, on its capital of £12,500, was struck by the
prevailing drought in the first half of the current
year, and dividends wilted to nothing. After the
half-year ended the rains came, and there is no more
trouble about water. A more permanent trouble arose
from the depletion of the co.’s old ground, but a
scrap here and a scrap there have been secured, and
the eo.’s life has been thus prolonged. Future re-
sults are, however, a gamble, and the management
speaks only in the most general terms regarding the
new ground. For instance, “a lease of 16 acres is
being acquired. . .the greater part of which should
prove payable.” The co.’s finances are still fairly
solid. The surplus of liquid assets runs to about
£'2ooo.
If there is a slight expansion in the Cas-
silis (Vic.) bank overdraft, there is a compression
in the directors’ report. The drought is responsible
for this, as it cut off both power and profit, and
accordingly gave V. J. Saddler a chance to ease up
his proneness to adjectival language. What the
Cassilis needed first and last was development and
good recoveries from the accumulated heaps of resi-
dues. With the hydro-electric power works hung
up through dry weather, connections between the
six and seven and the seven and eight levels have
been improved. Odds and ends of jobs have been
carried out, with the result that cheaper work is
ahead, and there is a chance of the old mine once
again getting its nose above the sludge. Lilburne is
wrestling with the problem of the residues, and
figures that fine grinding, with filter press cyanide
work, will run rings round roasting and chlorina-
tion methods, over which more than one expert got
into a horrible tangle. Well, anyone who throws
himself into Cassilis must have a big heart. Suc-
cess at the Cassilis means heaps to an out-of-the-way
corner of Gippsland, where mining has had to be
forced like a hot-house plant. Had the eo. the cash
it could help a dozen promising but starving little
cos. in the surrounding hills, now that it has cheap
power and has solved the metallurgical problem.
Without cash the road must be rocky. The biggest
boulder to shift is the £5OOO overdraft.
* * -X- *
South Langi Logan was the last of the
Ararat (Vic.) field to stir up. All because its shaft
hit on to wash. It is always nice to do this, pro-
vided the wash doesn’t burst up the shaft and smash
things generally, as was the case with the old Langi
Logan. A heap more precautions are taken nowa-
days about such trifles. The gold that was got
doesn’t mean anything to the co., for the shaft is too
far from the deep ground. It would take a Madame
Berry to stir enthusiasm over anything of the sort
after the smelly experience of other strikes of the
kind. More solid material is wanted to buttress the
market than a patch of wash in a shaft hundreds of
feet away from where the lead lies. Investors ought
not to be lead into drawing wrong deductions, and
the cold facts are, therefore, put at their disposal.
After a week of solid thought and a fort-
night of hard cogitation, a problem remains un-
solved over the results obtained from the Cathcart
(Vic.) clean-up of 61foz. from 35 fathoms, and the
next of from 17 fathoms. The first wash-up
called forth an explicit statement that the dirt “came
from different narrow streaks passed through....
making it hard to judge its value.” Still, the beam
went over 20z., which is good stuff. When, a week
later, the 6oz. nick was reached, there was no pub-
lication of reports. The weary man now asks the
office boy with the big diamond ring why this in-
difference. Is he up against the Wages Board or is
the Wages Board up against him? If so, why did
he send out word about the narrow streaks and let
the rich streaks sparkle unannounced ? If the office
boy isn’t out fishing or at the semi-final of a motor
race, he will help a dreary market by throwing out a
slab of news to illumine these points.
* ■* ■* *
Johnson’s Reef (Bendigo) is doing well
out of the stone, that is being, crushed at the No. 3
shaft on the Derby line. John Veale is sufficiently
sanguine to hold that he has four years’ work ahead
there. To think that the years of dreary call-making
could have been avoided if only the Board had had
the No. 3 shaft re-opened ! But what paltry work to
treat only 6000 or 7 000 tons a half-year! It is
cheery news to hear that some effort is" to be made
to get up to better figures. The strain on the Ben-
digo mine intellect of adding up whether it is
cheaper to yank dirt out or dawdle on in the leisurely
way to save the town, has been too great in the past
to be faced. But hard times are developing the
strength of the cerebellum. So, if No. 1 mine of the
Johnson’s yields- a quota of crushing dirt, and John
Veale has his way, he’ll rattle up his stamps to better
things. Strange to see Johnson’s shares, after having
been forfeited for unpaid calls, now putting up a
good dividend record.
Tougkah Compound (Siam) dredged
32,750 cub. yards during the first half of August for
32 tons Bewt. of tin oxide. The average is 2.2161 b.
of oxide, so it. is nearly double the average for the
half-year to March 31. Doubling the yield is just
as effective in increasing the profit as doubling'the
price would be. For the half-year to March 31 con-
centrates sold at £lO9 Per ton, but for April a yield
of 32 tons was officially valued at £3600, so it
seems fair to assume that the average price this half-
year will be £ll2 per ton. On April 18 the London
quotation for tin was £197 15s. Costs in April
amounted to £BOO. Allowing £lOOO for July, 52
tons of tin oxide would mean a surplus of £4824
After providing for the dividend of Is. paid in
August, there would be a substantial sum left. The
limited area and the comparatively low-grade wash-
dirt in portions of the compound somewhat limit
the scope of dredging operations, so a few good
yields must not be taken- as a -general average for
years ahead.
* * * *
Tongkali Harbor Tin Co. (Siam) is to
add a new dredge to its fleet,- making six altogether.
The yield for six months to March 31 was 794 tons
13cwt., but looking at the output of tin oxide, week
by week, for a long time past it cannot be expected
that the yield for the half-year ending in September
will equal the previous term. Still an effort will
doubtless be made to have a good clean up for Sep-
tember. It is doubtful if the monthly yield during
the current half-year will average much more than
90 tons, compared with 132 tons per month for the
first half of the fiscal year. During the latter
period no less than 1,647,634 cub. yards were dredsred
for 1,778,560 pounds of tin oxide. Such an enor-
mous quantity as this must have an exhaustive effect
on any submerged tin lead.
When last heard from, Mount Morgan
was smelting with four furnaces, and had managed
by means of an auxiliary up-cast to get rid of sul-
phur fumes. Details of the smelting for July show
that 32,130 tons of ore were smelted, comprising
19,989 tons from Mount Morgan (674 tons copper),
12,127 tons from Many Peaks (171 tons copper),
and 14 tons purchased ore (two tons copper) ; total,
847 tons copper. Mount Morgan smelter has a
capacity equal to, if not larger than, that of Mount
Lyell, which, however, smelts more ore per furnace
than Morgan. Pvritic smelting always shows to
advantage in this respect, for practically the whole
of the charge is counted as ore. At Morgan a very
large quantity of limestone is used, which is not the
case with Lyell. Many Peaks ore, in July, only aver-
aged 1.4 p.c. copper.
North Smithfield (Gympie, Queensland)
crushed 434 tons for £2563, and follows that up by
a much better return —300 tons for 8560 z. of
smelted gold (value about £3000). The slate coun-
try above 683 ft. carries a rich reef 12in. thick in
four back stopes.
Mount Elliott (Q.) shares lately ran up
to £8 55., bringing the total to over £1,200,000. It
would take an average profit of £2O On 60,000 tons
of copper to cover the market value, but there are
other considerations in boom times —for instance,
the chance of floating some of the leases recently
acquired into subsidiary cos. Ordinary brands of
copper averaged £63 for the 12 months ending June
30, during which period Mount Elliott produced
5286 tons of blister copper. It had also a large
stock of metal on hand at the end of the previous
year, so it has been realising on its goods under fav-
orable conditions. The present excitement is due to
the visit of Manager Corbould to London, the annual
report, and the numerous copper properties acquired.
The Hampden Consols, the Trafalgar arid the Sehvyn
appear to be developing on sound lines. At the Con-
sols the south level at 250 ft. is in 380 ft., with 7 p.c.
ore over a width of 4ft. Two prospecting shafts at
the Trafalgar are following a rich lode. The deepest
is down 79ft. Then there is the Native Companion
and King Solomon, Lady Fanny and Castle Mountain,
some of them options and some purchased for cash.
Mount Cuthbert (Q.) copper shares came
into the market with a swing when it was certain
that the railway from Cloncurry was to be pro-
ceeded with. The first section takes the line 42
miles to the northern bank of the Dugald River.
Distance from that point to Mount Cuthbert is, ap-
proximately, 33 miles. .Perhaps the construction of
the first section will bring Mount Cuthbert deben-
tures into better favor, and supply the co. with cash
to sink deeper below the 350 ft. level, which has been
extended more than 500 ft. to the south —following
the eastern wall of the formation—on a strong quartz
lode. The 150 ft. and 250 ft. levels contain the best
ore; but, unlike most of the Cloncurry mines,
Cuthbert has very little ore rich enough to ship to
the smelting works. The reserves are estimated at
141,400 tons, containing 12,632 tons of copper and
£15,120 worth of gold.
* * * *
The Vulcan (Q.) recent threepenny divi-
dend brings the total to £3 3s. Bd. per share, which,
calculated to the tune of 264,000 shares, gives some
idea of the past deeds of the patient old warrior.
For July, 643 tons of ore found daylight, which pro-
duced 21 tons 12cwt. of tin concentrates, equal to
£2048 odd. The ore at 1050 ft. is patchy, with some
good stuff, but most of it on the low-grade side. The
development of the 750 ft. level compensates for it
significantly. There No. 3 winze has been sunk a
full depth of 35ft., all on payable ore, while a 10ft.
rise has the same fortunate complaint.
* * * *
Brilliant Deeps (Charters Towers) has
five additional stampers employed, and the crushing
now going through is said to be looking' well. The
reef in No. 5 level, after pinching to a few inches,
has widened again to 3ft., but the unofficial report
doesn’t say whether the stone is in two sections or
in a solid reef. Charters Towers has a habit of
simply adding the width of a vein on the hanging-
wall to that on the footwall, though there is often
a quantity of barren formation between the two.
The warden’s report is very optimistic about B.
Deeps. It states the height and length of payable
stone proved as 300 ft. each way, and the width of the
reef is averaged at 4ft. On this basis it is calcu-
lated that there are 26,800 tons of stone, containing
£199,325 (£7 7s. 3d. per ton). Against that there
are 150,000 shares, valued in the market at
£225,000 (30s. per share). One has to look back
a very long time to find a Charters Towers reef
crushing 26,000 tons for an average of anything
like £7 per ton. The average of the best mines
near the Deeps is about £4 per ton. B. Block has
crushed a total of 163,163 tons, value £538,922;
N. B. Freehold, 86,073 tons, worth £43,615. These
two mines are neighbors of the Deeps. Total divi-
dends: B. Block, £81,250; Freehold, £116,250;
Mills’ United, £637,500.
* * * *
Brilliant P.C. (Charters Towers) con-
tinues to raise good-looking mineral stone from the
bottom of the . old winze, 200 and odd feet east of
the Freehold’s boundary. The trial lot put through
when the stone was first struck averaged over loz.
per ton. The Stockholm mine, which belongs to the
Brilliant P.C., is crushing an average of between
£ 4 and £ 5 per ton. The last monthly lot was
667 tons for £3OOO 10s.
* * * *
Brilliant St. George (Charters Towers)
crushed 51 tons for an average of £9 11s. 9d. per
ton. This was from close to Kelly’s Queen Block
boundary. Kelly’s itself crushed 301 tons for an
average of £2 7s. 4d. per ton, which means that the
co. will have to make a call, for Kelly’s has a lot
of development in hand on the St. George reef. This
reef may, perhaps, redeem its fortunes. In olden days
some rich shoots were worked on the St. George, in
the ground adjoining Kelly’s.
* * * *
Queen Bee copper (near'Cobar, N.S.W.)
is smelting on a small scale, which generally proves
expensive. The most encouraging prospects in the
mine are in No. 5 south level, which is in 336 ft.
from the crosscut, and has a face of ore the full
width of the drive worth 4 p.c. Much better ore
was obtained from the winze below this level, to-
wards which No. 6 level has advanced within a few
feet. The output of copper is very small for a big
co.
* * * *
Kyloe copper (N.S.W.), for the year to
June 29, raised 17,650 tons of ore, assaying 5.77 p.c.
copper, which compares rather poorly with 20,677
tons averaging 8.4 p.c. taken out of the mine for
the 12 months ending June. 1911. Still Kyloe, after
allowing £3984 for depreciation, came through with
a profit of £1489, and with the money in hand from
the previous year is able to carry forward a credit
balance of. £8543. Diminishing ore reserves, and
the necessity for more development, stand in the
way of a dividend ; but it is some encouragement to
know that Kyloe is shipping high-grade copper con-
centrates.
* * * *
The lodide Mineral Hill leases (N.S.W.)
have been reported on by J. H. Fawcett in favor-
able terms, and a co. with a capital of £150.000,
in £lO shares, is to be floated in Sydney. There
will be 85Q0 contributing shares, 5300 fully-paid,
and 1200 partly-paid. The ore reserves are estimated
at 12,500 tons, containing approximately £8 worth
of metals (total £100,000), all of which is above
the 60ft. level and within an area measuring 150 ft.
long by 30ft. wide. The bulk of the ore is below
shipping grade, for the total mining and metallur-
gical costs run to £8 per ton. Such high costs are
not to be wondered at, for this claim is 42 miles
from Condobolin railway station and 335 miles from
Sydney. As £8 per ton is equal to the value of a
ton of 60 p.c. lead ore with the base metal at £2O,
the ore will have to be rich in precious metals to
pay for shipment to Cockle Creek. Lead carbonate
ore of similar grade to that shipped by Block 14
(Broken Hill) could not stand costs at £8 per
ton. This show, as it stands, may be a sound
enough proposition, but its future depends very con-
siderably on an extension of the railway in its
direction.
Occidental Gold, outside Oobar (N.S.W.),
reports for its half-year a further loss of £2566, but
expects to stop the rot when the new plant is in full
swing. This is nearing completion. The ore crushed
was 21,834 tons; receipts totalled £24,043, against
expenses, £26,609 (including depreciation, £1055).
Building operations hampered the regular work, but
during the last month of the half-year the two tube
mills got going, and satisfactory results are claimed
to have been obtained. The extraction from the
slimes is said to have jumped up to 87 p.c. The sand
plant is practically closed down. The power plant
has been largely increased, electric motors being used
for the mill. The cash position has naturally suf-
fered, but there still remains £12,422 to go on with.
Liabilities are a shade more bulky, with £12,719.
Ore reserves are estimated to keep the mill going 18
months. A winze, 30ft. below the bottom level (No.
5), shows ore going 2joz. per ton, but this is a
spasm that is not likely to continue long. The
troublesome copper is more likely to increase than the
gold, but this is not mentioned.
* * * *
Payne’s Find (W.A.) is turning out just
as pars appearing on this page indicated it would.
The best returns from small parcels during the re-
cent run of the State mill ranged from an ounce
downwards, which was much less than local boom-
sters reckoned, and as no doubt all the ore treated
was picked, the next batch of crushings will prob-
bably average less. Rich patches certainly exist on
the field, but once again let it be said that indica-
tions do not point to permanency of values at
depth.
* * # *
It was said on this page some time ago
that the posit on of the Associated (W.A.) was
decayed, and official figures just to hand prove that
it is so. The other day it was announced that the
probable ore reserve was 500,000 tons, ranging in
value from 21s. to 295. Since then the annual
statement of accounts has come along,, and this
shows last year’s cut-fine costs, including develop-
ment, to be 275. As someone remarked at the meet-
ing, for all the good shareholders get out of it, or
for all the good they are ever likely to get out of it,
operations might just as well have been suspended,
and the co. wound up. It is obvious on the figures
quoted that the outlook for profitable mining is
hopeless, but with a faith worthy of a better cause
the management struggles on.
