Attempts are being made to boom Chaf-
fers’ (W.A.), on the off-chance of the Horseshoe No.
2 shoot ultimately getting there. “Over three months
ago,” remarks a local paper, “the Horseshoe cabled
that the lode in question was sft. wide and worth
14dwt. at 150 ft. from the boundary.” Suspicion is
then aroused by the additional remark that the
scripholder who can hold, and doesn’t, is mad.
Nothing is said about poor ore having been met with
at about 100 ft. from the dividing line. Which reminds
The Bulletin that one of the worst features of
Westralian gold mining is the press.
■' * * *
“Oof Bird” (W.A.) : It makes me sick
to see the_ way in which, day after day, bad mines
are proclaimed good, rogues praised, and lies sown
broadcast about new finds in this country. Bull-
finch was responsible for a good deal of this sort
of thing and now Kurnalpi is gett ; ng attention. “A
second Kalgoorlie” is one tale. “A new creator of
riches” is another. “Bound to turn out well at
depth” is a third, and there are others equally
absurd and misleading. One would-be oracle says
that. the Government ought to do some diamond
drilling. Boring is all very well in cases where a
search is being made in an established mine, for
the continuation of a known ore body in deeper
ground or for parallel bodies, but few persons out-
side a lunatic asylum would advocate putting an inch
hole down here and there on a new field. Looking
for the needle in the haystack would be a mere cir-
cumstance to hunting for values in this way. If
any of the Kurnalpi shepherds have any faith in the
value of their holdings, let them show it by getting
to work.
* * * *
Mount Bischoff (Tas.) shares, influenced,
no doubt, by the,-60s. paid in dividends in six months,
were skied to £6O. The market is now easier at
£52, because wages are rising, and last half-year’s
costs (4s. 7id.) are pretty certain to be exceeded
during the current term. As w 7 ill be noted from the
monthly mill returns, it often takes 20,000 tons of
crushing stuff to run out 100 tons of clean concen-
trates (66 p.c. to 67 p.c. metal). A rise of 6d. per
ton in costs makes a difference of (roughly) £5OO
per month, and the directors will not be slow to fall
on that excuse to reduce dividends, which, in the
face of a lot of necessary expenditure on plant, have
been dangerously near the net earnings. Last half-
year the net earnings were £42,856 and the divi-
dends £36,000, and the output of tin oxide from
the mine 600 tons; so net profit is at the rate of
£7l, and dividends at £6O per ton of black tin
oxide.
* * * *
Last year (1911) Wallaroo and Moonta
(S.A.) produced 6079 tons of copper, at an average
cost of £5l 12s. sd. Those conditions figure re-
markably well with copper at its present price, but
it is unlikely that costs will be so low this year, for
wages have a rising tendency, and this co. isn’t pro-
ducing so much copper precipitate from surface re-
sidues as it used to do. The old tailings have been
heavily drawn upon. In September, 1911, Wallaroo
and Moonta paid a dividend of Is., but for the quar-
ter ending September, 1912, it pays 2s. No doubt
speculators expect an increase in the dividend if the
high price of copper is sustained, but the co. has
had to do more development to open up deeper levels.
This old concern doesn’t advertise its month’s output
and value like Mount Morgan, and it has, throughout
its long life, had very little to say about ore re-
serves. It has paid £2,119,250.
* IT * *
“Diaranu” : Still one more valuable dis-
covery of natural wealth has been made in Papua.
Early in September, 1911, a find of oil was
made on the Vailala River by Lett and Thomas,
resident planters on the river. The first indication
was found near the village of Opa, some few
miles from the mouth of the river, in the form
of several mud and inflammable gas springs. The
first find was followed up, and resulted in the
discovery of further springs some 30 miles up the
river, not far from a village known as Akauda.
The prospectors took a few samples of the mixture,
and hurried to Melbourne to obtain any reward or
prospecting claim that they might be able to get
the Australian Government to hand over. Meanwhile
the Government of the Territory has requisitioned
the services of J. E. Came, Assistant Government
Geologist of N.S.W., who has reported on the dis-
covery as follows: —“Getting the natives to point
out any neighboring rock outcrops, we followed down
an adjacent creek to near its junction with the river
(Vailala), a few chains from which, where gas
bubbles were seen rising through the water, I was
fortunate in locating crude petroleum oil seeping
from the rocks in the creek-bed. With a tomahawk
and a small geological hammer we succeeded in
loosening sufficient of the bed, rock (sandstone and
mudstone) to enable the included oil to rise to the
surface of the water, sometimes in a continuous
stream of globules, and by skimming the surface
to nil several small bottles with crude oil. As the
rocks are fairly dense, and in the ease of sandstone,
hard, the oil must be under considerable pressure
to force it to the surface through fine joint and
cleavage planes. These rocks strike N. 70 degrees
k , and S. 70 degrees W., and dip N. 20 degrees
W. at 26 degrees. Following this strike across the
river two days later, I was again fortunate in finding-
crude oil seeping from ironstone in a creek bank
a short distance from the river, where no defined gas
emanations were observable.” Manv claims have
been pegged around the sites, and the Government
has published the terms under which prospecting
can be done. Applicants are permitted to mark out
a block of 300 acres, which they have exclusively
for two years, at the end of which time at least
400 ft. of boring must be - completed. They must
commence work within six months of the granting
of their application. Should payable oil be struck,
applicants may apply for a lease of one or more
blocks not exceeding five acres, leases to be given
for terms not exceeding 20 years. The Government
is to receive a royalty of 5 p.c. of the net profits,
and will have the right to purchase the output of
the bores at current market rates. As a source
from which to draw supplies of oil-fuel for the
Australian Navy, the discovery has value, because
the wells would be right on the coast, and the diffi-
culties of loading would be almost nil. With an
inexhaustible supply of good oil, Papua should rapid! v
come to the front, and even the machinations of
the Standard Oil Trust should not harm it <tf the
Australian Government see that proper restrictions
are made to prevent any dummying of the leases
Given good management and sensible development,
there is no reason why Papua should not have as
good wells as those at Batoum, on the Black Sea.
At the same time, this scribe would not recommend
people to put much money into the venture until
the find has been further proved by boring. The
country is covered with dense tropical jungle, so
the work of projecting will be slow, but even the
thickest* scrub will yield up its secrets to the axe.
The great advance made in the construction of In-
ternal Combustion Engines has now been further
demonstrated by the fact of Petrol, Benzine, Kero-
sene and heavy Oil Locomotives being brought into
daily and successful commercial use. The machines
are thoroughly reliable, and are cheaper in very
many insta.nces than steam or electric locomotives
Messrs. Ironside, Son and Dyckerhoff, of London],
have supplied a great number of their famous “New
Century” locomotives nearly all over the world, and
their machines are even working continuously and
safely in “gassy” coal mines. W. and J. Lempriere,
Melbourne, will be pleased to supply further par-
ticulars. *

WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, Septembee 2, 1912.
My Dear Aeinx, —
In the garden at Admiralty House, white
azaleas held up their thirsty mouths to the
heavy rainfall the other afternoon. Gor-
geous red geraniums—you will have noticed
that the geranium is in fashion —winked
their white eyelids under a large green um-
brella of mere shrub, being sun-lovers who
go to bits in the frequent torrents of August.
The Women’s Handicrafts Exhibition lurked
high and dry inside, and the wet end of the
show was not overflowing with the ardent
buyer. The Admiralty lady offered her
rooms for the show, in the goodness of her
heart and the inexperience of her commercial
outlook. Kirribilli Point is too far from the
city. The madding crowd likes to hear its
hoof ring on the pavement.
There is a bleak and aching void between
a garden party at Admiralty House, at
which Society (uncommonly careful of tak-
ing an eighteenpenny cab) spends nothing,
and a show of work at the same address,
which expects Society to part with its not-
too-ready cash. In town, the plain citizen
would have turned in to look at, and cer-
tainly buy, many of the exhibits. The butler
was not for sale. I fancy his price must be
above pearl ornaments. At four or so each
afternoon some junior understudy sent a
mystic message concerning tea. But the
hospitable cares of the slender Admiralty
lady were mostly cast, like plum cake, on
the face of the waters. On Thursday after-
noon the wind scampered round the chimney
pots, and the rain blotted out the scenery.
The dense mass of persons who were absent
from a good but too remote show ought, to
be a dreadful warning to the next eruption
of the Women’s Handicrafts Society.
As I write (Monday) Mrs. James Ashton,
Mrs. Garland and Miss C. Stephen are sit-
ting with enthusiasm on a meeting in
Paling’s concert hall. They hope to hatch
a benefit concert for the Female School of
Industry. This ferocious name for a meek,
long-suffering charity has come down from
the bad old days of 1826, and has the crack
of a whip in its tail. In the era when Gov-
ernor Darling’s wife founded the school,
“for the maintenance and training of female
children of poor parents in general domestic
duties,” female charity infants wore hor-
rible garments of melancholy brown wincey,
and heavy clogs on their feet. They dragged
their juvenile locks severely off their fore-
heads, at the age of from five to nine years,
and knew the Shorter Catechism backwards
and sideways and upside down. I under-
stand they are still examined in Scripture,
but modern domestic science takes the place
of the old rule of thumb food-boiling. Fe-
male infants remain under the eye of the
matron till they climb the steep ascent of 16
years. They are then ready for the battle
of the Boast and Boiled. The institution is
toiling along the stony track of Impecuni-
osity, and wants help.
At last year’s meeting of the Shipwreck
Belief Society, in the local Town Hall,
heroic adults were almost knocked out by
babies in knickerbockers. The dogged de-
termination of these infants to save the lives
of persons whose age and size gave them a
presumptive right to spank their rescuers
(for interfering with the business arrange-
ments of Fate) was only equalled by their
Sandow muscles. This year’s medal rally
was less remarkable than the 1911 upheaval
of young children in w T atery places. Only one
infant of six years (Albert Sommerlad) got
away with a silver medal. Chief Justice Cullen
trimmed the balance-sheet with the usual re-
marks, and Premier McGowen offered some
suitable prawn and salt-water observations.
St. Vincent’s Hospital ball will occur in
the Town Hall on September 10. The de-
coration committee is wrinkling its fore-
head up into its front hair in search of new
and extensive ideas in floral trimming. Mrs.
J. C. Willliamson left a trail of good works
behind her, to be hung out, as gal-
lery attractions, by Mr. Major and other
local upholders of the J.C.W. firm. Tickets
at 10s. 6d. (there are no champagne tops
to this good old-fashioned charity hop) are
reported to be selling like the sovereigns
which were put on the market at 6d. each.
When Wattle Day broke into bud about
three years ago, in that learned tomb of
archaic parchments, the Boyal Society’s
rooms, two objections stood up after the
manner of a kangaroo with its back to a
tree. One was that people would get into
the habit of tearing the baby blossom out of
its cradle. The second was that the wattle
could not stand for all Australia, as it only
shakes its yellow head in certain parts of
the eastern States. But botanist Maiden
wiped his scientific boots on the notion that
Australia’s shower of gold in bloom is as
local as the parish pump. It is a' fairly
widespread vegetable, and by wise tree-plant-
ing its orbit can be much further extended.
Objection number one then crept into its
lair. Somebody murmured a line snatched
from a Gordon reverie. “Oh, gaily sings the
bird, and the wattle boughs are stirr’d,” and
the thing was done.
Wattle-planting, as a universal habit, has
a weak patch in its fresh-turned sod. The
beauteous timber only lives for seven years
or thereabouts. Centennial Park will have
to be re-wattled in 1919, when the 1912
striplings will have passed into the dead-
wood series.
Three pedagogues from England—-Mr.
G. H. Archibald, Miss Archibald and Miss
Backhouse —are either pulling us by the
ear, or they sincerely believe in their amaz-
ing pose as Forerunners of Something New.
The art of expounding to your grandmother
how to suck eggs is supposed to have bluffed
that simple soul into the belief that the
urchin who performed the operation was an
inventor, and had patented the egg as his
personal discovery. Probably the lecturing
triplet are entirely sincere. They must have
a profound belief in their own apostleship
and a hearty contempt for the intelligence
of their audiences in a land which is, ot
course, very remote from Bourneville, where
the teaching Archibalds and chocolate sol-
diers come from.
On Saturday afternoon the drab old Pro-
testant Hall seethed with moderately young
children, who were to be demonstrated on
by Miss Backhouse. Before they surged
into the building, in a blithesome march (the
pretence of being outrageously happy on the
part of teacher and pupils is part of the
system), the audience straggled into its seat.
Mostly parsons and teachers, with an outer
layer of casuals, lay readers and very young
men with overhanging foreheads and serious
neckties. “The Organisation of Sunday
Schools” was the startling theme. Miss
Archibald, who smiled suddenly and for no
apparent reason (except as part of the sys-
tem) at frequent intervals in her brief dis-
course, warned the audience that the doors
would be “irrevocably closed,” and requested
ladies in the front seats to remove then-
hats. After a hurried tearing off of millin-
ery the youngsters trooped into their seats,
and the doors were closed and bolted.
The Protestant Hall looks too dead inside
to catch anything so fast as a sudden fire,
so the clanging bolt shot into its socket
without alarming the listening ear. Some-
times a loud banging incited one of the
apostles to creep stealthily to the dungeon
portal (which leaked daylight through its
panels) and apply one eye to a crack. When
the person outside persistently hammered
on the fortress and refused to be deprived of
the joys within, the person inside stuck a
card through the available slit. Presumably
the words written on the document were
“Get work.” It was pushed back with such
violence once or twice that an apostle in a
frock coat, etc., remarked that the persons
outside seemed “very much annoyed.”
Miss Backhouse, who demonstrated on the
juvenile brain-pan, is a tall, able-bodied
young woman, with the over-worked smile
which is obviously part of the. system. David
was the chief subject of the Bible lesson. He
was illustrated in one or two indifferent-
colored prints. The questions were as old
as Ur of the Chaldees. Here is a hurried
impression of a Bourneville Sunday Scrip-
ture meeting. After the youngsters had been
instructed to say a little prayer to them-
selves for anything they wanted, the teacher
plunged into the personal habits of Jona-
than’s father: —
“And what did David want?”
“A knife,” said one scholar.
I wondered if this boy had prayed for a
pocket-knife with a tin-opener and a cork-
screw and four blades. Teacher, running
her eye along the serried legion of hands
thrust into the air, got on the track of
David’s water supply. Having dragged forth
the thrilling information that the sacred
shepherd was rounding up his flock in dry
weather, she asked: “And where did David
look for water?”
“In his water-bottle,” said one pupil who
wouldn’t commit himself to the extent of a
creek. Teacher then dodged David to the
edge of the Psalms, and wandered off to
Robin Hood, log houses and the afternoon
collection.
“And tvlicit shall we do with our money?”
asks the perpetually smiling teacher. “Send
Bibles to the aborigines,” said a small Miss
Jellyby, with a row of very stiff corkscrew
curls tickling her eyebrows. The collection
amounted to about ss. After a hurried dis-
cussion, it was decided to buy a present for
the Children’s Hospital. “Longfellow or
Wordsworth’s poems,” suggested an infant
Pardiggle. “Shakspeare,” urged another
ripe young scholar. The system, from
Bourneville is reminiscent of Mrs. Pipchin’s
pleasant Sunday afternoons, and ought to
turn out young prigs by the gross. Inci-
dentally, it recalls the Sunday, school that
Tom Sawyer attended, where the superin-
tendent’s boots turned up at the toes, and
Tom said frankly that the first two disciples
were David and Goliath.
Preliminary notices concerning the length,
breadth and chest measurement of the
Women’s Work section in the next Easter
Show are soaking through the local press.
For several years this section has been
steadily rolling or sliding down-hill. It was
badly housed, and the stitchery looked tossed
and crowded together before it met the pub-
lic gaze. At last April’s great autumn rally
of horns and hoofs, the female ambition to
shine in seams and gussets had nearly
dropped dead. The standard of exhibits
had fallen to the level of the uppermost
Darling in the last days of the Old Man
Drought. Criticism, or some other electric
spark has galvanised the Royal Rural
authorities into new life. Glowing fore-
words tell of a women’s industrial depart-
ment which will supply abundant room for
the first prize goods, and for the goods that
are highly commended, and the goods that
“also ran.” Then there will be elbow room
for the tea, and the bun will have space
wherein to swing a cat, and there may even
be a glass wherein this wearied twentieth
century may look at its back hair, and
assure itself that all is well in this best ot
all possible worlds.
An avalanche of private dances will
descend on this clachan in or about race
week. Frantic rumors have been tearing
the air re the usual Australian Club call.
The landslide of information is to the effect
that dancing men may be overwhelmed with
two moderately small club dances instead ot
one large rotund ball. The private hop is
leaping to the fore, for the exclusive habits
of our Worst Society make that institution
tend more and more to flee from the vulgar
and numerous eye.
That bright-eyed, alert little woman, Mrs.
Jack London, dropped a letter to a Sydney
friend the other mail. At date of writing
the London duet were humming a deep-sea
chanty off the Horn. “We’ve been aboard
this four-masted steel ship, Dirigo, 116 days
from Baltimore, after spending the winter
in New York. We expect to be in Seattle
in another month (end of July), a long
passage, but we love the sea. The only land
we’ve seen these 116 days was Gape Horn.
Sailors seldom ‘lay eyes on it,’ _so we were
particularly luckv to get a glimpse. A
nautical friend, however, tells me that sailors
often see the Horn. The trouble is that
they stay there.
A London paper on the look-out for snappy
items for its perambulating camera, recently
ran a “white wedding” to the church door-
step. The bridal pair are mentioned as "fol-
lowing a custom of their country.” As a
fact, the Australian ’groom, in the southern
half of this continent, who turns out_ in
white with his male supporters clad in simi-
lar tropic lingerie, is an uncommon exhibit
in dressy circles. In mid-summer here he
climbs into much the same garb as a Lon-
doner does, when he stamps up the aisle m
that long-established British institution,
the sombre frock coat; and propounds
the indeterminate sad-colored tubes on
his legs. His black-silk tile he fur-
tively conceals in some handy place
till the “Wedding March” struts across the
organ. Mr. and Mrs. A. J. Sharpe—said to
be of Melbourne- —were the bridal pair (pro-
fusely illustrated) who headed the proces-
sion. It looked like an inter-State tennis
tournament with the parson as umpire.
July 8 was the date. Obviously, it had
dodged the English wet-match rainfall, for
only one member of the party had an um-
brella, and the white walking gentleman
looked bone dry.
An' enthusiastic Anglo-Indian named
Wayman has been stalking these shores, as
a preliminary to a tourist hunt next year.
Wayman is reported to be as keen as a new
knife, and he hopes to catch the Australian
earth-trotter at this end next year. The
Man from Ceylon represents the Tourist
Bureau of that spicy island, and he wants
to haul the local sightseer off to the cinna-
mon gardens beside the coast, and the tea-
caddy hills beyond Colombo.
Mrs. M. Forrest, the Queensland scribe,
celebrated her recent holiday in this town
by a prolonged argument with an influenza
germ. She resumed her usual health last
week, and took the overland trail to Bris-
bane on Saturday.
Mr. Sydney Philips, of St. Canice, Dar-
ling Point, is packing up for a trip to the
islands of the early-rising Japanee. Ihe
Philips rooftree, as I mentioned the other
day, is let for six months to the Edward
Noyes family.
Mrs. Louis Henderson (Double Bay) is
gathering together a large collection of
friends, countrymen, etc., to give the glad
hand of welcome to her sisters, Mrs. Bon-
thorne and Miss Bud Neill. They have fled
greatly in foreign parts, and they frequently
smote the Swiss glacier with an alpenstock
without damaging the scenerv. Yat someone
has declared that Switzerland is really run
as an outdoor show by J. C. Williamson,
and that the cardboard mountains are put
up by his scene-shifters in the spring, and
removed in the autumn. I should think it
would be dangerous to hit a country like
that hard with an alpenstock..
When does a perpetual curate cease to be
perpetual, I wonder? The Bev. Wilfred
Gore-Brown, whose parent used to be an
Excellency in Maoriland, cut his clerical
gaiters as a perpetual curate in England.
Thence he glided over to Boer-land, and re-
cently emerged with full-grown wings of
laundered white muslin, as the first Bishop
of Kimberley and adjacent mineral pad-
docks.
The white house, with tiny brown shingles
as window eaves, is the latest fashion in
suburban architecture on the expensive
shores of our best water-fronts. Most of
the new walls are so blazing white that they
would make the family ghost turn smoke
grey with envy, if he chanced to blow that
wav on a moonlit night. The long arm of the
builder and bricklayer has been at last re-
moved from the pale white homestead of the
O’Gorman Hughes. A pink cushion now
blushes through a lattice window that
faces the tram-line, and the owners have
been in possession for some weeks. The
gabled roof is under the shoulder of the hill
that melts away into Woollahra Point.
The Glaciarium has ceased to freeze for
skaters till next May. A complimentary
skating party of mixed sports to manager
Dunbar Poole closed the Gardens of Allah-
abad with a loud snap.
Roller skating still smites the polished
floor of the Imperial Roller Rink in Wil-
liam-street. Manager James Bendrodt was
peacefully taking the air at the top of his
imperial marble steps the other afternoon.
His occupation as pusher-out of undesirable
“pussons” has passed into the limbo of
things no longer wanted. The season rolls
on for another seven weeks with a dazzling
roll at its far end. After that, I presume
the placid young man with the pronounced
chin and capable hands will climb into a
hollow log or some other summer retreat.
Exasperating persons who give puzzle
parties are very fond of asking, “Where do
all the pins go to?” and other nerve-racking
questions. Why doesn’t someone work up
a new riddle and floor the company with
this problem: “Where do all the skating
managers go to when they shed their skates?
Do they hide in refrigerating plants or drift
along as summer boarders at Jenolan?”
Pianist Spencer Clay, who is generally
observed on the horizon a month or two
ahead of John McCormack, recently dropped
into this city. He’s some pounds avoirdu-
pois closer to the word “fat” than usual, and
blames the Australian climate. Clay’s spare
frame can stand an increase of flesh, for his
swallow-like darts up and down this planet
trained him to the size of a half-grown
McCormack shadow. The coming event is
a year or so ahead. Clay and H. N. South-
well are at present knocking their wits
together as joint directors of the only John’s
concert tour of Australasia, which begins in
Sydney on the doorstep of the October race
meeting, 1913.
An acquaintance who met Paderewski and
his commanding officer (Mrs. Pad.) on the
way from Switzerland to Paris reports all s
well with the long staccato locks. They are
as lengthy and electric as ever, and show
no signs of moulting. The matinee girl’s
raids in these parts once threatened to
pluck the summit of Paderewski as bare as
a marble. But his lynx-eyed lady is alleged
to be a battering-ram and round-tower of
defence against Continental collectors of
.lion’s manes, claws, autographs or other
valuable bric-h-brac.
Lottie Dearn is hurriedly swallowing a
few hearty snacks of German before start-
ing for Leipsic Conservatorium and the
Only Way to snatch a bursary or piano-
double forte scholarship. The clever young-
ster’s sister sets out, fiddle in hand, to keep
one eye on Lottie in Leipsic, and the other
on her own bow and bridge. The harmoni-
ous Dearns leave this port on October 2.
“Five Little Songs of Love” (music by
Isabel Varney) have been roosting in my
humble official pigeon-holes for the last
week or two, waiting for a vacant plot of
paper. The young singer who likes a tear-
drop in her lay will find a whole shower in
“A Lament” —a drawing-room dirge for a
wet Saturday evening. For older warblers
who have outgrown the wet handkerchief
moods of dreary 17, “Acceptance,” with a
spring growth in its brisk accompaniment,
will strike several responsive chords. There
are three other little ditties shrined in the
same cover. Paling’s are responsible for the
production.
The black silk choker —a poor relation of
the Gladstone stock —is struggling to the
ear-lobes of the smart young man about tea-
rooms. He wears it half-way up his collar.
The linen fence grows shorter, and is locked
across the front with a. severe black bow.
An unimportant youth, earmarked as the
last gasp of fashion, at a recent Society
Freeze, tried to look unconscious of the
rising-up of his new style necktie, which
tickled him under one ear when he pushed
his partner round the frenzied waltz. Pro-
bably something had given way in the back-
wash of his lingerie. Anyhow, even a woman
can foresee trouble for the inexperienced
young buck, with a swan-like area of neck,
who tries to wear a half-grown silk stock
across the middle of his Adam’s apple.
A dull week fainted quietly away in a wet
Saturday night splash. Between the rain-
drops of the Thursday water spout, the
Butterfly maidens, heavily coated, swished
into the Best Rag Stores, inspected the
new patterns in shot silks, and the wooden-
looking ruches on the Paris models. The
stiff skirts of shiny silk, with prim trim-
mings, and the little jackets of another color,
drift back to the mid-’Bo’s.
Spring millinery shows a tendency to climb
up in front like well-staked tomatoes. A
very small woman, with one of these on-end
stalks of floriculture, or a, stiff bow rising
like a ship’s mast out of the crown of her
head, is a sorrowful example of matter in
the wrong place, when a wind squall strikes
her top deck.
Some audacious prophet said that one-
button gloves and elastic-sided boots were
coming into fashion this year. But they
haven’t struck this end of the planet at date
of scratching this memo.
Yours as usual,
VANDORIAN.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, September 2, 1912.
My Dear Yarrie, —
The Gaud Mayor and his Gaudess are in-
viting the Best People to meet Governoi
Fuller and his lady at a sparkling dance on
Tuesday, September 17. The Best 1 eople
and tlie Fullers have, of course, fallen over
one another’s feet on innumerable occasions
already. But Society never wearies of meet-
ing itself and saying things behind its own
back. It is the modern edition of the ser-
pent that turns its engine round and bites
its own luggage van.
Governor Fuller opened Melbourne’s first
motor exhibition at the Big Barn, on Satur-
day. In the course of a breezy speech, he
mentioned a pathetic incident that happened
last month in London. The British Museum
bought a hansom cab to preserve as a relic
of a disappearing age. (What out-of-date
poet was it that wrote, "To hear the hansoms
slurring Once more through London mud” ?
And how much longer is it since Gilbert said
to the inebriated guest. "You are a four-
wheeler,” on the ground that the inebriated
one had taken him for a waiter, and clam-
ored out, "Call me a <;ab”?) Then his Ex.
started off on the grand tour of the 55
stands, and a flashing belltopper was quickly
lost to view amongst the wilderness of fiery
chariots. A reek of new rubber and fresh
enamel hangs in the air; but the gentle
visitor will sniff in vain for a whiff of oil.
That was barred on account of the fire risk,
so all the exhibits were towed in a lifeless
condition to the show.
Mrs. Peter, of Glenloth station, gave a
smart dance last week at her Northern Vic-
torian stronghold. The long-distance guests
arrived at 7.30, and left about five next
morning. They had to drive all the way
round a very full Lake Boort —a sheet of
water which has a curious habit, during dry
stretches, of becoming suddenly hard at the
edges, and leaving a smooth track for the
passing motor. When it is in a bad wet
mood the passing motor has to go round by
Adelaide and Bourke.
Last Monday, the Wattle maidens arrayed
themselves airily in white, and went forth.
But the weather had a bad relapse, and it
turned out to be a cold raid. One girl,
dressed as a Cingalee, actually invaded the
Vienna bar, and two of the Most Expensive
took possession of the Melbourne Club steps
and waved syren sprays of wattle at the
cowardly refugees within doors. It was bad
tactics, though, to call a refreshment halt
during the city’s dinner-hour. When the
attack was resumed, the enemy had taken
cover again, and the harvest of a bleak after-
noon wasn’t very rich. Grace Palotta, in
furs and tailor-mades, upset the Stock Ex-
change with the help of Ivy Bickford, Ilig-
ginson and Fred Leslie, made up as Dago
musicians. Grace is stated to have kissed
the chairman to win a guinea bet, while the
good Gaud. Mayor who wrestles with the
dust problem got a “half kiss” and a half-
crown spray of wattle for half a guinea. The
question i‘s : Was he cautious _or economical ?
Anyhow, what is a "half kiss”? Did the
Gaud Mayor kiss himself, or did Grace touch
her lips with her finger-tips and wave them
at him, or did he approach blushing in a
manner becoming an elderly gentleman, and
then retreat when he had gone half-way?
Whatever it was, the dust trouble was still
unsettled when all the kisses had been paid
for.
Marjorie Levey, christened at St. Peter’s
some 20 odd years ago, motored along there
last week to be married. A pair of parsons
converted the lady into Mrs. Albert Guy
Miller, of the large and wealthy clan that
helps to own Melbourne. The doctor bride-
groom looked properly nervous, whilst the
bride hid her blushes under some precious
Brussels lace that was the wedding veil of
Grandmamma Grice. Three Grice girls and
Athol Levey made a quartette of pale pink
bridesmaids, and lawyer Jim Purves’s two
youngsters swelled the joyous pageant as
train bearers. Osborne O’Hara, Hubert
Miller, Norman Hodges and Jack Carse, all
of them excellent afternoon-tea young men,
were in close attendance. They were garbed
in the well-known immaculate brand of
clothes, and wore butter-color gloves that
would have delighted the most exacting
dairy farmer. The two handsome mothers,
Mrs. Levey and Mrs. Albert Miller, wept
happily, and, later, entertained Toorak at
afternoon tea on the tennis court and in a
pale pink marquee at Bowden. A pink-
trimmed dance in the evening closed the
social disturbance.
Molly Atherton wore a big white hat in Lon-
don at her wedding to Captain Edward White,
of the Northamptonshire Regiment. The re-
sult is that the habitual bridesmaids in these
parts are confidently assuming that big hats
must be the very latest things in altar
fashions. A brother officer of the 48th sup-
ported the bridegroom at the ordeal, and
squired pretty sister Muriel, who appeared
as pink-and-white bridesmaid. The knot-
ting was got through at St. Paul’s, Knights-
bridge, and the toasts at the Alexandra
Hotel.
The death of Mrs. Alfred Isaacs will hit
her devoted family very hard. Jack Isaacs
could never find time for marriage in his
incessant attendance on his mother, and
Mr. Justice Isaacs paid court to her every
day, telephoning his homage when absent
from Melbourne. The old lady was a keen
Shakspearian student, and could almost
quote the bard backwards. She had a tena-
cious memory, and followed the topics of the
moment with the alertness of a brain of 30.
Also, she was guilty of writing verses.
Mrs. Oswald Watt quits her grass-widow
flat next week, and departs to England by
the Malwa. Now there is another luxurious,
secondhand electric “broom” on the motor
market.
The Lyceum Club is acquiring some new
rooms at the end of this week. President
Mrs. Deakin will solemnly drink a well-
brewed cup of tea, and make the apparently
indispensable remarks.
The two dozen debutantes of the Catholic
Ladies’ College dance “came out” in a
thoroughly painstaking spirit. They went
to Mass on Sunday morning, and afterwards
practised with their dancing master until
G o’clock, when they were let off further
rehearsal for the evening meal and vespers.
High Provisioner George lieid’s daughter
Thelma is engaged to Mr. Jack Playfair.
He is a Sydney young man who went to
London to ride at the Horse Show. If he
didn’t win, it was the fault of the animals
he bestrode, for no finer horseman exists
anywhere. The family’s wealth emanates
from Meat —in plain terms, they are but-
chers. Hence, no doubt, Mr. Reid’s enthu-
siasm over the projected alliance. Still,
there is, mayhap, a flaw in our Stipendiary
Feeder’s bliss. If only, now the pros-
pective son-in-law had come of a lolly-
manufacturing family! Thelma is very
young, but when much younger was
a book-learned and knowledgeable girl
who could argue with the best, and
stun people with quotations from Shaks-
peare and worse. When she went to
London, she blossomed out as a capable
entertaining help to her rather timid mother,
and she has been in the centre of the social
arena ever since.
Widower General Gordon has taken
Urana, in Punt-road, as a home-camp, with
young daughter Eily as housekeeper. The
warrior, just back from Westralia, has now
gone northwards to Brisbane, with inci-
dental camps at Albury and Sydney on the
way.
Penleigh Boyd, of the extensive Boyd and
a’Beckett clans, is trumpeting announce-
ments of his engagement from London. His
fiancee is Edith Anderson, one of Brisbane’s
nicest girls, and daughter of the late Gerard
Anderson. The two young people are just
out of their teens. Pen Boyd is an artist
who got a picture hung in the Royal Acad-
emy last year; also, he had quite a success-
ful' exhibition of his work in London. The
pair will marry and return to Australia
after Christmas.
On Monday afternoon, a number of politi-
cal big guns and social small arms wan-
dered along to see Margaret Baskerville’s
statue of the late Thomas Bent. Mrs.
Bleasby, the old politician’s daughter, after
looking at the image’s tight-fitting vest, vol-
unteered some information. “My father
always wore a long, loose, rumpled vest,”
she remarked. So the little sculptress got to
work, and started to make the statue less
tidy about the chest. While in , the studio,
Mrs. Bleasby marked for her own a Basker-
ville water-color sketch of Brighton Beach.
The Elsternwick Hockey Club gave an in-
vitation dance in All Saints’ Hall, the other
evening. Half the A team (alas!) are say-
ing good-bye to the grand old lunacy this
season. Mollison, the captain, has decided
that he is now too weighty to propel himself
violently across the sward in order to grunt
and Smack the spot where the ball was
before somebody hit it elsewhere. Bill
Dennet is facing matrimony-—a state of ex-
istence which admits of no diversions. He
is also going to Queensland. Guy Watson is
abandoning Dalgety’s and hockey for the
joys of agriculture. By the way, the
Eisternwick team can trace its beginnings
to the cow paddock of papa Dalgety Watson,
where it flourished under the title of the
lona Club.
A recent bride is said to hqve threatened
to have the knot tied in a cold, empty taber-
nacle, unless Mamma swore to.cut the name
of a certain heavyweight cleric off the list
of breakfast orators. Of course, Mamma
swore, but on the eventful day the poor lady
blushed every time she met the reproachful
eye of the rejected speech-maker. It appears
that the poor man has a perfect genius for
saying the wrong thing and putting his foot
in it. At any rate, the bride reckoned that
his oratorical footwork was too poor to let
him talk at her banquet.
Before they left Melbourne, the Canadian
cadets pronounced Melbourne “a bully
place.” It may have had something to do
with the local girls. Society pets pursued
the nice-looking importations and gave them
the time of their lives. Tete-a-tete drives in
cosy motor-cars and tea-parties for two were
going on all the time. The boys wepe fin-
ished skaters, and the Glaciarium saw a lot
of them. The fuss kicked up by their com-
manding officer in Sydney when free enter-
tainment wasn’t offering in sufficient Quan-
tities, was here translated into a grateful
farewell message of thanks as the contingent
resumed its travels.
In the death and funeral reports of Booth
there is no mention of younger son Herbert.
Herbert was in Melbourne with Ins wife
somewhere about the time of Brassey. lhe
Faller-off made an appearance at a great
Army rally in Melbourne Town Hall, at
which Mrs. Herbert spoke. She was a
fluent speaker, and a particularly handsome
woman. When her husband had spent some
years in Melbourne, he was transferred to
Collie (W.A.). The couple desired a more
civilised environment for themselves and
their small children; but the old General
stubbornly refused to shift them from the
backblocks. Herbert thereupon resigned
from the Army, and drifted back to Eng-
land, where he went in for slum work under
different management.
“Wanted some young girl over school age
who will act as telegraph messenger on drill
days.” This is a wail from Rutherglen
(Vic.) from a postmaster who feels the in-
convenience of Australia’s call to arms.
A fashionable woman who gives most up-
to-date bridge parties hereabout, finds that
she is expected to supply cigars as well as
whisky and cigarettes for her women guests.
The cigars are certainly small things; but.
like the cigarettes, they may grow long and
thick. Then, if the smoking woman buys the
same sort of cigars for herself as she does
for her husband, there will be a heavy,
singed-dog odor over the land.
Alexander Watson, of the apparently
flawless memory, stumbled the other even-
ing during “The Charge of the Light Bri-
gade,” and, after accepting three prompts,
rushed his men out of the Valley of Death
like the N.S.W. Sergeant-at-Arins _ on some
evening when the Opposition is feeling fresh.
It was seemingly the old trouble of being too
familiar with your piece. (Yearly and half-
yearly and other subscribers, also casual
investors of sixpence, will please refrain
from reading that sentence as slang.) .The
fine reciter is beginning to feel the strain of
giving continuous shows with a change of
programme every night. In crowded
London a lecturer can give the same pro-
gramme for weeks on end, and so give the
substance under his hat a chance.
Kirkby Lunn made the concert of last
week brilliant, in a clothes sense, by wearing
a lemon-colored dress with a vivid orange
belt. Long gloves that stretched almost to
the shoulders went with the striking cos-
tume, and her brown hair (whispered to be
all her own) was coiled into a most correct
coiffure that would have passed muster at
Queen Victoria’s Court. She is delightfully
correct as regards her singing. The three-
ply velvet voice is kept in Quaker-like sub-
jection, and is never the sport of strong emo-
tions. On Wednesday night, she carolled
some delightful little French songs. Some-
body appears to have slain the most deadly
of the Town Hall draughts. Though a
snow-trimmed Antarctic disturbance was
raging outside, the large audience sat com-
fortably through the concert.
The cloak-room lady smiled coldlv and
watched the women going in and out. “This
is only the second Kirkby Lunn concert, and
already I’ve lost three powder puffs and an
ivory comb. The Cisneros season cleaned
up two brushes, a lot of hairpins and pearl-
powder, and one nickel-plated shoe-horn. I
can’t watch the toilet gear and help every-
one with their wraps at the same time; so
the pilferers do it without much risk. Furs
should be kept under lock and key—if
women will insist on leaving them. Lace
scarves melt like butter in summer. The
fashionables are really the worst of the lot.
Some night one of these female pirates will
tear up the carnet and roll it out with a
few notabilities kicking inside it. Then
there will be trouble. They couldn’t very
well call that sort of thing kleptomania,
could they ?”
When Sylvia Ffrench goes to join adopted
mamma Melba after the Cup revels, she will
most probably travel under the wing of
Auntie Power, a noted lion-tamer in the
social arena.
From my Adelaide correspondent: —
The harassed housewife has been looking rather
less so these last few days, for lo! the Government
has leased a place at Norwood, which is to be used
as a home for assisted immigrants who have come
here as domestic helpers. The step is, of course,
disgracefully Socialistic, and I take it the place will
be avoided by all conscientious Liberals—ladies and
lydies alike. But to the rest of us it looks uncom-
monly like a Boon—provided we find somebody in the
house when we go there. Often before, have we gone
to premises where Domestic Helpers were alleged,
to be on sale, and have beaten with wild umbrellas
at the door, all to no purpose. Generally, we have
been offered a governess, or an elderly and decayed
actress, or a book-keeper, as a substitute for the
goods after which we craved. We have even been
tendered a reliable sundowner.
On the Government’s new premises the out-of-
work dish-breaker is to be dissected and classified.
And after her, hot-foot, will trek the out-of-maid
resident. The Home will be a sort of club. Books,
sewing machines and other delights will be pro-
vided. Also, cheap meals.
Froekily speaking, the A.R.C. meeting, at Victoria
Park, was a frost. A cloudy sky drove female race-
goers to the coat-and-skirt habit. Then the sun
smiled emphatically, and the effect was awful. Coats
and skirts which have seen a season’s hard wear,
simply hate being smiled on by the sun. The Gov-
ernor and his daughters were there, also Miss Phipps.
I mention this to show you that, as a sporting com-
munity, we are still loyal to the core. The Hunt
Club turned up in time ior the first race, after an
early morning run. Lockwing pleased the few who
backed him by paying £23 in the Steeple, and
Korea gave his supporters £29 odd for second place
in the Beaumont Trial.
Trade has been brisk during the past couple of
days at Government House, where an exhibition of
locally-owned curios has been on view. Admission,
2s. 6d. ; proceeds in aid of the D.T.N. Society. All
feminine Adelaide has turned out its glory-boxes and
garrets, and the result leaves us gasping at our own
artistic wealth. Two thousand curios, mark you!
The exhibits vary from exquisite miniatures to un-
flattering dagr.errotypes, from ancient samplers to
wondrous modern from gold model ships
to tea caddies and jewellery.
Mr. Jack Lindon (nephew of the Lindon of Gee-
long Grammar School) is engaged to Miss Adelaide
Pulton. She, like Miss Warren, the heroine of last
week’s engagement, is a connection of the Downer
clan.
The Hornabroolcs’ house, on East Terrace, changed
hands this week. For some years it has been leased
by the Arthur Waterhouses, and is now sold to the
Malcolm Reids.
From England comes news that Miss Martha
Bruggemann is working her way ahead in London.
Her teacher, the famous Bouhy, speaks well of her,
and she recently sang with no small success at one of
the Sir Henry ‘Wood “Promenades.” Another South
Australian to appear at the same concert was
violinist Daisy Kennedy.
The worth of draught horses, and Suffolk punches
will be weighed by a new judge at the September
Show —one John James, of Derrimat, near Cplae,
Victoria.
Yours affectionately,
JOHANNA.

A COMMON-SENSE TALK
By the WHISKYOLOGIST.
NE of the many important things worth noting
about Joshua’s Whisky is that you are not asked
to drink it merely because it is Australian. A request
of that kind would be as stupid as the refusal of some
men to ask for it merely because it is Australian.
Common-sense reasoning and common-sense decisions,
based on the evidence of the true merit of the beverage,
are all that are necessary to convince you of the goodness
of this Whisky.
You are invited to drink Joshua’s Whisky because
its age and its purity are supported by an absolute
Government guarantee.
With this “G.R.” label on the bottle the quality of
Joshua’s Whisky is removed from the debatable stage
and placed on a level of certainty.
Therefore, if considerations of health, quality and
value are of importance to you, you will ask for
Joshua’s Government guaranteed Whisky, and refuse
efforts to put you off with the imported brands.
Age, Quality and Purity
Government Guaranteed.

POLITICAL
POINTS
At last N.S.W. Speaker AVillis has hit the
Ark of the Covenant in the eye. In other
words, he has shut the Sydney Telegraph
reporters out of Parliament House. It
would have been hardly more terrible if he
had gone into the middle of Illawarra, and
there lifted his voice on high and d d
the great goddess Cow.
THE CONSTITUTIONAL RAILROAD.
Mr. W- E. Johnson argued the other day that N. S. Wales members should stand together, regardless
of their politics, and demand the State’s rights.... It is to be hoped that they will stand together.' They
will not be asking for any concession or favor to N. S. Wales, but only that reasonable effect shall be
given to the Constitution and Federal Legislation.—D.T., excitedly.
OUR HEROINE: “Oh, th' plottin' villain! After all my shrewdness an' schemin' for this
route some wretches 'ud dare try t' switch th' traffic onto some other line! 'Elp! ’Elp!"
THE SLAYER.
[Fon The Bulletin'.]
Mr. Kelly was giving some cogent arguments
against the proposal when the time for the motion
expired.— Parliamentary throe.
Alas 1 this moves us much to find
So fair a motion sped—
Before relentless Kelly’s wind
Old Time himself dropped dead!
N.S.W. P. E.
One D. M. Manilal, M.A., an Indian bar-
rister, recently yarned to a Sydney press-
man about his countrymen : —
The leading men of India are strongly averse to
the emigration of indentured labor. Vie hope the
day will soon come when it will be stopped alto-
gether. Only the riff-raff go as a rule —-often those
who have got into disgrace—but we don’t want to
lose even them. We can find employment in our own
country for them all. I am afraid that those who
know only the Indian hawker in Australia, or the
ind.an coolie in Hji, have not a very good oninion
of the Indians as a whole. But you must never for-
get that the better class of Indians are not to be
found in the coolie lines, nor peddling goods from
farm to farm in the country.
So the Indian hawker, it would appear, is
the scum of Hindustan, and Hindustan is
reported to be a rather scummy country.
His AVashup might consider this pronounce-
ment next time Ram Blast applies for a re-
newal of his hawking license.
Deakin, on the Federal Budget:—
It was generally recognised that the rise in prices
in Australia and the increase in wages had been eat-
ing up the small Protection which the manufacturers
of the Commonwealth enjoyed, and had in many
cases brought them into serious competition with
imports from abroad. The tariff of 1908 was,
broadly speaking, as much a revenue as a Pro-
tectionist tariff. It had been asserted that the in-
creases of wages by Wages Boards throughout Aus-
tralia were nullifying such Customs duties as were
in operation.
Cook, on ditto : —
The tariff in Australia, taken altogether, was not
far, if anything, behind the Canadian tariff. The
manufaetureres were having a good time —the fac-
tories were filled almost to bursting point.
That “honorable understanding” ordaining
that the fiscal subject shouldn’t even be
mentioned between them, which joined
Deakin and Cook in political matrimony,
looks a very gelatinous sort of bond these
times.
“Briny” writes : —
According to Winston Churchill, “The Reichstag
has passed five Navy Acts in 14 years. The pro-
gramme of 1898 was practically doubled two years
later. Had the law of 1900 been pursued without
variation, the annual expenditure would be only
about £11,000,000. It is £23,000,000. Nearly
four-fifths of the German Navy will be maintained
in full permanent commission, instantly and con-
stantly ready for war. The severe German criticisms
are only against the inadequacy of this! Naval war
can reach its decisive phase with awful suddenness.
The blow can be prepared at an hour’s notice, and
delivered with the swiftness of a thunderbolt. Yet
consider, the extreme slowness with which the instru-
ments are forged. Small ships take 18 to 20 months
to build. Large ones two, three, even four years.
Docks take over four years to build, seamen two or
three years to train, artificers much longer, officers
six or seven years. And the efficiency from harmoni-
ous combination of these elements is a plant of very
slow growth indeed. We ought to learn from our
German neighbors, whose policy marches unswerv-
ingly towards its goal across the lifetime of a whole
generation.” Bonar Law says: “Ten years ago we
had the command of every sea. Now only of the
North Sea. I am reminded of the decline of the
Roman Empire, when pressure at the heart forced
them to recall their legions. This has happened to
us.” Churchill admits that the coal strike pre-
vented £2,000,000 being spent on naval construc-
tion. Another strike or two might enable Germany
to overtake Britain in battleships. y
THE BIRD THAT TALKED TOO
MUCH.
Mr. Deakin made one confession. The Customs
tariff, he says, is mostly a revenue tariff. This was
not the description which was given to it when it
pissed. It was applauded as a magnificent instru-
ment for creating vast factories all over Australia.
—Daily paper.

YOUNG AUSTRALIA (to Fisher): “ B'gosh ,
Mister, I wisht you'd do away with com-
pulsory milkin'!”
“Dano” : I am afraid Albury, in the Ma
State, will soon have to consider the pro-
priety of changing its name to Al’zireh, or
something of the kind. Ten years
ago the Syrian draper was a small,
humble trader who lurked or hov-
ered or slunk in the lower part of
the town. Now he slinks brazenly
in the full glare of day, and there
are about 15 Syrian rag palaces,
some of them occupying the best
sites in the business area. Many
women who have no compunction
in running a big account with the
white trader scornfully spend their
spot cash with the alien, though
why this is so neither they nor I
can tell. It is a tragic jest that
the electorate which returns Bill
Lyne, White Australian and Pro-
tectionist, should be afflicted by
Benliadad in this fashion.
N.S.W. Government got a bad
scare last month, when a public
servant sued it for the cash value
of all the years of leave he had
omitted to take in his time. It
has now published a regulation
which says that the Service must
take its leave regularly. Any chap
that stacks his arrears up for
more than two years running starts
to lose it and all right to ever get
it. It is a salutary move. The
only man who never needs a holi-
day is the man who never works.
This type is of no value to any service. There
is another variety of non-holiday taker;
namely, the sort who doesn’t dare to leave
his job for fear it will be audited, or some-
thing, in his absence. And he is even more
useless than the other variety of chair-
warmer.
“Rule Nisi”: It would seem to be a tra-
dition with N. S. Wales Governments, Lib-
eral and Labor alike, to leap violently on
the aged public servant wherever a shadow
of excuse offers. The other day, one of the
tribe, by name Glen, wtat 61, asked for per-
mission to retire on the ground of ill-health.
The Government said he might, but after
retiring him refused to pay the usual grat-
uity on the grounds (1) that the amount
due had not been calculated, and (2) that
the plaintiff had voluntarily retired. The
Full Court’s, comments on these points were
that the first objection was idiotic, and the
second senseless, or words to that effect, and
piled Costs on top of the State as usual. It
is a noteworthy fact that in its campaign
against the aged of the Public Service the
N.S.W. Law Department has only won once
—and then it won on a foul, so to speak.

F. M. B. Fisher, of Maoriland, son of old
George Fisher wh m Wellington loved, is
still gifted with the old ingenuity which
once enabled him to
find a voucher which
wasn’t a voucher, or
else wasn’t the same
voucher that he had
discovered it to be. He
is now in the Massey
Ministry, and has out-
lined a novel plan of
selecting civil servants
by lottery. Influence
is to be shut out from
the departments over
which he has control,
and when two, five, or
20 equally well-quali-
fied men apply for a
job they are to toss
for it.
The N.S.W. State railways are doing not
much better, despite the great increase of
traffic, than the Federal Posts and Tele-
graphs. For the year to June, 1911, they
showed a profit of £4 13s. Cd. per cent, on
capital but for last year this slumped to
£4 Bs. 2d.
Melbourne’s two organs
of anti-Democratic opin-
ion are just as far from a
fusion of Tory sentiment
as ever they were. Every
item in the State Minis-
terial programme moves
them to discord. Premier
Watt, seemingly, has the
Age behind him, pushing
him on and patting his
back, whilst the Argus
attempts to shove him the
other way. In fact,
Watt’s unpopularity with
the Argus gives him a
decidedly progressive ap-
pearance. Even his de-
nunciations of Fisher
Government “fallacies”
fall unheeded on the ear
of that Tory family jour-
nal, inasmuch as he de-
nunciates in a Deakin-
esque, rather than a
Jokookian tone of voice.
Practically it is not a
one party support at all,
but only a mutual an-
tagonism to the other
party, from two different
standpoints. The two
posts meet at the top, but
their angle of inclination
is. not the same, and their
footing is insecure.
The proposal to put up
a Labor candidate against
Uncle Bill Lyne was
playing it low down on a valiant old warrior
with whom the world hasn’t gone too well.
Lyne, in recent years, hasn’t simply been “as
good as a Labor man.” tie has been a heap
more democratic than many Labor men, and
the only possible reason for trying to oust
him is that somebody wants the bearded
ancient’s salary.
The “general report regarding my visit to
America,” supplied by N.S.W. ex-Minister
Nielsen, the gentleman whose colleagues of
the McGowen Government sent him on a
long trip at £4 4s. per day for “expenses”
plus his Parliamentary salary, has been
printed. It covers about five pages, and
sets forth that the writer met Percy Hunter
(everybody meets P. Hunter somewhere),
that he got Mr. P. E. Quinn as assistant,
that he was ill for a while (stomach, possi-
bly), that colored labor is cheap in Hono-
lulu (well known before), that foreign ships
are not permitted to engage in the U.S.
coasting trade (very well known for many
years past), thet he went to certain places,
that owing to his illness (stomach, it may
be) he didn’t go to other places, and that
he left all these places and came home. In-
cidentally, he engaged a window in San
Francisco' in which to show N.S.-v*. pro-
ducts, but hadn’t any products to show. It
looks about the scantiest report that was
ever acquired at very high cost from a very
dissatisfied supporter. Now Mr. Nielsen,
still retaining his Parliamentary seat, is
off again at very high cost to struggle some
more with the vast commercial orb of his
fate, and he left so suddenly that his name
didn’t even appear in the passenger list.
Mr. Ocky writes :
At last somebody has been drawing attention to
the utter absurdity of the dredging that goes on,
year in and year out, at Port Phillip. To keep the
poor old place about halfway up to requirements
this submarine shovelling and digging and baling goes
on incessantly. Then the dug-out material has to be
sent away. Nobody ever dares face the expense
of nutting it on railway trucks and sending it to the
Mallee, so it is simply taken to another part of the
harbor and dropped in there. Then it gradually
slides and slithers and slumps back into the channel,
and Sisyphus, the dredge, gets it out again. If a
temporary retaining-wall was built across the Heads
and Port Phillip was pumped out (which, I believe,
could be done for about £100,000,000), and then a
wide 100 ft. channel was dug out with steam scoops,
and the material was taken away by train and
thrown on to the countenance of the country of
Karkarooc, in order that it might fertilise the same,
some permanent results might ensue. As it is, the
whole business seems a purposeless carrying of dead
elephants uphill to no-place, and meanwhile the con-
stant dashing of mud all around is destroying the
fishing beds. Now, is all this banging and slushing
a business-like distribution of money and time and
energy, and is it to go on for ever, whilst Portland
and other places, which require little or no dredg-
ing (and ■ which have more than a foot of water
between the bottom of the boat and the ocean bed),
remain the deserted victims of a ruinous centralising
mania? Also, can somebody tell me how many
dredges have been imported, made, or otherwise
obtained in Australia, in (say) the last 10 years,
and the exact amount in pence that has been spent
on the good harbors which need no dredging?

Do you hear the sound of marching ? ’Tis the
Nation standing still,
And in frenzy climbing downward up the flat and
fearful hill,
Where the army of right-thinkers holds the fort for
Freedom yet,
With its mind on open-shop and somewhat larger
profits set.
Do you hear the noise of armies marching, marching
swift and slow?
'Tis the Nation pushing forward with its back to-
wards the foe!
Hear the tramping of the legions, by their camp-
fires sitting tight,
While the sun shines fiercely on them through the
long, grim battle-night!
List, a thousand martial voices as the cohorts move
along—
A glum, retreating rabble, with a burst of cheerful
song
As the Nation’s broken leaders move more men up to
the front,
And the fighting knights of Slowgress lose their
horses in the hunt!
Fagged and fresh, and tired as daisies, and with
tallow on their heels,
Rearward march the starving forces, halting now
and then for meals.
They are marking time like blazes, every hope of
conquest gone,
Pushing forward still, and backwards, giving in and
battling on,
While their beaten colors, waving in the forefront
of the fray,
Help to keep ’em from rememb’ring that they
shouldn’t act that way.
Whence comes that growing clamor, that immense,
approaching roar
As of half a gross of breakers banging on a cliff-
bound shore?
What is that sound of cheering? Why that glad,
tremendous shout?
’Tis the Nation giving thanks because it’s wholly
down and out;
And the noise of its retreating can be heard far on
ahead,
As it hunts the foe —assisted by its somewhat lively
dead !

FOUL BREATH.
MISFORTUNE FOR MAN OR WOMAN.
The old saying that “Distance lends en-
chantment” was never more aptly. to the
point than when applied to those people
whose breath is laden with evil-smelling and
offensive odors. Many persons appear most
attractive at a distance, but produce a feel-
ing of pity and disgust when close to, be-
cause of the condition of their breath. For
instance, it makes little difference how beau-
tiful a woman may be, or how charming her
manner, if her breath is foul; her charm is
gone, and she is at a disadvantage whenever
she comes in contact with other people.
Foul breath in woman arouses in man a
feeling of repugnance and a desire to get
out of the company of such a woman as
quickly as possible; and even women will
avoid her. It is, therefore, important for
every woman who values her personal at-
traction to take such care of herself that
this unpleasant and unnecessary complaint
is avoided or corrected. Dr. Morse’s Indian
Root Pi‘ll§ will be found a searching and
cleansing remedy, which will overcome this
complaint. They give you clean bowels, a
healthy stomach, a lively liver, and blood
that is rich and red. They are a safe, sure
and reliable remedy, and will prevent many
of the complaints so common if taken once
or twice a week, to keep the system in a
healthy condition. They are purely vege-
table, and their action is not accompanied
by any nauseous or griping sensation, but is
mild and gentle. For all complaints arising
from Imperfect Digestion and Impure Blood,
Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills are unex-
celled.*
