SPORTING
NOTIONS
The first appearance of Paul Til, the
French lightweight, in Sydney, was an unqualified
success, despite the fact that he lost his initial
battle in the Commonwealth. His appearance was so
fair and warlike, his fighting so clean and chivalrous,
the way he took his punishment so manly and
debonair, that at one bound he achieved hero-worship
—the popularity that means a big crowd every time
he fights. What more could the heart of pugilist
desire? And all this he did in the face of battery
and defeat. The crowd cheered him as his fortunes
sank; it cheered his wife when she left the Stadium
in a depressed state; and it cheered him madly
when big black Sam McVea carried him from the
ring on brawny shoulders. Every time the plucky
Frenchman went to his corner, apparently in a bad
way, the admiring crowd gave him a cheer for luck.
What made the Gaul’s battle look so clean was the
low-down scrap which Herbert McCoy put up against
him. It was not the fight of a good sportsman,
put the roughing, tricky scrap of a pushite, and a
pushite in a tight place. Heaven only knows why
he did it —there was no necessity for it. He had
the advantage of weight and reach, and most of the
time the fight went his way, yet he repeatedly hit
Til with the heel of his palm, held and hit simul-
taneously, and earned disqualification at least twice
for this despicable manoeuvre. Til’s cheek had been
split by a smash of McCoy’s head on the cheekbone,
and thereafter McCoy aimed steadily for the place
that had been wounded, not legitimately, but by
accident. Also, McCoy roughed and wrestled and
tried to exhaust his man by hustling him round the
ring and swinging him off his feet. For once, referee
Arthur Scott must have been dreaming, wool-gather-
ing, or something, for his handling of the fight was
far from satisfactory.
Til’s style is diversified. He suggests a wrestler
more than a boxer when sparring for an opening. He
is extremely shifty on his feet, walks round with his
hands at his sides and his eyes apparently fixed on
the ground. On Saturday night he appeared to be
giving away about a stone in weight, but the scales
gave their * tonnage as: McCoy 9st. 101 b., and Til
Ost. 4.1 lb. But in this matter it must be remem-
bered half a stone between lightweights is a much
bigger difference than a stone among heavyweights.
Although McCoy scored on points from the middle
of the second round, he had several narrow; escapes.
Til carries a knock-out in either hand, and it missed
McCoy .by the decimal of an inch more than
once. The blood from his cut cheek obscured the
sight, of one eye and spoiled his timing. The end
came! after the ninth round. McCoy had roughed
and roughed, and once in a clinch swung him off his
feet, and Til limped to his corner. Finding his ankle
had gone the towel was thrown in, and the badly-cut
Frenchman was carried from the ring on the shoul-
ders of Samuel McVea.
* * * *
A fight crowd is just as easy to stampede
as a Sunday-school gathering, it seems. After the
battle at the Mclntosh Stadium on Saturday night, it
whs fopnd that* the supply pf . trains was totally inade-
quate to cope with the herd, which had exceeded
the wildest expectations. So it came to pass that,
from Rusheutter’s Bay to Darlinghurst, there was a
long, moving column of men on the footpath. Trams
went by in a procession, with horse-dragged vehicles
sandwiched in between them for safety, for the rest
of the- street had been commandeered by the horde
of motors, full of howling and shouting fiends, which
roared by at 30 miles an hour. Suddenly, that
crawling column of pedestrians haltpd, a wave-like
motion trembled through it, and then the mass
began to surge slowly backwards. Next, without a
word of warning, a thousand men put down their
heads and ran wildly, madly, for their lives, and
eddied up side streets and into garden gates and on
to verandahs. It only lasted two minutes, and then
they came back, ashamed and angry, and inquired
fiercely, “What the was wrong?” Nobody knew,
and unto this day no man khoweth, so far as this
scribe has discovered.
* * * *
The Rugby League’s sudden-death com-
petition for the Gitv Cup commenced on Saturday
last. It produced at least one big surprise, when
the despised and contemned South Sydney team at-
tacked and overthrew the Eastern Suburbs crowd,
which had just emerged from the big competition
with the premiership in its pocket. And it was no
snatch victory, either, but the win that comes of a
long and stubborn battle, fought out from end to
end. Final score was 21 points to 10. The riot
between Newtown and North Sydney in the same
campaign was also a surprise for Newtown. During
the first half it looked as though North was liable
to crumple right up at any moment, and Newtown
seemed to grow stronger as the game progressed. But
'ncarances are deceitful things, and in the end
Newtown was licked by 15 points to 10. The
wrecking of Annandale by Balmain was expected, and
the men from the Tattered Suburb won with 17 to
five. Glebe put Western Suburbs out of its misery
quickly with 21 to nil!
* * * »
The N.3.W. country team which invaded
Brisbane under the League banner scored again on
Saturday. At half-time the points were five all, and
both sides going strong., The second half was a wild
struggle, in which both sides threatened repeatedly to
score, and N.S.W. actually did it once. This casual
try settled the question an.d made history. It left
N.S.W. roof dog by eight points to five, and a
dispirited'cellar' dog went home.
*•* * *
In the Rugby competition, Glebe again
fell on University, with the usual result, and this
time University went down by 12 points, the score
being 17 to five. This biffed the ’Varsity: out of the
running for the premiership, and left it a matter to
be decided between Glebe and Western Suburbs. The
mill will take place at University Oval on Saturday,
and great will be, the strenuousity thereof. On com-
petition points Western Suburbs leads, having 26
points to Glebe’s 25. This inequality is due to the
fact that, of the 16 matches played, Western Suburbs
either won or lost outright, while on one occasion
Glebe bad a drawn game. It will be a great battle,
for both teams are in excellent fettle, and determined
to win somehow or other. The referee is in for a
hard time, and is getting his coat of mail back from
the armor-scourer’s.
* * * *
The long and glorious run of that Union
team'from Manly Superior Public School came to an
end on Saturday, when the Village bumped one
from ‘Glenmore-road. There was no fancy scoring,
and the Manly crowd was pleased to be leading by
three to nil when close to time. Then there was a
sudden rush, a howl, a scramble, a crowd of dust,
something that looked like an escaped lion, some-
thing . that sounded like all the doors in creation
slamming at once, and Glenmore had scored a try.
Before the staggered Manly crowd could get going
again the whistle blew and the game had ended in a
draw.',
The Soccer struggle proceeded in all divi-
sions, and in the Gardiner Cup, Sydney and Garden
Island met in a semi-final. It was an unconvincing
affair, as after a fearful expenditure of energy the
board said one goal apiece, and each team went away
feeling that Providence had played it a scurvy trick.
Now they have to wear out the ball all over again.
In the Rawson Cup, Balmain tumbled Glebe out of
its chance of the utensil by four goals to nil, and
stepped on to an equal footing with Sydney. For
the Association Cup, Northern Suburbs and Y.M.C.A.
made a drawn game with one goal each. However,
neither of them have any chance of the Mug. That
will fall to either Balmain or West Sydney. In this
riot Balmain has an unbeaten record, having scored
12 wins and one draw in 13 games, and so leads
with 25 competition points to the 23 of West Sydney,
which lost one of its 13 matches. There is a whisper
of a team being raised to tour Britain and the
U.S.A., but the officials say they know nothing of it.
Perhaps the time is not yet ripe.
* * * *
Saturday saw the end of the first round of
Vic. League hoofball, and to decide the ultimate issue
Carlton will meet Geelong this coming week-end, and
South will bump Essendon the week following, the
winners playing off. South and Carlton have both
won 14 of the 18 games, but as the Southern
boots were propelled with extra vigor, they accumu-
lated more points, and so their wearers have the top-
most place and the right to a deciding match if
beaten. As usual, only one of last year’s “final
four” drops out—-’Wood —and Geelong steps into its
place. This is the first time in 15 years that it has
reached that giddy eminence. Only three of Satur-
day’s games counted. South had to beat ’Wood to
retain its lead, and did so with some difficulty, the
ultimate tale being—-South 53 points, ’Wood 37.
Similarly, it was necessary for Geelong to beat Rich-
mond, to keep its fourth place, as it was level with
’Roy on games won, though many percentage points
ahead. So it made no bones about the job, and pol-
ished off the yellow-and-blacks without remorse or
the ghost of a chance, winning by 70 points to 33.
Carlton had no hope of displacing South unless it
snatched 130 points to ’Varsity’s nix, and as the Old
Blues early perceived the feat to be impossible, they
contented themselves with stalling off the Students’
rushes, and casually acquiring enough points to give
them the game—74 to ’Varsity’s 69. Melbourne put
together 68 points to St. Hilda’s 49, but the episode
was of no moment to anybody. The greatest clamor
of the day was at East Melbourne, where ’Roy was
making its last effort. There was just a “possible”
of Geelong being beaten, in which ease a bare victory
would give ’Roy the preference. Also, a 60-point
win over its opponents—the ’Dons—would displace
those hefty warriors, so ’Roy went for all it could
get. Unfortunately, all it got was a six-point wallop-
ing, ’Don’s speed and training standing to it
in the last half, and capsizing ’Roy’s hopes and an-
ticipations utterly and completely. A gaudy crowd
viewed the episode, and the keenness of the struggle
moved quite a number to get “into holts,” with more
or less disastrous consequences to their countenances.
On the North Melbourne ground Essendon and
’Scray indulged in a willing, but lop-sided, argu-
ment for the second Ass. semi-final. Honors were
fairlv even up to half-time, but thereafter ’Scray,
possibly realising all of a sudden that it had the
right to a deciding match if defeated, faded away,
and let matters slide to such an extent that it
wound up with only 48 points to ’Don’s 70. ’Scray still
has its chance, but it won’t come till September 21,
by which time' the team will have become slack, and
perhaps lazy, and when it comes to meet ’Don again
or ’Don’s conquerors, the knowledge that it has
already received a sound drubbing won’t inspire it
to any extent. Meanwhile, the question as to who is
to meet ’Don in the “first” final is arousing much
argument. Brunswick and North, having played a
drawn game, were ordered to play it again next
Saturday, but in the interim someone discovered that
’Wick played a man whom the Goulburn Valley Ass.,
affiliated with the League, had just disqualified for
using ungrammatical language to a delegate and
dropping an “h.” Certainly, neither the Ass. nor
the Brunswick committee had been notified of the
disqualification, and the player says he hadn’t been
either; in fact, he says he knew nothing of any
complaint against him, though this statement seems
peculiar in the light of the fact that he was fined £ 1
at Tatura Police Court for using a split infinitive.
Also, an Ass. ’bailer, who plays with a League-
affiliated club without permission, is, ipso facto, sup-
posed to be disqualified for evermore. So Brunswick
stands a chance of losing the semi-final it drew,
though the Ass. may take the sporting view and let
it be played out. It doesn’t matter much, anyhow.
* * * »
The Messengers are mighty hooters of the
inflated sphere. In this season’s competition Eastern
Suburbs has scored 54 goals, of which “Dally”
Messenger has booted 39 and his little brother
“Wally” 10. The Messengers were towers of
strength to their side.
* * * *
Norwood Football Club, which used to
boss things in Adelaide—it held the premiership
five times running, and eight times in 11 years—
struck this year its Sedan, and its Marathon, or
Malo-Y aroslavatz —also the Limit and the Dead
Finish. _ It was playing Sturt for the barren right
to get into the “semi-finals,” by being a poor fourth
on the season’s results. It s-'mply could not hit the
space provided for the purpose. The ball was con-
stantly Thereabouts, but not quite There, and the
end of the amazing struggle was a record in ineffici-
ency—one goal 17 behinds.
* * * *
The hoofballer of to-day gets like the
crusader of old—“a warrior mailed for the
hour of need.” One Collingwood (Vie.) for-
ward, now hopelessly crippled, long wore a
protecting shin shield, while in the later Vic.
League games this season one Carltonian wears
an aluminium shin pad ; a ’Royite a , woven wire knee-
cap protector; a Melbourne ’bailer dons a special
thumb support; another an elaborate steel-plated
body-belt; while straps and ankle-stays are seen on
almost every field. Vet, with every week-end, the
casualty list gets longer.
* * * *
N.S.W. Cricket Association had bight
nominations from which to appoint three selectors.
Another attempt was made to get M. Noble into one
of the places; but the delegates wouldn’t have it,
although president Joe Garruthers’s “bury the hat-
chet” advice was given as a reason why they should.
The gentlemen turned down were Noble, Hopkins,
Poidevin, Howard and Bardsley (selector last sea-
son). An exhaustive ballot selected" Victor Trumper,
J. B. Lane (skipper of the Sydney Uni., last season’s
premier team), and the Rev. E. F. Waddy. If
there’s any tommy-axe to be buried it’s interred
quite deep enough by the selection of one of those
three.
* * * *
Tom Richardson had a tribute, as nearly
as possible comic, paid to his memory, on July
13. The great fast bowler was being buried
that afternoon. On his old ground, Ivennington Oval,
the big match, “gentlemen” against “players,” was
going on. As a last sad tribute, the “gentlemen”
and “players” called a halt from 10 minutes to four
till 10 past. But instead of spending the 20 minutes
in tears they went to tea.
* * * *
“An” England Eleven coming directly
after “The” England Eleven on the Australian
cricketers’ programme, suggested something like a
consolation stakes, but though the wickets were
pitched hard by Norwich Cathedral, in search of the
divine blessing, the same old deluge happened.
Gregory and Co. drew with an opposition that
was decidedly of the “scratch” order, and included
has-beens such as K. L. Hutchings, B. J. T. Bosan-
quet (who invented the “Bosie” ball) and Walter
Brearley (sometime Fogland’s best “express”). After
a blank first day Australia totalled 136, Bardsley
actually smiting 47, with much enterprise. Sundries
(29) was the next best. Brearley took one wicket
(clean bowled) for six, and that one was Macartney’s.
Whitty and Hazlitt crushed through “An” England
Eleven, the former bagging seven for 40, and the
latter three for 25. The total of 79 included 14
sundries. In again, Australia declared at six for 79.
“An” XI wanting 137 to win lost four for 68 (Sun-
dries 13) when Time ran out. Whitty took all four
for 29; Hazlitt none for 15; Macartney none for
11. Queenslander Maclaren, who hadn’t played for
a month, was in the team, but didn’t bowl.
* * * *
Cricketer Macartney has had offers of a
“stiddy job,” with cricket trimmings, from both
India and England. Up goes the wail about The Gap
that will be Hard to Fill in Australian cricket.
Rabbits! A poor compliment to Australia’s teeming
reserves 1
* * * *
The final round of Sydney baseballl pre-
miership struggle on Saturday was a sorry business
for the leading teams. Burwood, which had sat
dejectedly on a very low rung of the ladder, sud-
dently sprang to its feet with a loud howl, and,
rushing like an army of Philistines on University,
slew it with 16 runs to 14 in a six-innings’ struggle.
Lane Cove got on its legs and clawed Leichhardt by
two to one in nine innings. Leichhardt and Univer-
sity still lead, with 22 points each, so an extra
final will be played off on Saturday to see which
shall occupy the throne, and wear the crown of
imperishable glory, and kiss Polly.
* * '* *
That the Victorian Inter-Club Cycling
Association has got a big grip on the road racers of
Victoria was demonstrated by the success of the
Cycle Traders’ Annual 100 Miles Race, the sixth of
the series. The event was run off in splendid weather
and over roads that were better than usual, but was
not altogether too fortunate in its working out. One
rider sustained a smash which resulted in concussion
of the brain. It is feared the base of the skull is
fractured, and at time of writing he is still uncon-
scious. Then a protest was lodged against the first
five men to streak home, on the ground that they
had the benefit of outside pacing—which, if it is
proved to be true, will drop them into outer dark-
ness. The winner, pro tem., till the protest is
upheld or dismissed, was E. Malley, who went out with
30min. handicap, and fled or shailed over the dis-
tance in shr. 22min. 45sec., and thereby, provided he
isn’t hit in the neck with the acquired the
cheque for £3O. The other four men who are await-
ing glory or disqualification are J. Barker (who won
a Thistle bike), T. J. Mills (winner of a Clifton
jigger), G. Cameron (temporary owner-elect of a
Barb, “spider”), and A. Treble (prospective proprie-
tor of a £5 ss. cheque). They were all long-
markers, and the best the scratch men could do was
to whirl in sixth and ninth. The fastest time was
put up by G. W. Bell, the scratch man, who finished
sixth. He bolted over the distance in shr. 3min.
14sec., and, in the event of the protest being upheld,
will be winner, and grab the £3O cheque. It was
a great race but a sad ending.
* * * *
The massive Dunlop Shield has found' a
permanent abiding place at last, in the locker of the
Western Suburbs Cycling Club (Sydney). The
club had previous legs-in in 1907 and 1909, and after
defeating the holders of the premiership in the
first round, tore vigorously over the remainder of
the foe, and in the final filled first, second, fourth
and fifth places, thereby losing 12 points only to
Eastern Suburbs’ 24. The 30 miles were spun over
in 80min. 25sec. —-a tall pace, which left no time
for a drink on the way.
* * * *
The start of the S.A. racing season by
the Adelaide Club’s Grand National meeting was a
big success. The youngsters’ race, Fulham Park
Plate, went to a Machine Gun filly, Rapine, and the
newly-instituted Guineas to R. M. Hawker’s Tadanga
(by Pistol —Cardinal and Black). First and second
in both events were fillies. Of- the big jumping
races, Bowman’s Hardrada won the Hurdles, and
Gregory’s Lockwing the Steeple. Over £43,500.
went through the totalisator in the two days, and
the dividends worked out thus: For winners, £l6
35., £9 165., £9 135., £2 Bs., £3 135., £l9 Is.,
£l3 165., £9 155., £4 odd (three times), £23
25., £3 Bs. and £l2 2s. But it is over the second
horse that backers per machine score. This
meeting showed dividends of £7, £B, £lO (twice),
£29, and £63 12s. for the beasts that nearly ,won
but didn’t. How much does Alexander Phelim
Galilee, the bookie, pay on the animal that nearly
wins but doesn’t?
* # * *
The illicit betting lists issued nowadays
are a lovely satire on the anti-gambling law
which prohibits newspaper publication of the
odds. These lists are for the information
of punters who have practically made up their
minds to back their fancies at the best price
obtainable, and who, in former days, would have
been protected, in a measure, from the rapacity of
the bag-carrier by newspaper criticism of the betting
market. For instance, the absurdity of the following
figures would have moved even the daily press to
scornful comment. They are taken from a sheet
issued last week by a very, big bookmaker, and repre-
sent the “cream” of the illicit betting market. Thus,
entries for the Melbourne Sta,kes are priced:—
3 at 14 to 1 12 at 33 to 1
3 at 16 to 1 21 at 50 to 1
10 at 20 to 1 97 at 66 to 1
9 at 25 to 1
and upwards. Twenty-five horses are quoted at not
more than 25 to one, 37 at not more than 33 to one,
and 58 at not more than 50 to one —six weeks before
the race. The other 97 gees may be discarded
for the sake of argument, though it is quite possible
that one of them may be the winner. We will con-
sider only the 58 entrants quoted on last week’s list
at 50 to one, or much less. Two-thirds of that lot
will probably be scratched before the day—but no
matter. If John Jones backed each of them for £1
at list price, he would lose £7 for certain if a 50 to
one chance won, £24 if a 33 to one chance won, £B2
if a 25 to one chance won, and £43 if a 14 to one
favorite scored what the papers called “a punter’s
triumph.” But if John Jones induced his friend
Jim Brown to open and close a one-man tote on the
race, and backed each of those 58 horses for £l,
what would happen? Brown would draw £6 for the
tote’s commission, and Jones would get back £52
of his money. Consider the bookmaker, what a
philanthropic “convenience” he is 1
* * * *
Greenhorns are tempted to go pony-racing
when they read this sort of stuff headlined in the
papers: “Ascot Races. Great Day for Backers.
Killorn Rides Four Winners.” Ascot being the Mel-
bourne suburban course where John Wren holds
sway, ahd Killorn being the crack horseman thereof.
In six races he steered four favorites to victory and
rode two losers. The four favorites started at five
to four on, seven to four on, six to four against and
five to four against. The other two winners, ridden
by other jockeys, were a second favorite at four to
one and an outsider at 10 to one. So a £1 invest-
ment on Killorn’s six mounts at s.p. would show a
profit of £2 2s. 6d. for the day. And this is a
great day for backers!
* * * *
The atmosphere of quiet calm that has
surrounded the preparatory work of the tug-o’-wars-
men at Sydney' Stadium was last week lashed into
wild waves of excitement by the announcement that
the Heekenberg family was entering a team. The
Heekenberg family is generally described as '“The
Parramatta Pygmies.” The weight of the 10 mem-
bers who will haul on the rope aggregates one
ton, which means an average of 16 stone. The
ages range from 21 to 29. It was really to provide
walking space for this family when it came to town
that Allen Taylor started widening the streets of
the city. So now there are over a score of teams
entered for the great hauling contest, and the
Mclntosh, overcome by the sight of so much great-
ness, has dropped another £4O into the bag they
will cut up among them. The final, the great In-
ternational affair, will be a real Babylonian hotch-
potch of races.
* * * *
“0.K.”: Melbourne Board of Health
action re the numbering of seats at theatres has
raised anew the old question as to whether it
wouldn’t be a fair thing for the V.R.O. to number
the grandstand seats at Flemington, and charge a
booking fee of 2s. 6d. on Cup Day. A heap of money
—two or three thousand pounds—could be added to
the club’s revenue by this simple device, and a couple
of valuable races endowed thereby. A time limit for
the reserving of booked seats at that intensely over-
crowded function would be cheerfully recognised, and
a lot of unpleasantness and futile endeavor in space-
hunting avoided. If the booking offices were estab-
lished on the course, and not in town, the railway
traffic would happen exactly as before, for the people
who are determined to get seats would have to go
early to buy tickets; and as for the extra cost—what
is 2s. 6d. to the squanderer of cash on Cup Day?
If it were a consideration to the squanderer, it could
be deducted from the outlay on frocks or bets. An-
other fair thing would be a sixpenny charge for ad-
mission to the Flemington Flat on the first Tuesday
in November, seeing that the I-lenley-on-the-Yarra
autocrats are allowed to reserve a large tract of fiver
bank on their annual regatta occasion. Under pre-
sent conditions, 50,000 people, or thereabouts, get a
view of the Cup race gratis, whilst most of them are
losing several sixpences to bookmakers, who wax fat
on their love of gambling. A “tanner” a head from
those at present unproductive “patrons of the Flat,”
would yield fully £lOOO to the club’s stake-monev
funds. The owners of the gees that give the enter-
tainment are entitled to the hope of a cut in at anv
extra stake money thus obtainable. And Flemington
Flat, treated as a showman’s reserve on only one day
out of the. 365, would remain just as free to the
public as is the soutli bank of Melbourne’s muddy
river. Democracy would suffer no wrong.
* * * *
Conundrum: When is a foal not a foal V
Answer (just supplied by the Jockey Club of Eng-
land! : When it is born dead. At Lingfield races,
there was an event giving a 31b. allowance to a mare’s
first produce,. and the winner was objected to on the
ground that it was not that. It wasn’t, but its only
predecessor had been born dead. This is a nice point,
for on the one hand there is no first at all to take
the allowance, but on the other hand a second foal is
getting the advantage considered right for a first.
The Jockey Club eventually decided that the dead
animal didn’t signify.
* * * *
The necessity of visiting Australia to chal-
lenge for the Davis Cup, has caused much talk in
London and Paris on a question of sporting ethics.
There was no point in England and France both send-
ing teams across the world, so they had a knock-out
match as preliminary. England won, and has now
named its team to come to Australia. But it isn’t
the team that beat France; in fact, France would
probably beat it comfortably. Many think that if
England couldn’t or wouldn’t see the thing through,
it should have stood aside and let France have a try.
It is an arguable point J and, anyway, there is no
proof that the: absolutely best Frenchmen could have
made the journey either.

THE ANNUAL GENERAL MEETING OF THE PROPRIETORS WAS HELD IN LONDON ON
22nd July, 1912.
Mr. H. P. STURGIS in the Chair.
The Chairman moved the adoption of the report and accounts as foUows:—
Gentlemen, —In moving the adoption of the report and accounts, I am glad to be able to congratulate
the shareholders on the excellent results of the half-year under review. After setting aside £30,000 as an
addition to the reserve fund, £lO,OOO to writing down our premises in Australia and New Zealand, and
£23,000 to provide a bonus to the staff, we are able to make a distribution to the shareholders equal to
:14 per cent., and carry forward the substantial sum of £42,766. During the half-year Australia and New
Zealand have continued to enjoy a period of prosperity. The general expansion of trade has continued,
while there is still a steady demand for land, on what would appear to be generally a sound basis. The
wool clip shows a slight increase over the previous year, and producers have been able to obtain satisfactory
prices. The estimates of the wheat crop, which suffered badly in some districts'from want of rain, point
to a considerably smaller yield, but growers have been compensated to some extent by the excellent prices
obtained for their produce. Dairying is an increasing and promising industry, but in the past season has
been adversely affected in several districts through scarcity of feed, owing to want of rain.. As regards
the future, the position may be considered generally very satisfactory. In the early part of this year there
was considerable anxiety on account of the extreme dryness of the season —the drought being especially
.severe in New South W’ales and South Australia. This anxiety was justified to a great extent in the
pastoral industry, as there have been serious losses of cattle, sheep and lambs, and especially of the latter,
and there is little doubt that this must inevitably result in some decrease in next season’s wool clip. On
the other hand, as regards the agricultural districts, splendid and universal rains came in time to save the
crops and ensure their immediate future.
Turning now to the figures:—

A Projected Reform which Seems to
Have Dropped Dead.
About a year ago the Governments of N. S.
Wales and Victoria, in reluctant response to
a press campaign against the laxity of the
authorities in regard to. venereal disease,
made more or less convincing .demonstrations
of an intention to grapple seriously with the
matter. The Murray Cabinet, which was
really more of a Bedstead than a Cabinet,
appointed an advisory committee ‘ to inquire
into and report upon the prevalence ot
syphilis and the best methods to be adopted
to check its spread.” The McGowen Cab-
inet talked gallantly of requiring all the
hospitals in receipt of subsidies to admit
sufferers from venereal diseases as ordinary
patients ; of making facilities for tbe appli-
cation of the Wassermann test available
throughout the whole State; and of seeing
to it that all classes should have full infor-
mation of., and easy access to, the recently-
discovered Salvarsan cure for syphilis. The
Victorian advisory committee, furnished a
staggering report as to the universal preva-
lence of venereal diseases in the then Weary
State. Also, it made a number of recom-
mendations, some of them drastic (for ex-
ample it suggested that the Legislature
should make it a penal offence for anyone
to knowingly communicate one of the dis-
eases in question, or for any person other
, than a qualified doctor to treat such cages) ;
and some of them mild and innocuous ( e.g .,
the proposal that pamphlets on the ‘‘Young
Man Problem,” the Hygiene of Sex, etc.,
should be guardedly circulated). The medi-
cal profession grew interested at the belated
prospect of a reformed condition of affairs.
On every hand,' doctors rose' up and drove
home the grave national danger of allowing
the Red Plague to rage on unchecked. A
; couple of them held forth in St. Paul’s
Cathedral,- Melbourne, on the subject. They
pointed out the wicked foolishness of .the
idea that these complaints are the merited
punishment of sinful ways; a fact which is
sufficiently obvious, since fully 50 per cent,
of the victims are innocent sufferers. It was
mentioned, authoritatively, that 40 per cent,
was a conservative estimate of the number
of young men who contracted venereal dis-
ease year by year ; that 45 per cent, of
sterile marriages were due to this cause, and
that the average mortality among the off-
spring of affected persons during the period
of infancy amounted to 75 per cent. Suffi-
cient hair-raising information was provided
by the profession, in the course of a couple
of months, on the subject of the Red Plague
and kindred horrors to galvanise any Ad-
ministration of energy and imagination and
courage into instant action. However,
neither N. S. Wales nor Victoria has a Gov-
ernment of that sort. Both of them need, as
a matter of course, to be kicked violently
into any move of a controversial nature.
Unless a proposition has votes dinging to it,
they habitually turn it down in accordance
with Rule I. of the game of Party Govern-
ment. There are no votes hanging to any
sincere measure for the extirpation of ven-
ereal disease. On the contrary. As Dr.
Rowland B. Harrold told the S. Aus.
branch of the B.M.A. the other day:—
When the Governments of the earth fully. realise
the contagious nature of syphilis, and place it with
other similar conditions as a notifiable disease, then,
and then only, will we be on the true road to stamp
it out by prevention.
The Government which does its duty to the
extent of making syphilis a notifiable dis-
ease, and penalising those who knowingly
communicate any venereal complaint, will
gain no immediate popularity. Further-
more, it will stand to scandalise and enrage
an immense herd of unreasoning fanatics of
the Wowser persuasion. The politicians
know it, and are acting accordingly. In
brief, the position is that the health of the
community is being sacrificed daily to help
keep the community’s political bosses in
office. But so much that is of value to the
coihmunity is sacrificed habitually in the
name of Party Government that a little
thing like this is hardly worth mentioning.
Move On.
This decision of Sydney Municipal Council
to move on the ladies who congregate around
the drapery emporium windows and gather
a'bout the doors of tony restaurants should
have far-reaching results. It should do more
good than the visit of a dozen Chapzander
missions. Eve’s besetting sin is drapery.
What is it brings the average frail sister to
the dust? Is it love of some gay inconstant
George? Not on your life! It is the lure
of the silken underskirt. That the silken
underskirt rarely transpires does not alter
the fact that it is the lure of it that scatters
the barriers of civilisation and bowls out the
Ten Commandments for a blob. AVhat is it
puts men in money difficulties and starts
them on a course of “dipping” which one
day lands them in the skilly? Is it backing
horses? Don’t believe it! It is the wife s
love of drapery, and when the wretched de-
faulter is asked what he did with the money
he lies bravely, and says “horses.” The
statement is cruelty to animals. Shop-
keepers spend an enormous sum on the dress-
ing of their windows, and a good window-
dresser is worth a heap of money. Why?
Because the destroyer of souls who runs the
show knows that the seed he sows in the
window will bring him a bounteous crop
from the swarm of fluttering doves that
hover round his panes and end by spending
the housekeeping allowance on a duck ot a
hat, or a pearl of a frock, or something ot
that sort. If the police _ hurry on
the woman who stops outside a huge
and well-baited window, the weaker
vessel will go determined to make up
the lost delight at the next one; but
if a vigilant officer is waiting there to
shoo her onward, the chances are she will
reach home with her money intact, bhould
the City Council maintain its austere atti-
tude long enough, display windows will drop
out of existence, and quite a. number of
pale-blooded window-dressers will go on the
land and become brown-skinnod and bearded
producers. Also, the proceeding will largely
assist in solving the Mary Ann question.
At present, Marianne works in a factory
and has a ninepenny tea in town for the
pleasure of tripping round, one of a party,
and looking at the shop-windows. When
the lure of the frock behind the plate-glass
is gone she will commence her reform by
going home and helping mother, and thereby
she will get some knowledge of how to wash
up, if she learns nothing else. And it is
really that question of the washing up that
Civilisation is likely to split upon. Home
life will grow upon her, like any other dis-
tinct novelty, and she will, naturally, set to
work to fit herself for it, and the end will be
marriage and a husband who will have less
indigestion than his mate of yesteryear. And
anything that makes for less indigestion
means elimination of rheumatism, religion
and cold feet —all good things to be got rid
of.
The Foster Fraser type of traveller; —the
sort that scrambles over a country like a
hunted cat going up a chimney, and mee-
yows dismally in one volume —is of no per-
manent use to anyone. He is a distracting
circumstance in national life, and the dust he
scatters in his flight makes people sneeze.
The advice he shouts from the window of
his express train, or mumbles to a reporter
whilst he fumbles with his luggage, is
founded on the little he has read, the very
little he has observed, and the great deal he
has been told by the people who overfed him.
His one asset is purely literary—he knows
how to buckle up poor old familiar Truth
into cheap and astonishing corsets and sell
her in a new print dress. The public realises
that when it has bought and read his book,
and the intellectual dust has settled. The
other sort of traveller is the land seeker who
doesn’t live by writing up the whole round
Earth in a superficial way, but has come
along to quietly inspect the country and look
for a homestead site. His honest purpose is
a guarantee of good faith and thoroughness,
and so, when he speaks, his words are gen-
erally worth noting. It is particularly
pleasing when he waxes enthusiastic about
a region which the small liar has blasted
with his most vicious efforts. The two
Americans who have been calling the Terri-
tory “God’s country,” and asking wonder-
ingly why the Commonwealth Government
does not go ahead with the northern trans-
continental line supply some much-needed
mental refreshment to those Australians
who want to see the maligned place get a
fair deal. A little more independent testi-
mony of this sort from reliable and pains-
taking travellers, and the liars and false
prophets will start to lose their voices.
When the Navigation Bill was first intro-
duced a howl went up from the pro-nigger
crowd in Australia that, unless this measure
was emasculated by exemptions and things,
Port Darwin would be boycotted by the
steamship lines which serve it at present,
and Ruin would materialise. For several
days the Tory dailies spoke of Darwin in
the sad, pitying tones that are employed in
the presence of a dying child of vast pro-
miser Items like the following were fre-
quent :
COLORED CREWS.
NAVIGATION BILL PROPOSALS OBJECTED TO.
HOW NORTHERN PORTS WILL BE AFFECTED.
Sydney, August 6.
Much concern is felt by residents of Darwin and
other northern ports at the prospect of the regular
steamer service being discontinued, owing to the
provision in the Navigation Bill now under considera-
tion. Etc., etc.
Latterly, Darwinites have been taking a
hand in the discussion, and it turns out
that they are not concerned at all. It is
known that, in consideration of a reasonable
subsidy, the two companies which now call
at Darwin are perfectly willing to make
their vessels all-white. There is no doubt
that a Government which represents a
people which has declared almost unani-
mously for a White Commonwealth will
meet the shipping people in this matter. It
is, of course, on the cards that the shipping
co.’s demands may be unreasonable. But
then it is also on the cards that the Gov-
ernment may take the obvious remedy and
provide for the needs of Australia’s North-
ern port by means of a nationalised service.
Sp, taking one thing with another, the Dar-
winites who have intelligence enough to
matter are not worrying.
Perhaps you have some special engagement, have
a headache and afraid to go. You needn’t be.
Stearns’ Headache Cure makes the going possible.*

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will be
taken of communications insufficiently stamped. Name
and address must appear on MSS. No liability is
undertaken re contributions voluntarily submitted,
whether sent by post or handed in; and no MS. will
be returned unless stamps accompany.
John L.: You have a fine capacity for
passion, John. A man who is capable of working
himself up into such an ungovernable rage over the
discovery of one alleged chestnut in a paper which
is inundated with gags day and night, should do
something really notable (say) on being publicly
kicked, or having his beer filched from him, or some
genuine wrong of that sort. Think over it, John.
And, John, how many thousand people do you think
a paper would have to employ so that it could be
sure that, among them, they would remember every
joke that had ever been published, and so bowl out
every fraud who tried to sell a chestnut for cash ?
Consider that, John. Of course, we have only your
wo-d that that jest was a chestnut, and you may be
a liar, John. Also, John, you may be a lunatic.
\ve ....ve Known various lunatics named John....

THE SPY.
A Jap spied out our district when I was a lad.
I didn’t understand then —1 oft wished since I had.
He inquired for a water race, brooklet or creek,
And scann’d out the country from each mountain
peak.
He slept close by in an old empty house;
I crept to the window as quiet as a mouse,
And there on the floor was the business in hand.
He steadfastly gazed at a plan of the land.
A log pressed to the door and a fire burning bright,
Little he dreamed I watched him that night.
He’d conquered our country, no doubt, in his mind,
When the soldiers should follow where he had out-
lined.
I’ve long since grown up, still I’m sorry to say
To know he carried those papers away.
Though now ’tis too late to hold him to scorn,
Still the day of invasion isn’t yet born.
So profit, my friends, while yet you may,
Or the curse of his color may come here to stay.
(Note. —A Jap passed through in 1902.)....

work on a small thing first, and polish it, and boil
it down, and then boil it up again, and condense it,
and then polish it again. After that boil it some
more. (The present writer doesn’t do this sort of
thing, but then he’s too old to reform.) .... G.J.H.:
Mercy is several cows if she must be exercised on
your behalf. You are fined two drinks or the falling
down of the Court. . . .A.C.8.: “A Hidden Indiscre-

Kipling, in his new short story of “2150
a.d.,” gets in a neat one sideways at the land
of roasted Ham. Chicago, being casually
mentioned, is remembered only as ‘ the little
place where there’s Salati’s statue of the
Nigger in Flames. A fine piece of old work;
they only unveil it once a year.”
“Chasuble” : The new volume of National
Biography includes Bishop Barry, of Syd-
ney. He had the ill-luck to send before him,
in another vessel, all his library, sermons,
lectures, literary stock, and preaching tools
and utensils. The ship was lost, with the
whole whack. Another eminent cleric had
his house burnt out and exclaimed: Im
ruined! All my sermons gone.” However he
found he could preach better without them.
Barry was not built that wav. The poor
fellow’s loss was irreparable. He had been
disappointed in missing the succession to
Dean Stanley, at Westminster Abbex The
Bishop might have got on in Sydney but tor
his eyes being in the back of Ins head, to-
wards the Cold Land. Common complaint.
Let’s breed our own Bishops and Arens.
Give the sin-shifting private the marshals
baton in his knapsack. He won t mind star-
vation screw if the gaiters, shovel hat and
prospective bingy are at the top ot tne
greasy pole.

HAINES —SPLAIN —ROBERTS —
GUEST.
DURSUANT to an Order of the High Court of
1 Justice, Chancery Division, made in the matter
of Re ANN HAINES, Deceased, PEACE i v.
ROBERTS (1910, 11. 2483), whereby the following
inquiry was directed, namely: —
Who are the persons entitled by virtue of or ac-
cording to the Statutes of Distribution to the estate
of the intestate Ann Haines, Deceased, living at the
time of her death, and whether any of them are
since dead, and, if so, who are their respective legal
personal representatives?
NOTICE IS HEREBY GIVEN, that William
Haines and William Roberts, the Younger, and all
persons claiming under the intestacy of the said Ann
Haines, and the children (if any) of Jane Splain
(nCe Haines), if she died before the 22nd March,
1909, or the issue or legal personal representatives
of the said William Haines and AVilliam Roberts, if
they or either of them died after the 22nd March,
1909, and the brothers or sisters of Ann Haines
(formerly Guest), or their children, if living on the
22nd of March, 1909, or their legal personal repre-
sentatives, if anv died after that date, are per-
sonally or by their solicitors, on or before the 2nd
dav of December, 1912, to come in and prove their
claims at the Chambers of Mr. Justice Joyce and Mr.
Justice Eve, Room 696, at the Royal Courts of
Justice, Strand, London, England, or in default
thereof they will be peremptorily excluded from the
benefit of the said order. _
Monday, the 16th day of December, 1912, at 2.15
of the clock in the afternoon at the said Chambers
is appointed for hearing and adjudicating upon the
claims.
Dated the 26th day of July, 1912.
A. KEEN, Master.
PEACOCK and GODDARD, 3 South Square,
Gray’s Inn, London, W.C., for Rickerby and
Son, Cheltenham.
NOTE. —The above-named intestate, Ann Haines,
died a spinster on the 22nd March, 1909.
The said Jane Splain was the daughter of James
Haines and Mary (Cove), his wife, of Cirencester,
Gloucester, and is believed to have married - ■
Splain, a schoolmaster, of. Minety, Wilts, and to
have gone to Ireland, and is believed to be dead.
The said William Haines was the son of James
Haines and Ann (Guest), his wife, of Cirencester,
Gloucester, and went to the United States of America
in 1851, and was in Ohio in the years 1861 or 1862,
since when he has not been heard of.
The said Ann Haines (formerly Guest) was mar-
ried to James Haines at Circencester, Gloucester, in
the vear 1825.
The said William Roberts was a son of William
Roberts and Ann (Haines), his wife, and was born
at Charlton Kings, Cheltenham, in the county of
Gloucester. He went to New South Wales about 40
years ago, since when he has not been heard of.

Business, Robbery, etc.
That the Victorian Wages Board, which has just
fixed the minimum wage for commercial clerks, didn’t
err on the side of mad extravagance, and the lavish-
ness of Heliogabalus, is shown by the fact that, while
the Clerks’ Union disapproves of the general deter-
mination—4Bs. per week —the Vic. Employers’ Fed-
eration cordially approves of it. The varying hours
constituting a week’s work will also create dissatis-
faction —clerks in retail shops, factories, mills, etc.,
48 per week; wool clerks, 37 hours from March to
September, 40 from September to March ; all others,
per week. The aggregate employers’ appreciation
of this determination is understandable, but they will
probably realise presently that the general offer of a
shilling" an hour will bring them few employees other
than the wasters and derelicts of the community,
especially when it is recollected that the wages for
juniors—practically apprentices—start at 10s. per
week at 15 years of age, and rise gradu-
ally to 40s. at 20. The Board affirmed
the principle of equal pay for the sexes —•
though typists and stenographers are not yet dealt
with- —anil how that will work out remains to be seen.
Anyhow, £2 Bs., though a fair present-day living
wage for a single woman, is an impossible sum for a
married man.
* * * *
A recent London bankrupt—one Arthur Ortesby—-
has included among his assets his prospective share
of £11,000,000, which certain Jesuits are alleged to
have buried at or near Sacanbaya (Bolivia), in 1767
and absent-mindedly left in the ground. The bank'
rupt, it seems, is a shareholder in a syndicate which
is looking for the treasure, and hopes,'should it find
the goods, to get out of Bolivia alive and solvent.
It looks like the poorest spec, on record. If the
Jesuits were the sort of folk who were in the habit
of losing the run of £11,000,000, or even 11 bob
through sheer mental oblivion, the Order would never
have put up the history it has done. And if Bolivia
could afford to let any syndicate get away with
£11,000,000 it wouldn’t be the penniless place it
is.
“Nauticus”: The Merchant Shipping Advisory
Committee to the British Board of Trade, reporting
on the Titanic disaster, made some important re-
commendations, one of which was :
“Two efficient hands to be allowed for every boat
that puts off from a vessel.”
When the above is made a statutory obligation—as
it, of course, will be —a tremendous ' dust-up will
occur in the British mercantile marine. The British
seaman of to-day is not the article,..he was 20 years
ago. The growing infrequency of the windjammer
has evolved a new and inferior type of white sailor.
And if the efficient British “seaman” is rare, the
efficient colored, one is rarer still. It is quite pos-
sible that public opinion will insist on the regula-
tions providing for efficient white crews for every
boat. Public opinion will never accept a colored
boat’s crew as efficient, even if it is declared to be
so by Act of Parliament. This will hit the P. and
O. hard. Nor wall that affluent co. suffer alone. All
the steamers trading between Adelaide and Asia—
the Currie line, the 8.1., the E. and A. and others—
don’t carry a handful of white seamen amongst
them.
* * * *
“N. or M.”: Tasmania’s old pet incubus, the
V.D.L. Co., seems to have collided with the public
interest even in its callow youth. Witness a reprint
from tne Colonial Advocate of 1828, at which time
the co. had been in existence a couple of years. The
Government of the day allowed the affair 'exemption
from quit rent when its expenditure in assigned labor
reacned a certain sum —£2000, i believe; each
man being reckoned at £l6 annually. The area of
selection was vaguely limited to land on the N.W.
of Tasmania, “several days’ journey from the out-
most settlement,” and the co. gathered up a nice
little lot of 250,000 acres, including some of the
finest agricultural land in the' country. The “sev-
eral days” naturally caused trouble. There b(ing
no roads to speak of, a day’s journey depended
greatly on the character of the country'. Governor
Arthur gave his decision that it should be considered
the distance reasonably covered if a road were built,
and even that left the clause as full of loopholes as
a length of wire-netting. The Government’s undue
generosity in the matter of foreshores also proved
a boon to the legal profession some ■ years ago.
Jorgen Jorgensen, the adventurous gambler who
staked money, life and reputation indiscriminately,
and rang the changes between the job of President
of Iceland and a convict’s cell, was at one time a
servant of the co., and did good work in exploring
the country. _ Headquarters were at Stanley, then
known as Circular Head. It is approached by a
long, low-lying neck of land edged by a curve of
white beach, at the end of which rises the solid bulk
of the “Nut,” looking, as Captain Cook prosaically
described it. like a plum cake standing in the sea.
The winds blow there continually, the trees lie at
right angles, and the little white town has crept
un under the lee of the “Nut.” to avoid being swept
off the landscape. Nelson’s flag-lifeutenant, who sig-
nalled the fleet that “England expects ev.erv man to
do his dutv” ("having, by the way, to spell the last
word flag by flag, as there was no special flag for
“dutv”) found a wife there. Her home was still
pointed out (unless the wind has blown it away
since last year)—an eccentrically-built dwelling,
"•ith the rooms radiating from a central chimnev.
The town runs a weekly news sheet, though “runs” is
rather an energetic word to employ in the connec-
tion. Communication with the outside world is
supolied bv the Burnie and Stanlev coach and the
little steamers that run to and from Melbourne.
Altogether an interesting little place, and pictorial,
but bloodless —strangled bv land monopoly.
THE PUBLIC DEBT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Memo, for the benefit of whom it may concern:
The N.S.W. bill for interest on the public debt is
now just £406,821 per annum more than it was
two years ago.' —B. 15/8/’l2.
The glad years come, the sad years go—
And round, and round, and round go we !
But whether Time flies swift or slow,
He bears with him our £ s. d. —
And one thing we may not forget,
The Public Debt —the Public Debt!
We pray for rain, we pray for health,
For luck, for virtue, and for bread;
AVe pray perpetually for wealth —
AVhich is quite useless when we’re dead !
The one enduring thing we get
Is Public Debt—the Public Debt!
A hundred years are but a span—
They pass like some uneasy dream.
A tiny chip indeed is man
On History’s tremendous stream!
But in that stream one rock we set —
The Public Debt —the Public Debt!
Administrations rise and fall,
Like Tweedledum and Tweedledee,
But from their graves the old loans call,
Right jovially to You and Me:
“We are alive and growing yet,
The Public Debt —the Public Debt!”
Cold rage possesses us at times,
When we reflect that these Old Loans
(Out great-grandfathers’ shameless crimes!)
Will grin and mumble o’er our bones —-
We fade before the years’ slow fret,
But not the Debt —the Public Debt!
Thrones, dominations, kingdoms, powers,
Festivals, fights—all pass and flee!
But this Eternal Debt of ours,
Faints not, nor fails —alas! that we
Should feed, and nurse, and guard, and pet
The All-Devouring Public Debt!
N.S.W.

HOW TO ELECTROLYSE SUPERFLUOUS HAIR
WITHOUT GOING TO AN EXPERT.
Some ladies in this country have lately heard
from Mme.- Tensfeldt of 120 a Princes Street, Ed : n-
burgh, Scotland, the well-known expert, that she has
perfected a s'df-acting apparatus which any lady cun
use for herself, in her own room. It will destroy
the roots of every hair. There is not the slightest
possibility of the hair growing anew, and the thing is
quite easily done. It causes no pain and needs no
skill. A lady has only to sit before a mirror in a
good light and select the first hair which she proposes
to eradicate. In about five seconds the hair either
drops out, or can be brushed off by a touch ; and it
can never grow again. So with every hair on the
face or arm. Mme. Tensfeldt guarantees satisfaction.
The apparatus costs £6 55., carriage paid, and can be
easily sold second-hand when done with. Mme. Tens-
feldt will take it back within six months and re-
turn £1 ss. for it if desired.*

IN
BANANA LAND
One after-result of the late General Strike
in Brisbane is that, out of 2000 shop assist-
ants employed in the city, only about 800 are
now enrolled in the unions. Previously, the
big majority of them were qualified union-
ists. And the railway employees are at
present dead against any movement fpr the
affiliation of their various organisations with
■ any outside body.
Announced recently that the Common-
wealth Government contemplates establish-
ing a horse-breeding station in the Northern
Territory, for the purpose of raising army
remounts'. The institution in such a remote
region (with no present prospect of railway
communication with the South) will be ex-
ceedingly useful, to any foreigner, Jap or
otherwise, who may occupy the northern
shores of Australia.
: “Kobbi”: W. M, Thompson, one-time
Mayor of Brisbane, was one of the band of
gold-seekers that rushed Australia in the
50’s and 60’s. He did no good as a miner,
and started as a bridge and railway con-
tractor. Various short branches, also sec-
tions of Bananaland’s old North Coast rail-
way, were built by him, and when the t>3
flood-torrent carried away everything it
struck and fled seaward with bridges in its
hair, Thompson got the job of replacing
what there was left of the old wooden
straddle across the Mary River. Later,
growing affluent, he became a Mayor, and
ultimately developed into W. Murray Thomp-
son. Now he’s dead.
As recently as 18 months ago the Queens-
land Government was full of enthusiasm
about the projected western line —a huge
work which was to extend in a north-west-
erly direction, joining the ends of the rail-
ways that spread towards the sunset from
Brisbane, Rockhampton and Townsville re-
spectively. It was to be joined on to the
Sydney-Bourke track on the south. On the
north it was to touch the N. Territory bor-
der, and then the Australian Government
was to extend the line from Port Darwin
to join it. Thus there was to be a great
road from Port Darwin to Sydney, and all
the principal Queensland ports were to tap
the traffic en route, and they were to annex
the business of the Territory, and Adelaide
was to be left out, and the Federal line from
Port Augusta to Oodnadatta was to be a
bad debt, and everything in the garden was
to be lovely. Eighteen dray-loads of money
were to be borrowed for the undertaking, and
the consequent applause was to tear the
skies asunder. Now, alas ! all this glory is
indefinitely postponed. The spirit—which is
Digby Denham—is willing, but the flesh—
which is Bull, who was to lend the cash —
is weak.
The luckiest politician in Queensland is
Jimmy Blair, who now succeeds Ken Grant
in the Digby Denham Ministry His ability
is nothing paralysing, and he has the tired
feeling in a chronic form. His best assets
are a big voice and an impressive way of
delivering platitudes. At the last elections
he overtopped Ryott Maughan for the
double-barrelled Ipswich constituency, though
Maughan worked as strenuously as James
loafed. As an instance of Blair’s languor,
according to a return recently tabled, he,
though in excellent health, and all the time
resident in Queensland, had only made 14
appearances in the Assembly during the pre-
vious session.
“Boswell”: The Denham Government
showed the yellow streak in its nature by
sooling its police upon W. N. Gillies, re-
cently elected in the Labor interest. While
qualifying for his 12 months’ citizenship,
Gillies had crossed the border into N.S.W.
to dispose of some property,, and when apply-
ing to be placed on the electoral roll, didn’t
deduct the month or so ./of his absence. It
was no case for a criminal court, anyhow,
and a jury at Ilerberton promptly acquitted
the member for the district. Gillies was
formerly a cane grower at Tintenbar, on the
Lower Richmond (N.S.W.), and ran against
W. M. Green —since blossomed forth, or
blown, or swelled, or something into Massey-
Green —for the Richmond Federal seat. He
put up a plucky fight against Perry, of the
water-bottle, for the Richmond State seat,
and now a political door has opened for him
in the North, which Denham’s officials
haven’t succeeded in closing.
IN THE STEAMER SMOKING ROOM.
BRIDGE FIEND (looking for a fourth):
“Do you play Bridge?”
CAUTIOUS PASSENGER: “ Well, I
do , but a lot of people say
I don't.”
Prison Comptroller Pennefather protests
strongly against the ridiculous system of
sending a hopeless inebriate to prison for
two or three months, and then letting him
loose again to go back to his old public-house.
Pennefather argues, quite logically, that the
indeterminate sentence system is peculiarly
suited for the hopeless beer class, which
should be segregated until expert testimony
shows that the patients have been cured of
the desire to lead a tight life. There is
sense in this. Inebriety is clearly a disease,
that calls for lengthy treatment, not for
brief leg-irons and sudden oakum.
Federal legislation so maddened one “Tu-
wong” that he broke out in Brisbane
Courier with a poem or ode or epic of about
120 lines headed “Australia’s King, Empire
and Flag.” Some fragments
The laws which we endure cause world-wide mirth;
Instance, prospective bonus for each birth.
And, though Australia brought him o’er the seas,
Once here, the legislator thinks his ease
Imperilled if he lets outsiders in,
Considers immigration mortal sin;
Sends French-cum-English British Belcourt out
Because he cannot Spanish, German, spout;
Sends Mrs. Gooey back to far Cathay
By laws permitting her good man to stay.
Avoid the Caucus as you would small-pox ;
Mind what elected you—the ballot-box.
It is curious how loyalty and doggerel go
together—especially doggerel.
The Usurper.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A white god sliding on a star
Went down the purple weather,
lie saw the world beneath him far,
Curled like a misty feather;
And near and nearer did he draw,
His blue robe trailing after,
Till, for the merry thing he saw,
He filled the skies with laughter.
Tlie little people went and came
Amid their swift wheels humming,
And toyed with gold, and played with flame,
Or sets their armies drumming;
And up and down, from sea to sea,
Their splendid deeds they boasted,
Or in their solemn flagons free
Their might and honor toasted.
He saw their little altars stand,
Their priests around them caper,
With offerings borne in every hand
Of cash or blotted paper ;
And sacrifice and sacrament
With curious eyes he noted,
As ever o’er his rate per cent.
Some dull, fat idol gloated.
He saw how each man went austere,
Untrusted of his neighbor,
For that fierce god had made it clear
He owned his life and labor ;
By such strict cords Fat held him bound
That if he did not pay him
The cash that made his godhead round,
One blot of ink would slay him.
Yet here and there, about the world,
Thin outcasts went blaspheming,
The holy incense softly curled
Above bright shekels gleaming;
But Fat, amid his altars proud,
In heavy scorn sat winking—
For who could make his voice so loud
To set the people thinking?
The white god sitting on his star
Filled all the skies with laughter.
To watch that dull, round thing afar
That drew the whole world after;
His mirth was such, a comet fell,
And two young suns were shaken.
To think old Cohen Israel
The throne of Jove had token.
QUEENSLAND.
THARGOMINDAll.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, August 31, 1912.
My Dear Thargomindah, —
We have partially recovered, and a!re
beginning to get glad. Wee Macgregor and
his spouse have come back to us out of the
sad, lone habitations of cow, and the effect
on our spirits has been much like that of a
cheerful moon rising over a sombre paddock
containing several sick sheep. Not that our
vice-regalities have gone anywhere or done
anything during the week, but the mere fact
that they are again in our midst has lifted
us from our gloom and sent a pleasant thrill
through every weary bosom.
We know that, before long, things will get
whirling again, for the G.-G. and his pic-
turesque retainers are to return, and lend a
brief, if passing, glory to our drab exist-
ence.
The National Council of Women held its
annual assemblage here during the week.
Mrs. Kingsbury, who bosses the circum-
stance, is a lady who combines in her kindly
soul an abiding charity with a sense of rol-
licking humor, and as the latter rollicks all
the way through her charitable endeavors,
the N.C.W. is as flourishing as a banana
bush in a shower which contains a rainbow.
Various persons, whose hearts swell in devi-
ous altruistic directions, read reports from
their special departments of activity on the
work of the year. It was wild work, some
of it, including all sorts of things, from
sending colored stockings to the benighted
heatheness to supplying homes for indigent
cats, but good intention was written all
over it.
One well-known lady created a feeling of
mild surprise, mingled with a huge compul-
sion to thoughtfulness, by her statement that
she hired a caretaker, when giving functions
for the young, to hunt, harry or chase the
youth of the country put of the neighbor-
ing bush-covered allottnents where it was
wont to take refuge and group itself in pic-
turesque and promiscuous hiding.
At Bishopsbourne* this afternoon, numer-
ous charitable females disposed of tea, home-
made sweets, and other dyspeptic allure-
ments to other females whose hearts burned
with kindly desire towards Foreign Mis-
sions. Our bachelor Archbishop remarked
genially that some people apparently pre-
ferred their refreshments to his speech, but
this was unkind of him, because quite a
number of good and hungry Christians had
heroically discarded their buns and other
edibles in order to hang upon his words.
The bunny-hugs and grizzly-bears this
week were the “Kismet” assembly on Tues-
. day, the “Orchid” assembly on Wednesday,
and the “Quaker Girl” last night. The
craze for the theatrical variety of hop, skid
and whirl is becoming fierce and devouring,
and the pleasant insanity grows by what it
feeds on.
The University feasted its tennis team
on Thursday evening, and to-night the Arts
students, who are entertaining their fellow-
criminals of the Science and Physics sec-
tions, will allow their visitors to dance in
their hall and eat their supper.
Our University is a glad, gay place, but
it is very, very young. Some day it may
grow up and come to years of humor, but
at present its idea of fun is simply a loud
noise, a.nd its idea of great fun is several
loud noises. The admired sounds are usually
derived from kerosene-tins, squeakers and
toOters; and the police, who are wholly un-
accustomed to this species of comedy, have
been casting an intolerant eye in the direc-
tion of our seat of learning. Still, youth
must have its way; but I confess that the
way of the horse, cow or cauliblossom seems
more cheerful and humorous than the road
it takes at present.
Madam Pfund and Mrs. Willett, two dear
little Melbourne ladies who have wintered
here, gave a farewell tea on Tuesday at
Eschenhagen’s, before leaving for the South.
On Thursday, Mrs. Stodart hostessed a
party in their honor at her home in
Cooparoo.
Mrs. Cowley was another of the week’s
hostesses. On Friday afternoon she gave
a big At Home in honor of her daughter,
Mrs. Gall, and her daughter-in-law, Mrs.
Eric Cowley. One of the chief purposes of
the function was the inspection of the
Cowley grandchildren. Quite a collection
of pasteboard was made, and everybody who
handled the kids or examined their points
from a distance seemed quite satisfied about
them.
Edith Anderson, the pretty daughter of
the late Under-Secretary for Education, who
is an artist of merit, and as nice a girl as
you could meet on a spring afternoon, is
engaged to Penleigh Boyd, an Australian
artist who hails from the South. The male
person who has made the excellent choice
has a lot of a’Beckett and Chomley cousins
in Melbourne. Both parties to the new
agreement are at present in England, but
it was in gladsome Paris that they originally
discovered, their joy. There, Miss Anderson
was studying under Phillips Fox, whose wife
was chaperoning the young Queenslander.
Another engagement which has blasted
the hopes of many a spinster was announced
this week, the man in the case being a well-
known and much pursued preacher of the
Word. If I can remember. I’ll tell you his
name and that of the maiden of his choice,
next week.
Yours breathlessly,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
Dalrymple, who died in Queensland this
week, was the phrase-maker of the Kanaka
party. He it was who saddled Ernie Stevens,
the Courier’s managing director, with the
injurious style of “a withered Adonis.” It
has stuck to the unhappy Ern ever since.
“Briny” holds forth :
Lord Charles Beresford’s protests against the
weakening of Britain’s Mediterranean Squadron should
find warm sympathisers in Australia—so long, that
is, as the Commonwealth has to depend for its
national and commercial safety on the Cold Land.
If Britain is superseded in this quarter, Australia is
in a most parlous plight. And it looks as though
the tragedy was imminent. Eight years ago
Britain had in the Mediterranean 12 of its largest
battleships, four new armored cruisers, and 20
destroyers. The fresh arrangement leaves Malta with
four small cruisers, three of them old, and Gibraltar
with four second-class battleships. It is on the cards
that these may be sent to the Atlantic or the North
Sea. The Malta ships could be sunk by one Austrian
Dreadnought. Australian and British commerce could
then be held up or destroyed, the Suez Canal seized,
and Gibraltar threatened, both by sea and land. The
eastern Mediterranean could not be defended by ships
at Gibraltar. It is 980 miles from Malta, and nearly
1800 from Alexandria. Ireland might as well be
defended by ships at Newfoundland.


Incomparable
THORNE’S o.hjvv WHISKY
THORNE’S OLD HIGHLAND MALT WHISKY won rapidly
a handsome reputation when it began its career 80 years ago.
Each decade has seen this fine old Glenlivet spirit more firmly
established in the public favor—enjoying the unswerving adherence
of men of taste.
Purity, Quality, Smoothness, and Matured Mellowness combine to
produce the inimitable “ Thorne Flavor ” —the flavor which ’has won
for this incomparable spirit its splendid place.
At the King’s Garden Party, during that noble
pageant of modern times—the Delhi Durbar —
Thorne’s was chosen, from among its many
competitors, for the regalement of the King’s
Guests.

A FASCINATING AUTHOR
The Complete
Works of Guy
De Maupassant
Issued to Subscribers only
... by ...
The Standard Publishing Co. Pty., Ltd.,
Publishers of the Complete Works of
Shakespeare, Balzac, Dickens, Scott,
Thackeray, etc.
IT is a literary event of more than ordinary im-
portance that The Standard Publishing Co. are
able to announce the issue to subscribers ot a
complete and admirable translation of the entile
Works of Guy De Maupassant. .
Henri Hene Guy De Maupassant is by wGll-nigii
universal consent the greatest short story writer in
all literature. It was when Baudelaire translated
the short stories of Edgar Allan Poe that the French
attempted to achieve mastery in one of the most
difficult forms of art.
Maupassant and Gautier alone improved upon their
teacher. But Gautier’s chief faipe rests upon his
poems and his romances. Maupassant produced a
series of little masterpieces, which are unsurpassed
for ingenuity of conception, for perfection of detail,
and for an almost miraculous adroitness in the pre-
sentation of character and incident within the tiniest
limits. The unwinding of one of his stories is one
of the fascinations of literature.
His stories deal with innumerable details ot the
human comedy as it presented itself to Maupassant s
ever-observant eye. He has given us strange tales of
travel and adventure of weird mystery and awesome
dread, and of the pure comedy that is never far
absent from human circles.
Only a few of these masterpieces have found their
way into English print. Strangely enough, this is
the first, collected edition which has ever appeared in
England. It has also been left for the Publishers to
make this edition more complete than the authorised
French edition.
This Standard Edition, in fact, contains many
stories and sketches which have actually never been
republished in France, and are now for the first tune
rescued from the oblivion of the. magazines in which
they originally appeared.
THE GENERAL FORMAT. —The Standard Edition
is an endeavor to produce a set of the Complete
Works of De Maupassant, which will conform to the
best dictates of style and art.
THE ILLUSTRATIONS. —There are thirty through-
out the set, from original drawings by noted French
and American artists. A number were executed by
De Maupassant’s friends —who declined compensation
—contending that it was an honor for them to show
their admiration in this way for his works. _
THE PAPER. —The paper used in this edition was
specially made, and is, therefore, of one color and
quality throughout. It is a pure antique, with
deckled edges.
THE PRINTING. —The printing was done from
original type and not from plates. This ensures a
special clearness. A new font of French Elzevir
type, bold and elegant in design and easy to read,
was specially cast for and used in this edition.
There are good margins to every page.
THE BINDING. —It has been our aim to secure
simplicity and quiet elegance in the bindings. The
books are handsomely bound in Blue Vellum Cloth,
with distinctive brown and gold title labels.
ANALYSIS OF THE SEVENTEEN
VOLUMES.
IN THE FIRST FIVE VOLUMES the author pic-
ires, with marvellous skill, the virile novelty of
luntry scenes, and the comedy and tragedy under-
ing the whirl of Parisian life in fashionable draw-
g-rooms, in studios of art, in greenrooms of opera
id theatre, and in the Quartier Latin of students
id grisettes.
THE SECOND SECTION OF FIVE VOLUMES
mtains the Romances which caused Tolstoi to hail
e Maupassant as the supreme realist and romance
riter of his century. Here are fotind the daring
splorations of his subtly intelligent mind into the
notions of the heart of womankind.
THE THIRD SECTION OF FIVE VOLUMES em-
races the Dramas, Poems and Travels, the last-
amed sparkling with gems of description—Meisson-
ier-like pictures in words —of Spanish, Italian, Cor-
can and Oriental life. The shorter Romances are
Iso included.
THE FOURTH SECTION contains stories belong-
lg to no one particular phase of life, unless it may
e human passion. Love and laughter, tragedy and
lars run side by side in these two volumes, the last
E the series.
AN APPRECIATION.
Elbert Hubbard writes: —
“This man never wrote to please the rabble. He
wrote because he had a message for the world, and
because Divine Energy forced it through his pen.
De Maupassant wrote with the conviction that in
life there could be no phase so noble or so mean, so
honorable or so. contemptible, so lofty, or so low, as
to be unworthy of chronicling ; no groove of human
virtue or fault, success or failure, wisdom or folly,
that did not possess its own peculiar psychological
aspect, and, therefore, demanded analysis. If occas-
ionally some intellectual pigmy, after reading,
screams in falsetto, please remember, snontaneity
and mediocrity are never kin. ' A WARNING: Those
of you who find enjoyment in blue-milk brain-wander-
ings should never seek De Maupassant. You will
be disappointed. You will not be able to digest nor
assimilate the strong elixir of his mind, so it will
curdle in your gullet-—then it’s you for mental con-
stipation.”
A FREE BOOKLET.
We have prepared an illustrated prospectus of our
Standard Edition of De Maupassant. This will be
sent Post Free to all responsible inquirers. The
booklet describes the edition in full detail, and shows
how the matter is arranged in the Seventeen Volumes.
This booklet will be sent to all book-lovers and liter-
ary students who use the form underneath.

obligation on my part, your illustrated booklet of

easy instalments.

WOULD NOT HEAT
After Three Months It Seemed to Be
Eating into Bone. Used Cuticura
Ointment. Thoroughly Healed.
Had No Further Trouble.
•‘The trouble [with my little girl’s toe]
first began by a knock the toe next the
big toe. It first became festered and in-
flamed. I then began to poultice and use
boracic acid. After I got the wound clean
and inflammation down I tried to heal it up,
but after three months it was still unhealed
and seemed to be eating into the bone. I
then showed it to a local chemist and he
gave me sorrie ointment which I was to use
with boracic acid. After giving it a fair
trial it did not improve. I then wrote for
Cuticura Ointment and after about a month’s
use the toe became thoroughly healed and I
have had no further trouble. It is thoroughly
cured.” (Signed) Mrs. M. Leach, 96 Cobden
St., South Melbourne, Vic., Dec. 13, 1910.

Those who are troubled with falling hair, dandruff,
baldness, itching scalp, etc., know full well the
humiliation that they cause. There is no need, how-
ever, of such conditions. These troubles can be
auickly overcome by my True Hair Grower.
I am not an advertising quack, but am a business
man. My hair began to grow thin about the time
I reached the age of thirty. When I had reached the
age of thirty-five the top of my head was quite bald.
I had considerable pride, and sought for something
to cause renewed growth. I invested m every pre-
paration that I could see or hear of, and here, let me
say that not onlv did I try all the English so-called
restorers, but those of Continental Europe. I also
visited specialists. My experimenting cost me many
sovereigns and considerable time —yet the hairs came
not I had given up the idea of ever growing hair
again. One afternoon, while enjoying a brief busi-
ness respite by an Alpine trip, I fell in with an old
collegiate chemist, whose hair was particularly pien-
tiful. I jokingly remarked that I wished he could
spare some of his hair for my benefit. This lead him
to tell me that he understood the human hair as well
as a good dentist understands teeth. He wrote out a
formula, and suggested that I have a quantity of the
compound prepared. I did this a few days later. 1
was astonished to see the hair coming forth after
several applications, and within six weeks my head
was covered by a firm, yet soft, black growth of hair.
Since then the haif on my head has been as luxuriant
as when in my school days. Having arranged for
the legal right to do so, I have for some time been
privately selling this marvellous hair-growing com-
pound. I hold over one thousand letters from people
who have actually grown new hair through the
medium of my true hair grower. I do not pretend
to say it is the only hair grower in the world—l am
not interested in decrying others —but I do asseit
that it is the most reliable, and in the end by far'the
cheapest. , .
