The Inky Way
Last month’s obituary list includes George Ather-
ton, for long shipping reporter of the Melbourne
HekalD. If someone were to write an exhaustive
treatise on reporting and reporters, the specialist
scribblers would fill a large place. Mining, shipping,
commercial, agricultural and sporting headings
overlook an inky mass of type, which stands
for any amount of skill, resource, experience
and hard work. Shipping reporters have a
special place of their own, and there is a
fine camaraderie among them. Perhaps the best
known in Australia was Jago, of Adelaide. He was
a statesman among the amphibia;, and his arrange-
ments were facilitated by the nature of the port.
At Port Jackson there has been less room for getting
in fine work, but the coastal reporters, in tne old
days of Maoriland, at the Bluff and Hokitika, were
magnificent. In Melbourne, Millar, of the Argus,
and Clark, of the Age, were held in high repute way
back in the time that patriarchs who draw old age
pensions can just remember. Atherton was the
jolliest chap of the lot. He habitually wore a brick-
dust complexion on his full-moon face, but that
didn’t prevent his getting early copy. The ease with
which he seemed to do things blinded many of those
who knew him to the real smartness with which he
extracted cockboat information and dressed it for the
compositors. #
“L.E.”: I disagree heartily with. “Wonga” re
anonymous journalism. His point is that many
writers with democratic sentiments . attached, to
Tory newspapers would be doomed if signed articles
come into vogue. Good enough for them, say I.
“Wonga” says that with signed articles some of our
best men would be driven into the enemy’s camp.
Aren’t they there now for all practical purposes?
The truth is that if articles were signed, the cause
of democracy would be strengthened. The position
of the good writer would be vastly improved, and if
good writers are democratic they would be strong
enough to; compel even the worst Tory paper to
mitigate some of its worst misrepresentations, or
hire inferior writers. The present type of journalist
has for so long tempered his sentiments to the
market that he has lost all sense of proportion, and
I have always found that the writer without con-
victions is a poor writer. At present even the. best
men count for little or nothing, but with signed
articles the good men would gain power and in-
fluence. Besides, there are more things in the Inky
Heaven and Earth than politics. In literary, artis-
tic and dramatic and musical criticism, it is most
essential that articles should be signed, but it would
be a bold man who would dare. to put his name to
the gush that passes for criticism in most of our
dailies. A man writes, let us say, a book of verses,
and some unknown Writer says it is tripe, or one of
the few volumes of perfect poems in the
English language. "Now it all depends who
says it. If the man who has previously
perused only the works of Ella Wheeler Wilcox
or Nat Gouid or Charles Garbage says the poem is
tripe, that would be regarded as rather a compliment
to the writer. And the dramatic critic who had
written a eulogistic account of the “Girl Who Knew
Something”—certain critics have gone even that
length-—would present himself to a wondering public
as an imbecile, a fit subject for an alienist. We
would then know our men, their standards (if they
had any), and their own peculiar brand of brain-
lessness. And the opinion of the sound men would
begin to matter. Tes, if articles were signed, many
a weighty anonym would find his reputation gone.
* •re * *
“Inky Wafer”; Here’s what I know about the
London Times special advertising editions (B.
15/8/’l2) : These special issues are not so . dear as
they look. Murray Alison, the ex-Australian who
controls the advt. depart, for the Times, has a staff
to deal with monthly puffs. Two of his canvassers
are ex-Australians, who earn big money—more than
they could do rabbit-trapping or standing for Parlia-
ment. Last year the Times got out a South Ameri-
can supplement, and another ex-Australian told me
the revenue was £6Boo—from 58 pp. at £lOO,
and £5OO each for the back and front pages. Two
South American embassies liquidated the .cost of this
hooray. And it pays these Dago Republics to spend
muchly on advertising. An ex-Australian who peddles
Thermos flasks, told me his firm had secured the
front page of the Daily Maid for £6OO for one in-
sertion. They were offered £4OO to surrender it by
a maker of bread pills or corsets —no matter which,
as both impede the digestion. There are only three
daily papers which get these fancy prices in London
—the Times, the Daily Telegraph, and the Daily
Mail. Except, perhaps, the Morning Post, most of
the London morning papers do not yield big profits
to the owners. And there are 7,500,000 inhabitants
in Greater London.
* * * *
“Doctor”: Re Holman’s proposal that all news-
paper articles should be signed by the writers
thereof: Macaulay, who didn’t sign his brilliant
essays in the Edinburgh Review, wrote in Knight’s
Quarterly Magazine, in 1823, concerning the edi-
torial “we”: “It has often been fatal to rising
genius, though all the world knows that it is only a
forni of speech very often employed by a single
needy blockhead.” But, of course, the hired
Petriana mythologists and Marriage: Tie Bursters
haven’t anything resembling Macaulay’s stuff to sign
for.
• * * *
A recent issue of Answers, a London weekly, is
rare in one particular. The whole of its advertising
space is taken up at a big figure by a single enter-
prising firm, which is not going to get another big,
but gratuitous, iidvt. by being, mentioned here.
Answers is the foundations on which the fortunes of
the house of Harmsworth have been built. It was
the first venture of Alfred Harmsworth, now Lord
Northcliffe, principal proprietor of the London Daily
Mail, England’s largest circulator. It is the only
one of the numerous rivals of the popular Tit-Bits
that has caught on and secured a permanent foot-
hold. It succeeded, not b,Y virtue of any literary
merit, but by reason of young Alf Harmsworth’s
remarkable ingenuity in inventing puzzles and prize
competitions, thereby arousing the cupidity of the
vast host of people who dearly love a cheap gamble.
“Kito”: In the long, dead days, when I edited a
backbloeks daily, it was my custom to go shooting
from Friday to Monday—and occasionally the jaunt
extended over Tuesday. Yet the subscribers to the
sheet never noticed the absence of the master hand.
I left behind a collection of leaders, mostly on such
safe topics as the need of dealing firmly with, if not
deposing, the Czar of Russia, and the comps., aided
by my solitary reporter, attended to the rest. But
the chess editor of a N.S.AV. weakly of my acquaint-
ance was the world’s winner in this line. It was
his custom to pay visits of three months and. up-
wards to Maoriland. He never allowed a substitute
to interfere in his job. Before leaving, he made up
a bundle containing 13 weeks’ supply of chess items,
each weekly budget being dated and ready for the
printer. A consideration of his methods convinced
me that chess is like death, which takes no count of
time, and claims all seasons for it 3 own.
A CURSE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
there's a special nook in the utmost Pit
Where Satan simmers the screaming souls,
With sharpened forks and a patent spit
And first-class devils and first-grade coals —
A cosy corner replete with pomp.
It’s all reserved for that cow, the comp.
I write of . “Envy, morose and dark,”
He makes it “ ’Enry,” my heart to break ;
I pen an “Ode to a Life-grief Stark”—
He types it “Hide of a Live Beef-steak,”
I sing “gay ruin in Circe’s cup”—
“Bay rum” is the way that he sets it up!
Ho, brother-bards, who have writhed and chafed
’Neath the heavy hand of the Beast, mark well,
In beer-glad hours there hath been vouchsafed
To me sweet Vision of seething Hell
With one addition to Dante’s plan:—
A special grid, for the lino, man!
VICTORIA.

ARE YOU
DEAF?
If you are deaf, or going deaf, you will be glad
to hear of a simple and scientific device which will
restore, your hearing, no matter how old you are.
there is no mystery or secrecy about it; we describe
it rully and explain just how it acts.
\ou use spectacles as sight magnifiers, and in the
same way you may use this sound magnifier as an
aid to weak hearing. But, unlike spectacles, the
Wilson Common-sense Ear-Drums are invisible and
comfortable. You can’t tell you are wearing them.
No one else can, either, because they are not seen.
They rest the ear nerves by taking the strain off
them—the strain of trying .to hear dim sounds.
What a blessed relief to have done with that
tiring strain !
Being made of the purest and softest Para rubber
they cannot be felt, even when the head rests on a
pillow. For the same reason, a blow on the side of
the head will not hurt the ear. In fact, they pro-
tect the ear, the tender inner parts that are so
senstive, from cold winds, dust, sudden and piercing
sounds.
So that you may clearly understand why these
facts are true, and why the Sound Magnifier restores
the hearing, we give an illustration of one as it lies
in the ear. You will notice that the Sound Magni-
ħ

like a trumpet,

ed to carry the faintest sound right down to the
natural ear-drum.
To persons who are' nearly deaf, it is not only
trying to have people shout at them, but it hurts
the ear-drum. When age comes the ear-drum some-
times thickened, or loses its tightness, and
shouting does not act properly on it. It Only
makes a confused and tiring noise. But the
Sound Magnifiers concentrate the sound in the best
spot for perfect hearing.
* (: °i;rse, if you have lost the hearing-nerve
completely, or were born deaf, we cannot pretend
to cure you. Only a miracle could do that, in spite
ot some of the absurd claims made to-day. But if
you can hear the faintest sound, do not despair
You will never regret trying the Wilson Ear-Drums.
We have letters to show you from deaf people in
Australia and New Zealand, who have been cured,
and you can be cured, too. You may wear the Ear-
Drums day and night, and take them out whenever
you wish. Children can wear them (we have spe-
cial sizes for children from six to twelve). Even
a child can insert and remove them without the
least danger.
The price is £1 (the fee for a doctor’s single
visit). This includes a pair of drums (they
are only sold in pairs), a fine nickel remover and
also an inserter. After the first pair is bought you
may purchase a single drum at any time for Bs.,
but the pair you get at first will last quite
two years. Do not ask for a trial pair. We do
not send them out on trial, and so they are never
secondhand. Use the Order Form below, and the
Outfit will be sent you at once, with the fullest
instructions. Don’t delay, but have the comfort of
good hearing as soon as you can.

THE following case of Mr. John Bath, of
Queenton, Queensland, affords another
typical instance of the prompt and perfect
curative powers of Bile Beans.
Mr. Bath is postmaster at Queenton, and writes :
“ 1 have been using that grand family medicine, Bile
Beans, for the past four
years or more, and have
derived srreat benefits from
it. At one time I suffered greatly from head-
ache. The terrific throbbing was terrible. My
head felt as if it had been crushed in a vice, and
I could not bear it to touch the pillow.
“ I took a dose or two of Bile Beans—a box
of which I am never without—and was soon
relieved of the agonising pain, and after a few
hours I was quite well again. I was able to
attend to my duties as usual, which 1 feel sure I
would not have been able to do but for Bile
Beans. •
“ Bile Beans are a splendid family medicine,
gentle in action and certain in effect, and I
recommend them to anyone I hear complaining
of feeling unwell.”
Mr. J. Bath—grateful to Bile Beam.
THE STANDARD FAMILY MEDICINE.
BLOOD IMPURITIES.
Impure blood poisons the whole system. The complexion and the whites
of the eyes assume a yellowish tinge, and the eyes become dull. The
pores of the skin are obstructed, and (rive rise to pimples, boils, blotches, etc.
Bile Beans act on the sources of the blood supply, the organs of digestion
and assimilation, and so invigorate them as to send a tide of rich red blood
through the veins and arteries, bringing the hue of health to pale checks.

An Apology of Pleasant
Worship.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
HE gods of ancient Greece were fair,
White limbed and clear of eye,
They floated down a stronger air,
And sailed a finer sky;
The smoke of woodland altars dim
Was blue about the world,
And at the far sea’s magic rim
Old clouds of wonder curled.
Chaplet and garland, prayer and song,
The oracle that spake—
The dreams men dreamt were wild and long;
They saw Athene shake
Her deep blue robe across the height,
And young Apollo’s lyre,
Trembling with music of delight,
Filled strong men’s hearts with fire.
When Thor and Odin ruled the North,
The swift Valkyries flew
To. bear the soul that fluttered forth
Baptised in battle dew,
The Thunderer’s hammer smote the cloud,
The red-forked lightning sprang,
A song of courage deep and loud
The warrior shipman sang.
Religion clenched the smiting hand
And drove the battle keel,
Red sacrifice in many a land
Made dark the warrior steel;
A moving splendor in their dreams
Mocked at the gaunt death’s call,
And down the night they saw the gleams
Of Odin’s feasting-liall.
But times are changed. The gods are dead ;
In this commercial day
Men haste to take their prayers as read,
While Wowsers cold and grey
Shake through the world their whiskers pale,
Or into business dash
To run a large salvation sale
On terms of strictest cash.
With ribald Bacchanalian songs
To holy language wed
They save, as with angelic tongs,
The brand from burning red.
While pictures wrought in Tophet flame,
And shrill with Sheol’s scent.
The erring sinner’s heart may tame
And win the penitent.
The gods are dead. Thor comes no more;
Athene’s shrine is cold ;
Apollo’s splendid reign is o’er,
And lost his lyre of gold :
But raging up and down the land
Hired zealots madly prate,
And prayerful persons deftly hand
Round the collection-plate.
But we who might have bowed the head
Wi ! hin the Delphic cave,
Or worshipped him whose beard was red
Above the wild North wave,
With old religious mirth must wake
Some gods our souls to cheer,
And sacrificial offerings make
Of brown and blessed beer.
We arc not irreligious men,
As Wowser folk avow,
The gods are dear to us as when
The first men learned to bow
In adoration at the shrine
Of earth, and sky, and air,
When love was love, and wine was wine,
And all the world was fair.
N.S.W.
GEORGE STREET.
’Tween Drinks.
A PRACTICAL JOKE AT PIBROCH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
PIAYE more than once drawn
your respectful attention to Angus
Trevor,” said Patrick Mackaboy, bar-
man. “Angus was the man who made a
great tragedian of me between breakfast and
7 p.m. of February 11. He caught me in a
blue dungaree apron, chasing the dead flies
and odors of nocturnal dissipation out of
Scanlan’s billiard room at 9.30, and had me
juggling with the paternal curse in ‘The
Golden Farmer, or The Last Crime’ at the
Pipeclay Mechanics’ Institute that same
evening.
“This Angus Trevor was proprietor, man-
aging director and star gymnast of the
Orion Dramatic Organisation. He had been
a very promising young actor for 35 years
when we met, and was the nicest man that
ever unwound a struggling publican for
three days’ board and bar expenses. Those
faithful performers who wriggle out of
masses of iron manacles, and worm their
way through old oak chests do nothing at
all to compare with Mr. Trevor’s bewilder-
ing feat of escaping from a closed pub, her-
metically sealed, with a proprietor on the
mat, all doors in possession of the enemy,
and a printer’s summons waiting on the
front step. I am reserving for my old age
the task of writing ‘The Escapes of Angus
Trevor.’ They will rank with those of
Latude and Casanova.
“It was at Pibroch, New South, we struck
the first and worst frost of my brief but
brilliant artistic career. We were playing
‘lngomar the Barbarian,’ and I may say the
performance was not received with general
favor. The native youth, who grow to a
great height and are uncouth and woolly in
the Pibroch hills, finding small excitement
in the show, set about extracting diversion
from Angus Trevor. They met him in Wes-
ton’s bar parlor after hours.
“Angus was a tall, slight man, with
fluent grey hair, kind eyes, a sensitive
mouth, and an expression of great and abid-
ing sweetness. The ringleader of the leg-
pulling bee was a precocious young cocky,
aged 30, with a small head and some smat-
terings of the art of ironical deception.
His name was Henry Jenkins. He professed
to be animated with respect approaching
reverence on being introduced to the justly-
celebrated tragedian. His hat was in his
hand, he bowed low, and discoursed with
whispering humbleness. His retinue of
grass-fed satirists did likewise.
“ ‘Proud and happy to know you, sir,’
said Mr. Jenkins. ‘lt is indeed a privi-
lege.’
“ ‘Not at all, young gentleman,’ Angus re-
plied. ‘Not at all.’ The old actor was mildly
gratified, but sufficiently modest.
“Henry insisted on it. ‘Believe me, it is
no little thing to have shaken the hand of an
artist whose reputation is so high, and whose
name is a household w T ord from Pibroch tc
the Pacific Slope, and from the Pacific
Slope to Old Bailey.’
“ ‘Tush, tush, my dear sir, you are too
generous. It would be discourteous in me
to decline. A little gin, and just a jet of
peppermint, please.’
“ ‘We witnessed your marvellous perform-
ance to-night. The finest Hamlet ever seen
out of Muddy Water Holes,’ Jenkins pro-
tested.
“ ‘lngomar, Mr. Jenkins —Ingomar.’
“ ‘No matter, it was stupendous.’
“ ‘Sublime !’ said McLoughlin.
“ ‘So scholarly, so restrained, yet pro-
foundly emotional,’ said Gotch.
“ ‘lt should be given in London,’ thought
Herring.
“ ‘Or Tiddywaddy,’ said Mosley.
“They kept at it till two in the morning,
by which time Angus Trevor had consumed
two-thirds of a bottle of square-face, and a
judicious supply of the antispasmodic. He
was still the dear, urbane and ingenuous
elderly gentleman, utterly oblivious to the
fact that anywhere in the whole world could
be found five young men who might take
pleasure in baiting a simple soul like him-
self. He had paid for nothing up to now,
but no one seemed to notice it. All hands
were too busy with his elastic left leg, a
limb susceptible to unlimited elongation, it
would seem.
“Here or hereabout Henry Jenkin sprang
his splendid jest. For three long, sodden
hours he had been leading up to it. He
called for silence, and, assuming a marked
gravity, said :
“ ‘Mr. Angus Trevor, sir, we have not
hesitated as representative men of this dis-
trict to convey to you some idea of the
great respect in which we hold you. We
have endeavored to give you an impression
of the deep admiration we have for your
genius. It remains for us to express the
disgust we feel for Pibroch’s shameful lack
of appreciation of your enterprise in bring-
ing the finest fruits of dramatic art to its
doors. We cowered with shame on behold-
ing the wretched audiences before which
you have played here, and feel ourselves
called upon to do what in our power lies to
redeem Pibroch’s good name. With that
intention, and your kind permission, we pur-
pose starting a subscription for Angus
Trevor, artist, gentleman and public bene-
factor.’
“The suggestion was greeted with clamor-
ous approval by the representative residents.
Angus Trevor was touched. He drew a
handkerchief, and with a small portion
soaked a tear from the left corner of
his right eye. ‘Gentlemen,’ he said, ‘you
stir me—you stir me profoundly. I thank
you. Angus Trevor thanks you from the
depths of his soul. It is true the muses
offer their humble votaries small rewards',
especially in Pibroch. I cannot disguise
from you the fact that such a douceur as
you purpose offering would be most welcome,
not for my sake alone, but for the sake of
those brave, simple, faithful followers who
repose their trust in me. But, good, kind
friends, dear brothers, since it is your inten-
tion to make me the unworthy object of your
beneficence, it would become me best, I
think, to retire at this juncture, and leave
you to plan out your work of humanity.
Good-night, brothers. God bless you!’
“He buried his face in the handkerchief,
and, bursting into tears, made an affecting
exit down C. that would have reminded you
of the late Henry Irving, with a new knot
in his left knee.
“When he had gone the or', ne mover of
the great jest slid to the floor, and
writhed there in an ecstasy of mirth. The
other representative Pibrochs did likewise.
They squirmed silently, for fear of alarm-
ing the quarry. Presently, having con-
torted to the extent the exquisite wit of the
conspiracy seemed to demand, they arose,
and set about preparing the documents.
“The great joke that was to bring glory
to Pibroch was abroad early next day. We
all discovered evidence of it when we walked
Main-road. There was an alertness in the
township previously unnoticed; obviously
the people were calling each other up to
inspect the victims. Trevor was an object
of peculiar regard. He was narrowly ob-
served, but trod the street in gracious
beatitude, erect, grey, grave, a refined and
impressive figure. It was a pity, he drank.
“Meanwhile the subscription list was
going round. I had serious suspicions
regarding it from the first, having had some
experience of the predisposition of way-
back peoples to a kind of dray-horse humor.
They will drag a joke 90 miles over the
hills, and toil at it for weeks, with the
patient assiduity and dumb reserve of
working bullocks, for the poor two-minutes’
gratification they can get out of exploding it
under some chicken-brained, dribble-chinned
drifter, who, after all, in nine cases out of
10, hasn’t wit enough to even discover that
anything unusual has gone off in his vicinity
of late. Being a very subsidiary character
with the co., concerned more with the up-
keep of the horses than scholarly renditions
of the man Hamlet, I was not so much in the
public eye at Pibroch, and. with the assist-
ance of a grafted moustache and a dummv
swag, contrived to get an eye on the said
list, as it lay at Hill’s general store. It
was contrived of several pages of a ledger,
pasted together, and rolled on a scrap of
broom handle, and, as far as I went, it
ran:—
COMPLIMENTARY SUBSCRIPTION.
From, the Admiring Residents of Pibroch to our
Gifted and Distinguished Visitor,
ANGUS TREVOR.
The collected valuables to be Presented to that
Fine Artist on the Occasion of the Last Appear-
ance of his magnificent Orion Dramatic Organisation
in Pibroch Shire Hall.
Henry Jenkins 1 peck fermented tomatoes.
lorn Gotch 3 doz. eggs—feverish.

A. Allard . . 3 hens—dead and in active preparation.
2O dead-shot turnips.
Stephen lliil 51b. corrupt butter.
Angus McLoughlin .... 1 half-bag tuberculous spuds.
And so on and on. Decayed vegetables and
unhealthy poultry were already promised in
sufficient numbers to bog the Shire Hall I
got back to Trevor, and entered into ex-
planations.
“ <i:t ’ s hish . time t 0 P ull out,’ I said.
Ihe.v are putting a nasty joke up on our
grand aggregation of histrioni.c wonders

The Daily Palseontolograph.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Institute of Surveyors, having heard the pre-
meditated plans for the Federal capital discussed, is
reported to have been of the opinion “that the Aus-
tralian capital should be built on an Australian de-
sign-” Why should an Australian necessarily be
chosen f— Sydney Telegraph>*(22/8/T2).
Visiting members of the British Association will
not only impart knowledge, but will also have a
splendid opportunity to acquire it. To the anthro-
pologist Australia is the most interesting country on
the face of the earth, since it contains the sole human
survivors of the palaiithic age.
Same paper, two days later.
OF course, you are all good scientists,
c amerados? One may take that for
granted ?
High, low, medium, ground and lofty scien-
tists —geologists, anthropologists, zoolo-
gists. conehologists, meteorologists, beer-
ologists, feminologists, seismologists,
astronomers, horsologists, quidologists,
icliyologists and palaeontologists?
Particularly palaeontologists—experts and
authorities upon the Science of Fossils !
Especially 'do I address the palaeontological
camerados— the men and brethren who
know all that can be known about dino-
saurs and megatheriums !
Also, do I request the rivetted attention of
those given to studying the manners and
customs of the elosaurs, the dinothe-
riums, the dimorphodons and the giant
petrified jimodactylobaddebtolophodon.
Further, finally and ill conclusion : Such of
yoli as have survived unto this present,
after a course of iguanndon, triceratops,
and Chimera Major, perpend mes
enfants!
Shortly you are to receive, and the miser-
able, unscientific camerados are to re-
ceive, intellectual cavalry of the most
heavily-learned description!
You will be' called upon to repel hoarders
filled to the brim With knowledge of
every misdescription !
They are coming to push forward the fron-
tiers of human knowledge, and human
achievement, and. ill the words of the
Daily Pal.xontolograpii, their pres-
sence must, or should, have an inspiring
effect upon you! (Do you not feel in-
spired, O Boeotians?)
They will impart knowledge to you. and you
shall enter the Halls of Learning and
gaze astonished at its majestic columns
of statistics !
All the -ologists shall have imparted to them
some new knowledge, save only our per-
fect palaeontologists!
Now you perceive, now you appreciate, O
palaeontological camerados, why especi-
ally do I address you !
The plethoric scientists from afar will teach
the mere -ologists and the beerologists.
and the ignorant, who cannot differenti-
ate between a Doctor of Phlebotomy and
a psedobaptist i
But from you, the Imparters of Knowledge,
are to acquire it!
The Daily says so;
therefore, it must be so.
Do you doubt the Pax.eontolograph?. ...
God forbid !. . . .
Very well, then! What are you going to
teach the Teachers?
You are going to teach them that this is the
most interesting country on the face of
the earth—
Rather —if I may be permitted to sharpen
the PALiEONTOLOGRAPii’s point—you are
going to teach them that this is the
most interest-paying country on the face
of the earth !
You are going to teach them that Rex
O’Malleus (on whom he peace!) is the
greatest man on the face of the earth!
You are going to teach them that the alleged
capital of an entire continent is to be
the most magnificent one-horse town on
the face of the earth!
You are going to teach them that the alleged
design of the aforesaid one-horse capital
had to he imported!
You are going to teach them that, strictly
in accordance with the tenets of the
Science of Fossils, everything should be
imported !
You are going to teach them that the Aus-
tralian capital must, necessarily, not
be of Australian design !
For, in the Pilatic phrase of the incompara-
able Palxcontolograpii : “ Why should
an Australian necessarily he chosent ”
Necessarily the Yankee, the Finlander and
all foreigners who have never even seen
Australia know all about it!
Ilow can a mere local competitor in toWti-
faking know anything of local condi-
tions and configurations?
Necessarily these things are revealed in
visions to .foreign designers, but con-
cealed froth otir sight!
To Australian eyes there is no water Worth
speaking of at Yass-Canberra, but be-
hold! the Yankee seer taketh up seven
rivers-full !
And great is the fulness thereof!
Again, therefore, as the redoubtable I'al./L-
-ontolograpii saith “ Necessarily the
science or art of faking-in water, where
water is not, has an exclusively cosmo-
politan application, instead of beimr
local in the driest sense of the word !!!"
fOr something like that.)
Why should an Australian necessarily he
chosen to do this thing?
Why should an Australian daily paper neces-
sarily he chosen for our reading?
Are we not importing the design?
Could we not import our daily papers, cam-
rrados?
Can we not import this capital, also? Is
icater non-importable?
Does not this country contain the sole human
survivors of the Palaeolithic Age?
Does not this country rejoice in possessing
the Daily Bal.xontolograpii?
You should teach the visiting Imparters. ot
Importers of Knowledge these things.
cam erados !
O Cosmos! O Crikey!
